I call it bread and scrape!\xe2\x80\x9d solemnly said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt only has butter in the little holes of it, not at the top, Miss\r\nFosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d said, in an odd pleading kind of tone, a stout good-humoured\r\ngirl of thirteen, with face, hair, and all, a good deal like a nice\r\ncomfortable apricot in a sunny place, or a good respectable Alderney cow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it would be better not to grumble, Susan, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d replied, in\r\na low voice, a pleasant dark-eyed young lady who was making tea; but the\r\nboys at the bottom of the table neither heard nor heeded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary, Mary, quite contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d was Sam\xe2\x80\x99s cry, in so funny a voice, that\r\nMiss Fosbrook could only laugh; \xe2\x80\x9cis this bread and scrape the fare for a\r\nrising young family of genteel birth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d with a pathetic grimace, cried the pretty-faced though sandy-haired\r\nHenry, the next to him in age, \xe2\x80\x9cif our beloved parents knew how their\r\npoor deserted infants are treated\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fine large infant you are, Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m an infant, you\xe2\x80\x99re an infant, Miss Fosbrook is an infant\xe2\x80\x94a babby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the more civilized Sam, clenching his fist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Miss Fosbrook, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cyour brother is quite\r\nright; I am as much an infant in the eye of the law as little George.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, I said I would!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Henry; \xe2\x80\x9cdidn\xe2\x80\x99t I, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you what?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Susan, not in the most elegant English.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Martin Greville twitted us with having a girl for a governess,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Henry; \xe2\x80\x9che said it was a shame we should be taken in to think her\r\ngrown up, when she was not twenty; and I said I would find out, and now I\r\nhave done it!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried triumphantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody is quite welcome to know my age,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, the\r\ncolour rising in her cheek.  \xe2\x80\x9cI was nineteen on the last of April; but I\r\nhad rather you had asked me point blank, Henry, than tried to find out in\r\na sidelong way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry looked a little surly; and Elizabeth, a nice-looking girl, who sat\r\nnext to him and was nearest in age, said, \xe2\x80\x9cOh! but that would have been\r\nso rude, Miss Fosbrook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRude, but honest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; and Susan\xe2\x80\x99s honest eyes twinkled,\r\nas much as to say, \xe2\x80\x9cI like that;\xe2\x80\x9d but she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe Hal\r\nmeant it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Mary, this plate is done\xe2\x80\x94more bread and\r\nbutter; d\xe2\x80\x99ye hear? not bread and gammon!\xe2\x80\x9d and he began the chant, in\r\nwhich six voices joined till it became a roar, pursuing Mary down to the\r\nlower regions:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cThick butter and thin bread,\r\n    Or it shall be thrown at Mary\xe2\x80\x99s head;\r\n    Thick bread and thin butter,\r\n    Is only fit for the ducks in the gutter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth looked appealingly at Miss Fosbrook; but Miss Fosbrook was\r\nleaning back in her chair, her handkerchief up to her mouth, in fits of\r\nlaughing, seeing which, the children bawled louder and louder; and\r\nElizabeth only abstained from stopping her ears because she knew that was\r\nthe sure way to be held fast, and have it bellowed into them.\r\n\r\nLittle Annie blundered in her eagerness upon\r\n\r\n                        \xe2\x80\x9cThick bread and thin butter,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nwhereupon there was a general outcry.  \xe2\x80\x9cNanny likes thick bread and thin\r\nbutter, let her have it!\xe2\x80\x9d and Sam, Henry, and Johnnie directed a whole\r\nbattery of their remaining crusts towards her cup, which would presently\r\nhave been upset into her lap but for Miss Fosbrook, who recovered\r\nherself, and said gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cThis must not be, Sam; I shall send you away\r\nfrom the table if you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam wanted to see whether she would, and threw the crust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam,\xe2\x80\x9d she said very decidedly, though there was a quiver in her voice,\r\nas if she were frightened.\r\n\r\nSam looked up, and did not move.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss Fosbrook!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cwe were all just as bad.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t punish\r\nSam!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is time that Sam should show that he has the feelings of a manly\r\nboy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, looking full at him.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe knows that I must\r\nkeep my word, and that I have no strength to fight with him.\xe2\x80\x94Sam, go and\r\nfinish your tea on the window-seat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer clear brown eyes looked full at him as she spoke, and all the young\r\npopulation watched to see what he would do.  He hesitated a moment, then\r\ntook up his cup and plate, and sat down in the window-seat.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook breathed freely, and she had almost said, \xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbut she did not think this was the time; and collecting herself, she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cFun is all very well, and I hope we shall have plenty, but we\r\nought not to let it grow riotous; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was of a good sort\r\nwhen it was complaining of the food provided for us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children were all rather subdued by what she said; some felt a little\r\ncross, and some rather ashamed; and when Mary brought back the dish\r\nreplenished with slices, no one said a word as to whether the butter were\r\nthick or thin.  The silence seemed to David a favourable occasion for\r\nrenewing the great question, \xe2\x80\x9cHow does a pig pay the rent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a general giggle, and again Miss Fosbrook was as bad as any:\r\nwhile David, looking affronted, tapped the table with the handle of his\r\nspoon, and repeated, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you, Davy man,\xe2\x80\x9d began Henry, first recovering.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe pig is a\r\nvery sagacious animal, especially in Hampshire, and so he smells out\r\nwherever the bags of money are sown underground, and digs them up with\r\nhis nose.  Then he swings them on his back, and gives a curl of his tail\r\nand a wink of his eye, and lays them down just before the landlord\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfeet; and he\xe2\x80\x99s so cunning, that not an inch will he budge till he\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\nthe receipt, with a stamp upon it, on his snout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; now is that a true story?\xe2\x80\x9d cried little Annie, who was the only\r\nperson except David grave enough to speak; while Sam, exploding in the\r\nwindow, called out, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know that\xe2\x80\x99s why pigs have rings in\r\ntheir noses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a lady loved a swine;\r\n       \xe2\x80\x98Honey,\xe2\x80\x99 says she,\r\n    I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you a silver trough.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n       \xe2\x80\x98Hunks!\xe2\x80\x99 says he,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\ncontinued Hal; \xe2\x80\x9cthat shows his disinterestedness.  Oh, werry sagacious\r\nhaminals is pigs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Elizabeth, \xe2\x80\x9cto confuse the children with such\r\nnonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think I know how the rent is paid?  I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen Papa on\r\nrent-day hundreds of times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the pigs, Hal; did you ever see the pigs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThousands of times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBringing bags of gold?  O Hal!  Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to know,\xe2\x80\x9d continued David, who had been digesting the startling\r\nfact, \xe2\x80\x9chow the pig swings the bag on his back? I don\xe2\x80\x99t think ours could\r\ndo it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a sort made on purpose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMade on purpose by Mr. Henry Merrifield,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, at last able to\r\nspeak.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t believe one word, David dear; Hal is laughing at you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how does a pig do it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked David, returning to the charge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you want to know, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xe2\x80\x99s sister said so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cDavy went out with the nursery children\r\nto-day, and they went to see Mary\xe2\x80\x99s sister.  Her husband is drowned\r\nbecause he was a sailor; and the _Mermaid_ went to South America; and\r\nthere are five little tiny children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the mermaid\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Harry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; the _Mermaid_ was the ship, and it was wrecked, and they have\r\nnoticing to live upon; and she takes in washing, and is such a nice\r\nwoman.  Mamma said we might take them our old winter frocks, and so David\r\nwent there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she said if she had a pig to pay the rent she should be quite\r\nhappy,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.  \xe2\x80\x9cHow could he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cthe pig would live on her garden-stuff,\r\nher cabbage-leaves and potato-skins; and that when he was fat she would\r\nsell him, and pay the rent with the money.  Am I right, Sam? you know I\r\nam a Cockney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou could not be more right if you were a Hampshire beg,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJack Higgins was her husband\xe2\x80\x99s name, and a famous fellow he was; he once\r\nrigged a little boat for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he sailed with Papa once, long ago,\xe2\x80\x9d added Susan; to which Sam\r\nrejoined,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore fool he to go into the merchant service and get drowned, with\r\nnothing for his widow to live upon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we give her a pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do!\xe2\x80\x9d earnestly exclaimed David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll catch one,\xe2\x80\x9d broke from John and Annie at once; \xe2\x80\x9csuch lots as there\r\nare in the yard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would catch it, I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam disdainfully; while Susan\r\nexplained,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; those are Papa\xe2\x80\x99s pigs.  Purday would not let you give them away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry, \xe2\x80\x9cthat was only those little geese.  I meant to\r\nmake a subscription among ourselves, and give her the pig; and won\xe2\x80\x99t she\r\nbe surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! yes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the children; \xe2\x80\x9clet\xe2\x80\x99s do it all ourselves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got one-and-threepence, and sixpence next Saturday,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve eightpence,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve a whole shilling,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve fourpence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Johnnie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve not much, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, feeling in her pocket, with\r\nrather black looks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9ceverybody knows simple Sukey never has a farthing in her\r\npocket by any chance!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I have, Sam;\xe2\x80\x9d and with an air of great triumph, Susan held up\r\nthree-halfpence, whereat all the party screamed with laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but Bessie always has lots!  She\xe2\x80\x99s as rich as a little Jew.  Come,\r\nBet, Elizabeth, Elspeth, Betsy, and Bess, what will you give?\xe2\x80\x94what have\r\nyou got?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and one hand came on her shoulder, and another on her arm but\r\nshe shook herself free, and answered rather crossly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve got something else to do with my money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! you little stingy avaricious crab!\xe2\x80\x9d was the outcry beginning; but\r\nMiss Fosbrook stopped it before Elizabeth had time to make the angry\r\nanswer that was rising on her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my dears, you must not tease her.  Each of you has a full right to\r\nuse your own money as you may think best; and it is not right to force\r\ngifts in this manner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a little affected pussy-cat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, much annoyed; \xe2\x80\x9cI know what\r\nshe wants it for\xe2\x80\x94to buy herself a ridiculous parasol like Ida Greville,\r\nwhen she would see poor Hannah Higgins starving at her feet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth bit her lip, and tossed up her head; the tears were in her\r\neyes, but she made no answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, never mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s as obstinate as a male when she gets\r\na thing into her head.  Let\xe2\x80\x99s see what we\xe2\x80\x99ve got without her.  I\xe2\x80\x99ve only\r\nsevenpence: worse luck that I bought ball of string yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe addition amounted to three shillings and elevenpence halfpenny: a sum\r\nwhich looked so mighty when spread out, chiefly in coppers, on the\r\nwindow-seat, that Annie and David looked on it as capable of buying any\r\namount of swine; but Sam looked rather blank at it, and gazing up and\r\ndown, said, \xe2\x80\x9cBut what does a pig cost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, what does a pig cost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook shook her head and laughed, saying that she knew much less\r\nof pigs than they did; and Susan exclaiming, \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s Purday in the\r\ncourt,\xe2\x80\x9d they all tumbled to the window, one upon the top of the other.\r\n\r\nThe window was a large heavily-framed sash, with a deep window-seat, and\r\na narrow ledge within the sill\xe2\x80\x94as if made on purpose, the first for the\r\nknees the second for the elbows of the gazers therefrom.\r\n\r\nAs to the view, it was into a walled kitchen court, some high chestnut\r\nand lime trees just looking over the grey roofs of the offices.  On the\r\nground lay a big black Newfoundland dog, and a couple of graceful\r\ngreyhounds, one of them gnawing a bone, cunningly watched by a\r\nkeen-looking raven, with his head on one side; while peeping out from the\r\nbars of the bottle-rack was the demure face of the sandy cat, on the\r\nwatch for either bones or sparrows.\r\n\r\nA stout, stumpy, shrewd-looking labourer, in a short round frock, high\r\nbuskins, an old wide-awake, short curly hair, and a very large nose,\r\nstood in front of the dairy door, mixing a mess of warm milk for the\r\nyoung calves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPurday!  Master Purday!\xe2\x80\x9d roared nearly the whole young population above;\r\nbut he was so intent on his mixture, that he went on as if he were deaf,\r\ntill a second explosion of \xe2\x80\x9cPurday!  Purday!  I say!\xe2\x80\x9d made him turn up\r\nhis face in an odd half-awake kind of manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPurday, what\xe2\x80\x99s the price of a pig?\xe2\x80\x9d and, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does a pig cost, Purday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat d\xe2\x80\x99ye all holler at once for?  A body can\xe2\x80\x99t hear a word,\xe2\x80\x9d was all\r\nthe answer they got; whereupon they all started together again, and\r\nPurday went on with his mixture as if they had been so many hens\r\ncackling.\r\n\r\nThen Sam got up his breath again and called alone, \xe2\x80\x9cPurday!\xe2\x80\x9d and Hal and\r\nSusan by pats and pinches strangled the like outcry from Annie and John,\r\nso as to leave the field clear for the great question, \xe2\x80\x9cPurday, what does\r\na pig cost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than your voices up there, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Purday, making some laugh;\r\nbut Henry cried impatiently,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Purday, we really do want to know what is the price of pigs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey was high last market,\xe2\x80\x9d began Purday.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care if they were high or low,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal; \xe2\x80\x9cI want to know what\r\nmoney they cost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDifferent pigs cost different prices,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth the oracle, so\r\nsententiously, that Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders shook with laughing as she\r\nstood a little in the background of the eager heap in the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice little pig, such as you\xe2\x80\x99d give\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, hush, Hal, it\xe2\x80\x99s a secret,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA pretty sort of secret\xe2\x80\x94known to eight already, and bawled out all over\r\nthe yard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t tell him what it\xe2\x80\x99s for; you can ask him without that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice little young pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9csuch as you\xe2\x80\x99d keep all the summer,\r\nand fat in the winter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t for you, Purday,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever fear my being disappointed, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the free-spoken Purday,\r\nwith a twinkle of his eye, which Hal understood so well that he burst\r\nout,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you think I can never do what I say I will; but you\xe2\x80\x99ll see, Purday,\r\nif we don\xe2\x80\x99t give a pig to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was screamed at, and pulled into order and silence, ere the words,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHannah Higgins\xe2\x80\x9d had quite come out; and Sam repeated his question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purday at last, \xe2\x80\x9cif pigs was reasonable, you might get a\r\nnice little one to fat, at Kattern Hill fair, somewhere about ten\r\nshillings, or maybe twelve\xe2\x80\x94sometimes more, sometimes less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen shillings!\xe2\x80\x9d  The community stood round and looked at one another at\r\nthe notion of such an awful sum; but Hal was the first to cast a ray of\r\nhope on the gloom.  \xe2\x80\x9cKattern Hill fair ain\xe2\x80\x99t till Midsummer, and perhaps\r\nGrandmamma will send us some money before that.  If anybody\xe2\x80\x99s birthday\r\nwas but coming!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter save it out of our allowance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cHow long is it to the\r\nfair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s pocket-book declared it to be four weeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, \xe2\x80\x9cwe three big ones have sixpence a week each,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s six shillings, leaving out stingy Bess, and the little ones\r\nthreepence, that\xe2\x80\x99s three times three is nine, and three times nine is\r\nthirty-six, that\xe2\x80\x99s three shillings, and six is nine, and very near four\r\nis fourteen.  We shall do the pig yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Hal; but if pigs are reasonable, I am afraid three times nine never\r\nyet were so much so as to make thirty-six,\xe2\x80\x9d objected Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nSam whistled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-seven\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s three and twopence\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s all the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal;\r\nthen at the scream of the rest, \xe2\x80\x9cat least two and threepence.  Well, any\r\nway there\xe2\x80\x99s plenty for piggy-wiggy, and it shall be a jolly secret to\r\ndelight Hannah Higgins, and surprise Papa and Mamma: hurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cbut then nobody must have any fines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, and Sue must keep her money.  That will be a wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Harry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll try,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try not to have a single fine, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll not buy a single lump of sugar-candy, for I do want poor Hannah to\r\nhave her pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so will we!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the younger ones with one voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly,\xe2\x80\x9d added Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cI must buy Dicky\xe2\x80\x99s canary seed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I must have a queen\xe2\x80\x99s head to write to Mamma,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! never mind that, such trumpery as your letters are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma could say them by heart before she gets them.  What does she care\r\nfor them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLittle Annie looked very deplorable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cmammas always care for little\r\ngirls\xe2\x80\x99 letters, and you are quite right to keep a penny for your stamp\r\nfor her.\xe2\x80\x94You see, Hal, this scheme will never come to good if you\r\nsacrifice other duties to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry twirled round impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we set up a treasury, and put\r\nall in that we can properly afford, and then break it open on the day\r\nbefore the fair, and see how much we have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! yes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the children in raptures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you help, Miss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, clasping her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to do a very little, if you will take this silver\r\nthreepenny; but I do not think it would be right for me to spare one\r\npenny more, for all I can afford is very much wanted at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall we have for treasury?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, looking round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cHere, in the baby-house; here\xe2\x80\x99s the Toby, let\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nput it inside him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe so-called baby-house was an old-fashioned cupboard with glass doors,\r\nwhere certain tender dolls, and other curiosities, playthings too frail\r\nto be played with and the like, were ranged in good order, and never\r\ntaken out except when some one child was unwell, and had to stay in-doors\r\nalone.\r\n\r\nToby Fillpot was a present from Nurse Freeman.  It was a large mug,\r\nrepresenting a man with a red coat, black hat, and white waistcoat, very\r\nshort legs, and top-boots.  The opening of the cup was at the top of his\r\nhead, and into this was dropped all the silver and pence at present\r\nmustered, and computed to be about four shillings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, Miss Fosbrook, you\xe2\x80\x99ll not be cross about fines?\xe2\x80\x9d said Johnnie,\r\nlooking coaxing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I shall not be cross,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I do not engage to let\r\nyou off any.  I think having so good a use to put your money to should\r\nmake you more careful against forfeiting it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Johnnie disconsolately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I never get fined,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal joyfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept for running up stairs in dirty shoes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! there\xe2\x80\x99s no dirt now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see, what are the fines?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the list,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; and sighing, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I shall\r\nnever do it!  If Bessie only would help!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fines of the Stokesley schoolroom were these for delinquencies\xe2\x80\x94each\r\nvalue a farthing\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  For being dressed later than eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\n  For hair not properly brushed.\r\n\r\n  For coming to lessons later than five minutes after ten.\r\n\r\n  For dirty hands.\r\n\r\n  For being turned back twice with any lesson.\r\n\r\n  For elbows on the table.\r\n\r\n  For foolish crying.\r\n\r\n  For unnecessary words in lesson-time.\r\n\r\n  For running up stairs in wet shoes.\r\n\r\n  For leaving things about.\r\n\r\nEach of these bits of misbehaviour caused the forfeit of a farthing out\r\nof the weekly allowance.  Susan looked very gloomy over them; but Hal\r\nexclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, Susie; we\xe2\x80\x99ll do it all without you, never fear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cI vote we have some fun in the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome readers may be disposed to doubt, after this specimen, whether the\r\nyoung Merrifields could be really young ladies and gentlemen; but indeed\r\ntheir birth might make them so; for there had been Squire Merrifields at\r\nStokesley as long as Stokesley had been a parish, and those qualities of\r\nhonour and good breeding that mark the gentleman had not been wanting to\r\nthe elder members of the family.  The father of these children was a\r\ncaptain in the navy, and till within the last six years the children had\r\nlived near Plymouth; but when he inherited the estate they came thither,\r\nand David and the two little ones had been born at Stokesley.  The\r\nproperty was not large; and as Captain Merrifield was far from rich, it\r\ntook much management to give all this tribe of boys and girls a good\r\neducation, as well as plenty of bread and butter, mutton, and\r\napple-pudding.  There was very little money left to be spent upon\r\nornament, or upon pleasuring; so they were brought up to the most homely\r\ndress suited to their station, and were left entirely to the country\r\nenjoyments that spring up of themselves.  Company was seldom seen, for\r\nPapa and Mamma had little time or means for visiting; and a few morning\r\ncalls and a little dining out was all they did; which tended to make the\r\nyoung ones more shy and homely, more free and rude, more inclined to love\r\ntheir own ways and despise those of other people, than if they had seen\r\nmore of the world.  They were a happy, healthy set of children, not\r\nfaulty in essentials, but, it must be confessed, a little wild, rough and\r\nuncivil, in spite of the code of fines.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\n\r\n\r\nMRS. MERRIFIELD had taught her children herself, till Samuel and Henry\r\nbegan going to the Curate for a couple of hours every day, to be prepared\r\nfor school.  Lessons were always rather a scramble; so many people coming\r\nto speak to her, and so many interruptions from the nursery; and then\r\ncame a time when Mamma always was tired, and Papa used to come out and\r\nscold if the noises grew very loud indeed, and was vexed if the children\r\ngave Mamma any trouble of any kind.  Next they were told they were to\r\nhave a governess\xe2\x80\x94a sort of piece of finery which the little savages had\r\nalways despised\xe2\x80\x94and thereupon came Miss Fosbrook; but before she had been\r\na week in the house Mamma was quite ill and in her bed-room, and Papa\r\nlooked graver than he had ever done before; and Mr. Braddon, the doctor,\r\ncame very often: and at last Susan was called into Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s room, and it\r\nwas explained to her that Mamma was thought so ill, that she must go to\r\nbe under a London doctor, and would be away, she could not tell how long;\r\nso that meantime the children must all be left to Miss Fosbrook, with\r\nmany many injunctions to be good and obedient, for hearing that they were\r\ngoing on well would be poor Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s only comfort.\r\n\r\nIt was three days since Captain and Mrs. Merrifield had gone; and Miss\r\nFosbrook stood at the window, gazing at the bright young green of the\r\nhorse-chestnut trees, and thinking many various thoughts in the lull that\r\nthe children had left when they rushed out of doors.\r\n\r\nShe thought herself quite alone, and stood, sometimes smiling over the\r\nodd ways of her charges, and at what they put her in mind of, sometimes\r\ngravely thinking whether she had said or done the wisest things for them,\r\nor what their mother would have most approved.  She was just going to\r\nmove away from the window, when she saw a little figure curled up on the\r\nfloor, with her head on the window-seat.  \xe2\x80\x9cBessie, my dear, what are you\r\ndoing here?  Why are not you gone out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought they were to have a great game at whoop-hide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like whoop-hide.  Johnnie pulls the clothes off my back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, I hope you are not staying in because they called you those\r\nfoolish names.  It was all in good humour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, her throat swelling.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt was not\r\ntrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not; but you did not speak to give your reasons; and who could\r\ntell how good they might be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve a right to my secrets as well as they have,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little\r\nmaiden.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook looked kindly at her, and she turned wistful eyes on the\r\nyoung governess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, will you keep a secret?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want my money to buy some card-board\xe2\x80\x94and some ribbon\xe2\x80\x94and some real\r\ntrue paints.  I\xe2\x80\x99ve got some vermilion, but I want some real good blue.\r\nAnd then I want to make some beautiful bands with ties\xe2\x80\x94like what Papa has\r\nfor his letters\xe2\x80\x94for all Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s letters in her desk.  There\xe2\x80\x99s a bundle of\r\nPapa\xe2\x80\x99s when he was gone out to the Crimean War, and that\xe2\x80\x99s to have a\r\nfrigate on it, because of the _Calliope_\xe2\x80\x94his ship, you know; and there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\none bundle of dear Aunt Sarah\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s to have a rose, because I always\r\nthink her memory is like the rose in my hymn, you know; and Grandmamma,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s to have\xe2\x80\x94I think perhaps I could copy a bit of the tower of\r\nWestminster Abbey out of the print, because one sees it out of her\r\nwindow; and, oh! I thought of so many more, but you see I can\xe2\x80\x99t do it\r\nwithout a real good paint-box, and that costs three and sixpence.  Now,\r\nMiss Fosbrook, is it stingy to wish to do that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, my dear; but you could not expect the others to understand\r\nwhat they never were told.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d have said something if they had not called me stingy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly was rude and hasty; but if we bear such things\r\ngood-naturedly, they become better; and they were very eager about their\r\nown plan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch a disagreeable thing as a pig!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf it had been\r\nanything nice, I should not have minded so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but, my dear, you must remember that the pig will be a more useful\r\npresent than even your pretty contrivances.  You cannot call them doing\r\ngood, as the other will be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are like them!  You think I ought to spend all my money on a\r\ngreat horrid pig, when Mamma\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and the tears were in the little girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed, my dear.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t think anyone is called on to give their\r\nall, and it is very nice and quite right for a little girl to try to make\r\na pretty present to please her mamma.  There is plenty of time before\r\nyou, and I think you will manage to have some share in the very kind\r\naction your brothers and sisters are contriving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth had not forgiven, as she should have done, the being called\r\nstingy; it rankled on her feelings far more than those who said the word\r\nunderstood; and she presently went on, \xe2\x80\x9cIf they knew ever so much, they\r\nwould only laugh at me, and call it all Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s nonsense.  Miss\r\nFosbrook, please, what is affectation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe it is pretending to seem what we are not by nature,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cputting on manners or feelings that do not come to us of\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I shall tell them they make me affected,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed she.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\r\nlike to be quiet and do things prettily, they teaze me for being\r\naffected, and I\xe2\x80\x99m forced to be as plain and blunt as their are, and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like it!  I wish I was grown up.  I wish I was Ida Greville!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause then things might be pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cEverything is so\r\nplain and ugly, and one gets so tired of it!  Is it silly to like things\r\nto be pretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, far from it; that is, if we do not sacrifice better things to\r\nprettiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth looked up with a light in her dark eyes, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss\r\nFosbrook, I like you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was very much pleased, and kissed her.\r\n\r\nShe paused a moment, and then said, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, may I ask one\r\nquestion?  What is your name?  Mamma said it must be Charlotte, because\r\nyou signed your letter Ch. A. Fosbrook, but your little sister\xe2\x80\x99s letter\r\nthat you showed us began \xe2\x80\x98My dear Bell.\xe2\x80\x99  If it is a secret, indeed I\r\nwill keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no secret at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, laughing.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy name is\r\nChristabel Angela.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth opened her eyes, and said it by syllables.  \xe2\x80\x9cChristabel Angela!\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s a prettier name than Ida.  Does it make you very glad to have it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like it for some reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cMamma always says we should not be a bit\r\nhappier if our names were pretty ones; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I feel as if one\r\nwould; only the others like to make things plainer and uglier than they\r\nare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never could call your name ugly; it is such a dignified, old,\r\nrespectable name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but they call me Betty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they call me Bell, and sometimes Jelly-bag and Currant-jelly,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMiss Fosbrook, laughing and sighing, for she would have liked to have\r\nheard those funny names again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it is no good to you!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that we talk of good in such a matter.  I like my name\r\nbecause of the reason it was given to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, why?\xe2\x80\x9d eagerly asked the little girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was born, my papa was a very young man, and he was very fond of\r\nreading poetry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I thought your papa was a doctor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought only ladies, and poets, and idle silly people, cared for\r\npoetry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey can hardly be silly if they care rightly for real poetry, Bessie,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cat least, so my papa would say.  It has been one of\r\nhis great helps.  Well, in those days he was very fond of a poem about a\r\nlady called Christabel, who was so good and sweet, that when evil came\r\nnear, it could not touch her so as to do her any harm; and so he gave his\r\nlittle daughter her name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow very nice!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not envy me, my dear, for I have been a good deal laughed at\r\nfor my pretty name, and so has Papa; and I do not think he would have\r\nchosen anything so fanciful if he had been a little older.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen isn\xe2\x80\x99t he\xe2\x80\x94what is it you call it\xe2\x80\x94poetical now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed he is, in a good way;\xe2\x80\x9d and as the earnest eyes looked so warmly\r\nat her, Christabel Fosbrook could not help making a friend of the little\r\nmaiden.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe has very little time to read it; for you know he is a parish\r\nsurgeon in a great parish in London, full of poor people, worse off than\r\nyou can imagine, and often very ill.  He is obliged to be always hard at\r\nwork in the narrow close streets there, and to see everything sad, and\r\ndismal, and disagreeable, that can be found; but, do you know, Bessie, he\r\nalways looks for the good and beautiful side; he looks at one person\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npatience, and another person\xe2\x80\x99s kindness, and at some little child\xe2\x80\x99s love\r\nfor its mother or sister, that hinders it from being too painful for\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is that poetry?  I thought poetry meant verses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVerses are generally the best and most suitable way of expressing our\r\nfeelings about what is good and beautiful; but they are not always\r\npoetry, any more than the verses they sang to-night about the bread and\r\nbutter, because, you know, wanting thick butter was not exactly a\r\nbeautiful feeling.  I think the denying themselves their little\r\nindulgences for the sake of giving the poor woman a pig, is much more\r\npoetical, though nobody said a word in verse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey both laughed; and Elizabeth said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat wasn\xe2\x80\x99t what you meant about\r\nyour papa.  Susy cares for goodness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it was not all I meant; but it was seeing high and noble thoughts\r\nexpressed in beautiful verses that gives him pleasure; and when he has a\r\nlittle bit of leisure, it is his great treat to open a book of that sort,\r\nand read a little bit to us, and tell us why we like it.  He says it\r\nmakes him young again, and takes him out of the dingy streets, and from\r\nall his cares as to how the bills are to be paid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you like coming here?\xe2\x80\x9d was Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s home question; and Miss Fosbrook\r\nwinked away a little moisture, as she said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was glad to be growing a woman, and to be able to help about some of\r\nthose bills; and then I was glad to come into the beautiful country that\r\nPapa has so often told us about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not know there was anything beautiful here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Bessie, you never lived in London!  You can\xe2\x80\x99t think how many things\r\nare beautiful to me here!  I want to be writing about them to Papa and\r\nKate all day long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cMamma has pretty things in the drawing-room,\r\nbut she keeps them out of the way; and everything here is so dull and\r\nstupid!\xe2\x80\x9d and the little girl gave a yawn.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook understood her.  The wainscoted room in which they were\r\nsitting had been painted of a uniform creamy brown; the chairs were worn;\r\nthe table was blistered and cracked; the carpet only covered the middle\r\nof the room, and was so threadbare, that only a little red showed here\r\nand there.  All that was needful was there, but of the plainest kind; and\r\nwhere the other children only felt ease and freedom, and were the more\r\ncontented and happy for the homely good sense of all around them, this\r\nlittle girl felt a want that she scarcely understood, but which made her\r\nuncomfortable and discontented, even when she had so much to be thankful\r\nfor.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook moved nearer to the window.  Down below there was certainly\r\nnot much to be seen; only Pierce cleaning the knives in the knife-house,\r\nand Martha washing out her pans before the dairy-door; but that was not\r\nwhere she looked.  She turned the little half-fretful face upwards.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook there!\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cand talk of seeing nothing pretty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see nothing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you not see the pale clear green of those noble horse-chestnut leaves\r\njust sprung into their full summer dress\xe2\x80\x94not in the least worn nor\r\nstained yet?  And those fine spikes of white blossom, all tending\r\nup\xe2\x80\x94up\xe2\x80\x94while the masses of those leaves fall so gracefully down, as if\r\nlifting them up, and then falling back to do them honour.\xe2\x80\x9d  Bessie\r\nsmiled, and her eye lighted up.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd see the colour against the sky\xe2\x80\x94look\r\nat the contrast of that bright light green with the blue, so very deep,\r\nof the sky\xe2\x80\x94and oh! see that train of little clouds, red with soft sunny\r\nlight, like a little soft flock of rosy lambs, if there were such things,\r\nlying across the sky. O Bessie! you can\xe2\x80\x99t talk of wanting the sight of\r\npretty things while you have that sky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie was coming closer to her, when in burst Sam and Johnnie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Bess! moping here, I declare!  I suppose you and Miss Fosbrook\r\nare telling each other all your secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was just coming out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.  \xe2\x80\x9cI want to make out\r\nsomething about those noble flowers of the horse-chestnut, and why they\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t look whiter.  Could you gather one for me, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam was only too glad of an excuse for climbing a tree, however cheaply\r\nhe might hold one who cared for flowers; and by the time Bessie had put\r\non her lilac-spotted sun-bonnet\xe2\x80\x94a shapeless article it must be confessed,\r\nwith a huge curtain serving for a tippet, very comfortable, and no\r\ntrouble at all\xe2\x80\x94he had scrambled into the fork, and brought down a\r\nbeautiful spire of blossoms, with all the grand leaves hanging round in\r\ntheir magnificent fans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do with it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the children, standing round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think you could ask Mary to spare us a jug, Susan?  If I might\r\nput it in water in the schoolroom fireplace, it would look fresh and\r\ncheerful for Sunday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, pleased with the commission, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I will;\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\naway she ran, while Miss Fosbrook examined the spike to her own great\r\nenjoyment.  \xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthe flowers are not really white, they\r\neach have a patch of pink or yellow on them, which gives them their\r\nsoftness.  Yes; and do you see, Bessie, they are in clusters of three,\r\nand each three has one flower with a pink spot, and two with a yellow\r\none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very curious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie: the fretfulness was very much gone\r\nout of her tone, and she stood looking at the beautiful flower, without a\r\nword, till Susan came back, when she began to show her what Miss Fosbrook\r\nhad pointed out.  Susan smiled with her really good nature, and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow funny!\xe2\x80\x9d but was more intent on telling Miss Fosbrook that she had\r\nbrought the jug, and then on hauling Elizabeth away to a game at Tom\r\nTittler\xe2\x80\x99s ground.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook said she would put away the flower and come back again; and\r\nshe settled the branch in the chimney, where it looked very graceful, and\r\nreally did enliven the room, and then walked out towards the lawn.\r\n\r\nThere was a lawn in front of the house, part of which had been formerly\r\nlevelled for a bowling-green, and was kept clear of shrubs or\r\nflower-beds.  Beyond was a smooth, rather rapid slope towards a quiet\r\nriver, beyond which there rose again a beautiful green field, crowned\r\nabove by a thick wood, ending at the top in some scraggy pine-trees, with\r\nscanty dark foliage at the top of their rude russet arms.  Fine trees\r\nstood out here and there upon the slope of the field; and Captain\r\nMerrifield\xe2\x80\x99s fine sleeked cows were licking each other, or chewing the\r\ncud, under them.\r\n\r\nThere was a white Chinese bridge, the rails all zigzags, and patterns\r\nrunning this way and that, so that it must have been very ugly and\r\nglaring before the white paint had faded so much.\r\n\r\nThe house was a respectable old stone building, rather brown and grey,\r\nand the stone somewhat disposed to peel off in flakes; the windows large\r\nsashes, set in great projecting squared stones, the tallest and biggest\r\nat the top.  It was a house of a very sober pleasant countenance, that\r\nlooked as if it had always been used to have a large family in it; and\r\nthere was a vine, with all its beauteous leaves, trained all across the\r\ngarden front, making a pleasant green summer-blind over the higher half\r\nof the drawing-room windows, that now stood open, telling of the\r\nemptiness within.\r\n\r\nChristabel stood for a few moments looking round, and thinking what a\r\nparadise of green rest this would be to her hard-worked father and\r\nanxious mother; and how she should like to see her little brothers and\r\nsisters have one free run and roll on that delicious greensward, instead\r\nof now and then walking to one of the parks as a great holiday.  Yet hers\r\nwas a very happy home, and, except her being absent from it, nothing had\r\nbefallen her to sadden her merry young spirits; so when she heard the\r\njoyous cry behind her\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m on Tommy Tittler\xe2\x80\x99s ground,\r\n    Picking up gold and silver,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nshe turned about, and laughed as she saw the gold-finders stooping and\r\nclawing at the grass, with eyes cast round about them for Hal, who was\r\npursuing Susan in and out, up and down till, with screams of exultation,\r\nshe was safely across the ridge of the bowling-green, that served as\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Hal turned back, Miss Fosbrook was as heedfully and warily picking\r\nup gold and silver as any of the rest of them.  He was resolved on\r\ncapturing her; but first David was such a tempting prize, with his back\r\nso very near, and so unconscious, that he must be made prisoner.  A catch\r\nat the brown-holland blouse\xe2\x80\x94a cry\xe2\x80\x94a shout of laughter, and Davy is led up\r\nbehind the standard maiden-blush rose, always serving as the prison.  And\r\nnow the tug of war rages round it, he darts here and there within his\r\nbounds, holding out his hand to any kind deliverer whose touch may set\r\nhim free; and all the others run backwards and forwards, trying to\r\ncircumvent the watchful jailor, Tom Tittler, who, in front of the\r\nrose-bush, flies instantly at whoever is only coming near his captive.\r\n\r\nHa!  Susan had nearly\xe2\x80\x94all but done it, while Hal was chasing away Annie.\r\nNo, not she; Hal is back again, and with a shriek away she scours.  Sam!\r\noh, he is very near; if that stupid little Davy would only look round, he\r\nwould be free in another moment; but he only gapes at the pursuit of\r\nSusan, and Sam will touch him without his being aware!  No\xe2\x80\x94here\xe2\x80\x99s Hal\r\nback again.  Sam\xe2\x80\x99s off.  What a scamper!  Now\xe2\x80\x99s the time\xe2\x80\x94here\xe2\x80\x99s Miss\r\nFosbrook, lighter-footed than any of the children, softly stealing on\r\ntip-toe, while Hal is scaring Johnnie.  Her fingers just touch Davy\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFreed!  Freed!\xe2\x80\x9d is the cry; and off goes he, pounding for home! but Hal\r\nrushes across the path, he intercepts Miss Fosbrook, and, with a shout of\r\ntriumph\xe2\x80\x94There is the sound of a rent.  Everybody stands a little aghast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only the gathers,\xe2\x80\x9d says Miss Fosbrook good-humouredly.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tuck\r\nthem up and sew them in by and by; but really, Hal, you need not pull so\r\nfuriously; I would have yielded to something short of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGowns are such stuff!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, really meaning it for an apology,\r\nthough it did not sound like one, for her good-natured face abashed him a\r\nlittle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTouch and take used to be our rule,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nBessie eagerly said that would be the best way, the boys were so rude;\r\nbut all the rest with one voice cried out that it would be very stupid;\r\nand Miss Fosbrook did not press it, but only begged in a droll way that\r\nsome one would take pity on her; and come to release her; and so alert\r\nwas she in skipping towards her allies from behind the rose-bush, that\r\nBessie presently succeeded in giving the rescuing touch, and she flew\r\nback quick as a bird to the safe territory, dragging Bessie with her, who\r\notherwise would have assuredly been caught; and who, warm with the spirit\r\nof the game, felt as if she should have been quite glad to be made\r\nprisoner for her dear Christabel\xe2\x80\x99s sake.\r\n\r\nAn hour after, and all the children were in bed.  Susan and Annie\r\nagreeing that a governess was no such great bother after all; and\r\nElizabeth lying awake to whisper over to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cChristabel Angela,\r\nChristabel Angela!  That\xe2\x80\x99s my secret!\xe2\x80\x9d in a sort of dream of pleasure\r\nthat will make most people decide on her being a very silly little girl.\r\n\r\nAnd Christabel Angela herself sat mending her gathers, and thinking over\r\nher first week of far greater difficulties than she had contemplated when\r\nshe had left home with the understanding that she was to be entirely\r\nunder Mrs. Merrifield\xe2\x80\x99s direction.  Poor Mrs. Merrifield had said much of\r\nregret at leaving her to such a crew of little savages, and she had only\r\ntried to set the mother\xe2\x80\x99s mind at rest by being cheerful; and though she\r\nfelt that it was a great undertaking to manage those great boys out of\r\nlesson-hours, she knew that when a thing cannot be helped, strength and\r\naid is given to those who seek for it sincerely.\r\n\r\nAnd on the whole, she felt thankful to the children for having behaved\r\neven as well as they had done.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGRANT to us, Thy humble servants, that by Thy holy inspiration we may\r\nthink those things that be good, and by Thy merciful guiding may perform\r\nthe same,\xe2\x80\x9d spelt out David with some trouble and difficulty, as he stood\r\nby Miss Fosbrook on Sunday morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother \xe2\x80\x9cWell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs wanting to buy a pig one of the \xe2\x80\x98things that be good\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything kind and right is good, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, a little\r\nvexed at a sort of snorting she heard from the other end of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavy thinks the pig is in his Collect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\nHe was one of those who were especially proud of being downright, and in\r\nhim it often amounted to utter regardlessness of people\xe2\x80\x99s feelings, yet\r\nnot out of ill-nature; and when Susan responded, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t teaze Davy\xe2\x80\x94he\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t bear it,\xe2\x80\x9d he was silent; but the mischief was done; and when Miss\r\nFosbrook went on saying that the wish to help the poor woman was\r\nassuredly a good thought, which the little boy might well ask to be aided\r\nin fulfilling, David had grown ashamed, and would not listen.  But the\r\nmention of the pig had set off Master Henry, who was sitting up in the\r\nwindow-seat with Annie, also learning the Collect, and he burst out into\r\ndescriptions of the weight of money that would be found in Toby, and how\r\nhe meant to go to the fair with Purday, and help him to choose the pig,\r\nand drive it home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore likely to hinder,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides, Papa wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let you,\xe2\x80\x9d added Bessie; but Hal did not choose to\r\nhear, and went on as to how the pig should ran away with Purday, and jump\r\ninto a stall full of parliament gingerbread (whereat Annie fell into\r\nconvulsions of laughing), and Hal should be the first to stop it, and\r\njump on its back, and ride out of the fair holding it by the ears; and\r\nthen they should pop it into the sty unknown to Hannah Higgins, and all\r\nlie in wait to hear what would happen; and when it squealed, she would\r\nthink it the baby crying; but there Susan burst out at the notion of any\r\none thinking a child could scream like a pig, taking it as an affront to\r\nall babyhood; and Miss Fosbrook took the opportunity of saying,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHadn\xe2\x80\x99t you better hatch your chickens before you count them, Henry? If\r\nyou prevent everyone from learning the Collect, I fear there will be the\r\nless hope of Mr. Piggy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! we don\xe2\x80\x99t have fines on Sundays,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma says that on Sundays naughtiness is not such a trifle that we can\r\nbe fined for it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not naughtiness we are ever fined for,\xe2\x80\x9d added Elizabeth: \xe2\x80\x9c_that_\r\nwe are punished and talked to for: but the fines are only for bad\r\nhabits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!  I hope I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t have any this week,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re sure of them for everything possible\r\nexcept crying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Bessie gets all the crying fines,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal; \xe2\x80\x9cand I hope she\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave lots, because she won\xe2\x80\x99t help the pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie started up from her place and rushed out of the room; while Miss\r\nFosbrook indignantly exclaimed,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, boys, I can\xe2\x80\x99t think how you can be so ill-natured!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey looked up as though it were quite a new light to them; and Susan\r\nexclaimed,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss Fosbrook! they don\xe2\x80\x99t mean it: Sam and Hal never were\r\nill-natured in their lives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you call ill-natured,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cunless it\r\nis saying the very things most likely to vex another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to vex anybody,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry, \xe2\x80\x9conly we always go on so, and\r\nnobody is such a baby as to mind, except Bessie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Sam muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cOne can\xe2\x80\x99t be always picking one\xe2\x80\x99s words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to argue about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cand it is time\r\nto get ready for church.  Only I thought manliness was shown in kindness\r\nto the weak, and avoiding what can pain them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went away; and Susan was the first to exclaim,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think she\xe2\x80\x99d have been so cross!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStuff, Sue!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s not being cross.  I like her for having a\r\nspirit; but one can\xe2\x80\x99t be finikin and mealy-mouthed to suit her London\r\nmanners.  I like the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt would have been well if any one had been by to tell Mr. Samuel that\r\ntruth of character does not consist in disagreeable and uncalled-for\r\npersonalities.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook did not wonder at little Elizabeth for her discomfort under\r\nthe rude homeliness of Stokesley, where the children made a bad copy of\r\ntheir father\xe2\x80\x99s sailor bluntness, and the difficulties of money matters\r\nkept down all indulgences.  She knew that Captain Merrifield was as poor\r\na man for an esquire as her father was for a surgeon, and that if he were\r\nto give his sons an education fit for their station, he must make his\r\nhousehold live plainly in every way; but without thinking them right\r\nfeelings, she had some pity for little Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s weariness and discontent\r\nin never seeing anything pretty.  The three girls came in dressed for\r\nchurch, in the plainest brown hats, black capes, and drab alpaca frocks,\r\nrather long and not very full; not a coloured bow nor handkerchief, not a\r\nflounce nor fringe, to relieve them; even their books plain brown.\r\nBessie looked wistfully at Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s pretty Church-service, and\r\nsaid she and Susan both had beautiful Prayer-Books, but Mamma said they\r\ncould not be trusted with them yet\xe2\x80\x94Ida Greville had such a beauty.\r\n\r\nWas it the effect of Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s words, that Sam forbore to teaze\r\nBessie about Ida Greville?\xe2\x80\x94whose name was a very dangerous subject in the\r\nschoolroom.  Also, he let Bessie take hold of Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s hand in\r\npeace, though in general the least token of affection was scouted by the\r\nwhole party.\r\n\r\nIt was a pretty walk to church, over a paddock, where the cows were\r\nturned out, and then along a green lane; and the boys had been trained\r\nenough in Sunday habits to make them steady and quiet on the way,\r\nespecially as Henry was romancing about the pig.\r\n\r\nBy and by Elizabeth gave Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s hand a sudden pull; and she\r\nperceived, in the village street into which they were emerging, a party\r\non the way to church.  There were two ladies, one in stately handsome\r\nslight mourning, the other more quietly dressed, and two or three boys;\r\nbut what Elizabeth wanted her to look at was a little girl of nine years\r\nold, who was walking beside the lady.  Her hat was black chip, edged and\r\ntied with rose-coloured ribbon, and adorned with a real bird, with glass\r\neyes, black plumage, except the red crest and wings.  She wore a\r\nneatly-fitting little fringed black polka, beneath which spread out in\r\nfan-like folds her flounced pink muslin, coming a little below her knees,\r\nand showing her worked drawers, which soon gave place to her neat\r\nstockings and dainty little boots.  She held a small white parasol,\r\nbordered with pink, and deeply fringed, over her head, and held a\r\ngold-clasped Prayer-Book in her hand; and Miss Fosbrook heard a little\r\nsigh, which told her that this was the being whom Elizabeth Merrifield\r\nthought the happiest in the world.  She hoped it was not all for the fine\r\nclothes; and Sam muttered,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a little figure of fun!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartin and Osmond Greville went daily to Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s, like Sam and Hal,\r\nso the boys ran on to them; and Mrs. Greville, turning round, showed a\r\nvery pleasant face as she bowed to Miss Fosbrook, and shaking hands with\r\nSusan and Elizabeth, asked with much solicitude after their mamma, and\r\nhow lately they had heard of her.\r\n\r\nSusan was too simple and straightforward to be shy, and answered readily,\r\nthat they had had letters, and Mamma had been sadly tired by the journey,\r\nbut was better the next day.  The little girls shook hands; and Mrs.\r\nGreville made a kind of introduction by nodding towards her companion,\r\nand murmuring something about \xe2\x80\x9cFr\xc3\xa4ulein Munsterthal;\xe2\x80\x9d and Miss Fosbrook\r\nfound herself walking beside a lady with the least of all bonnets, a\r\nprofusion of fair hair, and a good-humoured, one-coloured face, no doubt\r\nMiss Ida\xe2\x80\x99s German governess.  She said something about the fine day, and\r\nreceived an answer, but what it was she could not guess, whether German,\r\nFrench, or English, and her own knowledge of the two first languages was\r\nbetter for reading than for speaking; so after an awkward attempt or two,\r\nshe held her peace and looked at her companions.\r\n\r\nSusan and Mrs. Greville seemed to be getting on very well together; but\r\nElizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s admiration of Ida seemed to be speechless, for they were\r\nwalking side by side without a word, perhaps too close to their elders to\r\ntalk.\r\n\r\nAnnie and David were going on steadily hand in hand a little way off; and\r\nMiss Fosbrook chiefly heard the talk of the boys, who had fallen behind;\r\nperhaps her ears were quickened by its personality, for though Sam was\r\nsaying, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what, she\xe2\x80\x99s a famous fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d the rejoinder was,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! do you mean to say that you mind her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal\xe2\x80\x99s voice; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, she sent him away from tea last\r\nnight, just for shying crusts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did he go?\xe2\x80\x9d and there was a disagreeable sounding laugh, in which\r\nshe was sorry that Hal joined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch the Fr\xc3\xa4ulein serving me so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe never tries!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knows better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Sam, I thought you had more spirit.  You\xe2\x80\x99ll be sitting up\r\npricking holes in a frill by the time the Captain comes back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Hal will be mincing along with his toes turned out like a\r\ndancing-master!\xe2\x80\x9d continued an affected voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo such thing!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal angrily: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not a fellow to be ordered\r\nabout!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Grevilles laughed; and one of them said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nshow it?  I\xe2\x80\x99d soon send her to the right-about if she tried to interfere\r\nwith me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook could bear it no longer; and facing suddenly round, looked\r\nthe speaker full in the face, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94but you should not speak quite so loud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys shrank back out of countenance; and Sam, who alone had not\r\nspoken, looked up into her face with a merry air, as if he were gratified\r\nby her spirited way of discomfiting them.\r\n\r\nOsmond tried to recover, and muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a sell!\xe2\x80\x9d rather impudently;\r\nbut they were now near the churchyard, and Mrs. Greville turning round,\r\nall was hushed.\r\n\r\nChristabel felt much vexed that all this should have happened just before\r\ngoing into church; she felt a good deal ruffled herself, and feared that\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s head was filled with nonsense, if Hal\xe2\x80\x99s were not with something\r\nworse.\r\n\r\nThe church looked pretty outside, with the old weather-boarded wooden\r\nbelfry rising above the tiled roof and western gable; and it was neatly\r\nkept but not pretty within, the walls all done over with pale buff wash,\r\nand the wood-work very clumsy.  Sam and Susan behaved well and\r\nattentively; but Bessie fidgeted into her mamma\xe2\x80\x99s place, and would stand\r\nupon a hassock.  Miss Fosbrook was much afraid it was to keep in sight of\r\nthe beautiful bird.  Hal yawned; and Johnnie not only fidgeted unbearably\r\nhimself, but made his sister Annie do the same, till Miss Fosbrook\r\nscarcely felt as if she was at church, and made up her mind to tell\r\nJohnnie that she should leave him at home with the babies unless he\r\nchanged his ways.  Little David went on most steadily with his\r\nPrayer-Book, and scarcely looked off it till the sermon, when he fell\r\nasleep.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook had one pleasure as she was going home.  The children had\r\nall gone on some steps before her, chattering eagerly among themselves,\r\nwhen Sam turned back and said abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\n_that_, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat those boys were saying? It depends on you whether you make me mind\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to make any rows if I can help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I hope you will be able to help it!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I\r\nshould do if you did!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam gave an odd smile with his honest face.  \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a good\r\nspirit of your own.  It would take something to cow you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray don\xe2\x80\x99t try!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam laughed, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI did promise Papa to be conformable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I was very much obliged to you yesterday evening.  The behaviour of\r\nthe other boys depends so much on you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it so much now I see you can\r\nstand up for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides, what would it be if I had to write to your father that I could\r\nnot manage such a bear-garden?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take care that sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t happen,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt would hinder\r\nall the good to Mamma!  I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, after a\r\nconfidential pause, \xe2\x80\x9cif we get beyond you, there\xe2\x80\x99s Mr. Carey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you did not mean to get beyond me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam looked a little disconcerted, and it struck her that, though he would\r\nnot say so, he was doubtful whether the Greville influence might not\r\nrender Henry unmanageable; but he quickly gave it another turn.  \xe2\x80\x9cOnly\r\nyou must not plague us about London manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean by London manners.  Do you mean not bawling\r\nat tea? for I mean to insist upon that, I assure you, and I want you to\r\nhelp me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! not being finikin, and mincing, and nonsensical!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I\xe2\x80\x99m not so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, laughing heartily; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntell you one thing, Sam, that I do wish you would leave off\xe2\x80\x94and that is\r\nteazing.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether that is country manners, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nto see a sensible kind fellow like you just go out of your way to say\r\nsomething mortifying to a younger one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is fun.  They like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they really like it, there is no objection.  I know I should like\r\nvery much to have my brother here quizzing me; but you know very well\r\nthere are two sorts of such fun, and one that is only sport to the\r\nstronger side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBessie is so ridiculous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the very one I want to protect.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that teazing her\r\ndoes any good; it only gives her cross feelings.  And she really has more\r\nright on her side than you think.  You might be just as honest and bold\r\nif you were less rude and bearish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to see her so affected and perked up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not affectation.  She is really more gentle and quiet than you\r\nare; you don\xe2\x80\x99t think it so in your Mamma, and she is like her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma is not like Bessie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then about Davy.  How could you go and stop the poor little boy when\r\nhe was trying to think and feel rightly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was so funny,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will think another time whether your fun is safe and kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can\xe2\x80\x99t be so particular,\xe2\x80\x9d he said impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear it.  I thought the only way to do right was to be\r\nparticular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe grunted, and flung away from her.  She was vexed to have sent him off\r\nin such a mood; but, unmannerly as he was, she saw so much good in him,\r\nthat she could not but hope he would be her friend and ally.\r\n\r\nDinner went off very peaceably, and then Susan fetched her two darlings\r\nfrom the nursery, George and Sarah, of three years and eighteen months\r\nold.  Her great perfection was as a motherly elder sister; and even Sam\r\nwas gentle to these little things, and played with them very nicely.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook reminded Hal of his Collect; but he observed that there was\r\nplenty of time, and continued to stand by the window, pursuing the flies\r\nwith his finger, not killing them, but tormenting them and David very\r\nseriously, by making them think he would\xe2\x80\x94not a very pretty business for\r\nthe day when all things should be happy, more like that which is always\r\nfound \xe2\x80\x9cfor idle hands to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvening service-time put an end to this sport; but Miss Fosbrook could\r\nnot set off till after a severe conflict with Johnnie.  She had decreed\r\nthat he should not go again that day, after his behaviour in the morning;\r\nand perhaps he would not have minded this punishment much if David had\r\nnot been going, which made him think it a disgrace.  So, in the most\r\nindependent manner be put on his hat, and was marching off, when Miss\r\nFosbrook stood in front of him, and ordered him back.\r\n\r\nHe repeated, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to church.\xe2\x80\x9d  It was plain enough that he had\r\nheard what those boys had said about not submitting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChurch is not the place to go to in a fit of wilfulness, Johnnie,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid; and his sisters broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cO Johnnie!\xe2\x80\x9d but the naughty boy,\r\nfancying, perhaps, that want of time would lead to his getting his own\r\nway, marched on, sticking up his toes very high in the air.\r\n\r\nHal laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohnnie, Johnnie dear,\xe2\x80\x9d entreated Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat would Mamma say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn would not hear, and walked on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cif you do not come back directly, I must\r\ncarry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had measured her strength with his: he was only eight years old, and\r\nshe believed that she could carry him; but he heard the church-bells\r\nringing, and thought he should have his way.\r\n\r\nShe laid hold of him, and he began fighting and kicking, in stout shoes,\r\nwhose thumps were no joke.  She held fast, but she felt frightened, and\r\ndoubtful of the issue of the struggle; and again there was Hal laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor-shame, Henry!\xe2\x80\x9d burst out Sam; and the same moment those two feet\r\nwere secured, and John was a prisoner.  Miss Fosbrook called out to the\r\nrest to go on to church, and she and Sam dragged the boy up to the\r\nnursery, and shut him in there, roaring passionately.\r\n\r\nNurse Freeman, knowing nothing about it, could not believe but that the\r\nstranger lady had made her child naughty, and said something about their\r\nMamma letting him go to church; and \xe2\x80\x9cwhen the child wished to go to\r\nchurch, it seemed strange he should not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook would not defend herself, for she was in great haste; but\r\nSam exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cStuff! he was as naughty as could be all this morning,\r\nand only wanted to go now because he was told not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohnnie bellowed out something else, but Miss Fosbrook would not let Sam\r\ngo on; she touched his arm, and drew him off with her, he exclaiming,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoolish old Freeman! she will pet and spoil him all church-time, till he\r\nis worse than ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was lucky for her that she was too much hurried to dwell on this\r\nvexation; she almost ran to save herself from being late, and scarcely\r\nheard Sam\xe2\x80\x99s mutterings about wishing to break Martin Greville\xe2\x80\x99s head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou need not hurry so much,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s a shorter cut, only I\r\nsuppose you can\xe2\x80\x99t get through a gap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d she laughed; and he led her on straight through the\r\nShort-horns.  Some of them looked at her more than she fancied, but she\r\nknew she might give up all hopes of Sam if he detected her fears.  Then\r\ncame the gap, where a tree had been cut down in the hedge, and such a\r\njump down from it!  But she gathered up her muslin, and made her leap so\r\ngallantly, that the boy cried,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah! well done!\xe2\x80\x9d and came and walked close to her, saying\r\nconfidentially, \xe2\x80\x9cI say, do you think we shall ever do the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure it might be done.  If you are likely to do it you must know\r\nbetter than I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I much care about it.  It will be rather a bother;\r\nonly now we have said it, I shall hate it if we don\xe2\x80\x99t do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the pleasure of giving it will be a delightful reward for a\r\nlittle self-command.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly Hal and the girls will make such a work about it.  I\xe2\x80\x99m glad, after\r\nall, that Bessie has nothing to do with it, or she would want to dress it\r\nup in flowers and ribbons.  Ha-ha!  But what a little crab it is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be too sure of that.  People may have other designs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBessie\xe2\x80\x99s can\xe2\x80\x99t be anything but trumpery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes present trumpery is a step to something better.  \xe2\x80\x98A was an\r\nArcher\xe2\x80\x99 is not very wise, but it is the road to reading\xe2\x80\x94and even if it\r\nwere not so, Sam, it is not right to shame people into giving; for what\r\nis not bestowed for the true reasons, does no good to giver nor to\r\nreceiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam looked up with a frown of attention, as if he were trying to take in\r\nthe new light; but he did take it in, and smacking his hands together\r\nwith a noise like a pistol-shot, said, \xe2\x80\x9cAy, that\xe2\x80\x99s it!  We don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nwhat is grudged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook thought of words that would another time be more familiar\r\nto Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cNot grudgingly, nor of necessity, for God loveth a cheerful\r\ngiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat she said was, \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, if you plague Bessie too much, to make her\r\nlike ourselves, when she is really so different, you are driving her to\r\nthe shamming you despise so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut ought not she to be cured of being silly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we have quite made up our minds upon what silliness is.  There, the\r\nbell has stopped.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE most part of church-time Johnnie was eating Nurse Freeman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nplum-cake.  Perhaps this did not make him any easier in the conscience,\r\nbut he had a very unlucky sentiment, that as he was already naughty and\r\nin disgrace, it was of no use to take the trouble of being good till he\r\ncould make a fresh beginning; and after what the Grevilles had said, he\r\ndid not think that would be till Papa and Mamma came home; he did not at\r\nall mean to give in to a girl that was not even twenty.  So he would not\r\nturn to the only wise thing he could have done, the learning of his\r\nCollect, but he teased Nurse out of more cake and more, and got what play\r\nhe could out of little George, and that was not much, for Johnnie was not\r\nin a temper to be pleasant with a little one.\r\n\r\nComing home from church, Collects were to be learnt and said before tea:\r\nbut Hal, after glancing over his own, took up his cap and said, \xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\nalong, Sam, Purday will be feeding the pigs; I want to choose the size of\r\nours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve not done,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa never said we were to say them to Miss Fosbrook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe meant it though,\xe2\x80\x9d was all Sam\xe2\x80\x99s answer.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t hinder me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve no notion of being bound by what people mean,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Hal;\r\nand no one could imagine the torment he made himself, neither going nor\r\nstaying, arguing the matter with his elder brother, as if Sam\xe2\x80\x99s coming\r\nwould justify him, and interrupting everyone; till at last Miss Fosbrook\r\ngathered all her spirit, and ordered him either to sit down and learn\r\nproperly at once, or to go quite away.  She was very much vexed, for\r\nHenry had been the most obliging and good-natured of all at first, and\r\nlikely to be fond of her; but such a great talker could not fail to be\r\nweak, and his vanity had been set against her.  He looked saucy at first,\r\nand much inclined to resist; if he had seen any sympathy for him in Sam\r\nhe might have done so, but Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s steady eye was too much for\r\nhim, so he saved his dignity, as he thought, by exclaiming, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want to stay in this stuffy hole with such a set of owls; I shall\r\ngo to Purday.\xe2\x80\x9d  And off he marched.\r\n\r\nThe others stayed, and said their Collects and Catechism very\r\nrespectably, all but John, who had not learned the Collect at all, and\r\nwas sent into another room to finish it, to which he made no resistance;\r\nhe had had enough of actual fighting with Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nThen she offered to read a story to the others, but she found that this\r\nwas distasteful even to her friend Sam; he thought it stupid to be read\r\nto, and said he should see after Hal; David trotted after him, and Susan\r\nand Anne repaired to the nursery to play with the little ones and the\r\nbaby.  She minded it the less, as they all had some purpose; but she had\r\nalready been vexed to find that all but Davy preferred the most arrant\r\nvacant idleness to anything rational.  To be sure, Susan sometimes,\r\nBessie and Hal always, would read any book that made no pretensions to be\r\ninstructive, but even a fact about a lion or an elephant made them detect\r\nwisdom in disguise, and throw it aside.  She thought, however, she would\r\nmake the most of Bessie, and asked whether she would like to hear\r\nreading, or read to herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie; and there was a silence, while Miss Fosbrook,\r\nglad of the quiet, began reading her _Christian Year_.
