Presently she\r\nheard a voice so low that it seemed at a distance and it made her start,\r\nfor it was saying \xe2\x80\x9cChristabel!\xe2\x80\x9d then she almost laughed, for it seemed to\r\nhave been an audacious experiment, to judge by little Elizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s scared\r\nlooks and the glow on her cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I say it sometimes when we are alone together?\xe2\x80\x9d she said timidly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do like it so much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is such a pleasure to you, I would not deprive you of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMiss Fosbrook, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cbut don\xe2\x80\x99t do so, except when we are alone, for\r\nyour Mamma would not like me to seem younger still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thank you!  Isn\xe2\x80\x99t it a nice secret?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie, clinging to her\r\nhand: \xe2\x80\x9cand will you let me hug you sometimes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA little love was pleasant to Miss Fosbrook, when she was feeling lonely,\r\nand she took Bessie in her lap, and they exchanged caresses, to the\r\ndamage of the collar that Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s sister had worked for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you don\xe2\x80\x99t call me silly?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat depends,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer, with some arch fun; but Bessie had not\r\nmuch turn for fun, and presently went on\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you saw Ida Greville?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you think of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had not much opportunity of learning what to think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut her parasol, and her bird!  Did you think her mama very silly to\r\ngive her pretty things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, certainly not, unless she wore them at unsuitable times, or thought\r\ntoo much about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIda has so many, she does not think of them at all.  And she has shells,\r\nand such a lovely work-box, and picture-books; she has all she wants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you quite sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, quite sure! and they don\xe2\x80\x99t tease her for liking pretty things;\r\nher brothers keep quite away, and never bother about the schoolroom; but\r\nshe learns Italian and German, and drawing and singing.  Mr. Greville\r\nsaid something about our spending the day there.  Oh! if we do but go!\r\nWon\xe2\x80\x99t you, Miss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I am asked, and if your Mamma would wish it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mamma always lets us go, except once\xe2\x80\x94when\xe2\x80\x94when\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I cried,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, hanging down her head; \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nit.  It did seem so tiresome here, and she said I was learning to be\r\ndiscontented; but nobody can help wishing, can they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere must be a way of not breaking the Tenth Commandment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t covet; I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to take things away from Ida, only to have\r\nthe same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but what does the explanation at the end of the Duty to our\r\nNeighbour say, filling out that Commandment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see what Susie is doing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.\r\n\r\nChristabel sighed as the little girl walked off, displeased at having her\r\nrepinings set before her in a graver light than that in which she had\r\nhitherto chosen to regard them.\r\n\r\nShe saw no more of her charges till tea-time, when the bell brought them\r\nfrom different quarters, Johnnie with such a grimy collar and dirty\r\nhands, that he was a very un-Sunday-like figure, and she would have sent\r\nhim away to make himself decent, but that she was desirous of not\r\nover-tormenting him.\r\n\r\nSunday was always celebrated by having treacle with the bread, so the\r\nbutter riot was happily escaped; and Bessie was not in a gracious mood,\r\nand the corners of her mouth provoked the boys to begin on what they knew\r\nwould make her afford them sport.  Hal first: \xe2\x80\x9cI say, Bet, didn\xe2\x80\x99t Purday\r\nwant his gun to-day at church?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth put out her lip in expectation that something unpleasant was\r\nintended, and other voices were not slow to ask an explanation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShooting the cocky-olly birds!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA general explosion of laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say (always the preface to the boy\xe2\x80\x99s wit), shall I get a jay down off\r\nthe barn to stick into your hat, Betty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said such a deplorable offended voice, that Sam, who had\r\nreally held his tongue at first, could not help chiming in,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; a cock-sparrow, for her London manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that\xe2\x80\x99s for me, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel good-humouredly.  \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nLondon-bred sparrow; a pert forward chit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe really had found a safety-valve; the boys were entertained, and\r\ndiverted from their attack on their favourite victim, by finding everyone\r\nan appropriate bird; and when they came to \xe2\x80\x9cTomtits\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cDishwashers,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwere so astonished at Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s never having seen either, that they\r\ninstantly fell into the greatest haste to finish their tea, and conduct\r\nher into the garden, and through a course of birds, eggs, and nests,\r\nabout which, as soon as she was assured that there was to be no\r\nbird\xe2\x80\x99s-nesting, she was very eager.\r\n\r\nBessie ought to have been thankful that her persecutors were called off,\r\nbut she was in a dismal mood, and was taken with a fit of displeasure\r\nthat her own Christabel Angela was following the rabble rout into the\r\ngarden, instead of staying in the school-room at her service.\r\n\r\nThe reason of her gloom was, that Miss Fosbrook had spoken a word that\r\nshe did not choose to take home, and yet which she could not shake off.\r\nSo she would neither stay in nor go out cheerfully, and sauntered along\r\nlooking so piteous, that Johnnie could not help making her worse by\r\nplucking at her dress, by suddenly twisting her cape round till the back\r\nwas in front, and pushing her hat over her eyes, till \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t Johnnie,\xe2\x80\x9d in\r\na dismal whine, alternated with \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell Miss Fosbrook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristabel did not see nor hear.  She had gone forward with a boy on\r\neither side of her, and Susan walking backwards in front, all telling the\r\nstory of a cuckoo,\xe2\x80\x94or gowk, as Sara called it in Purday\xe2\x80\x99s language,\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nthey had found in a water-wagtail\xe2\x80\x99s nest in a heap of stones; how it sat\r\nup, constantly gaping with its huge mouth, while the poor little\r\nfoster-parents toiled to their utmost to keep it supplied with\r\ncaterpillars, and the last time it was seen, when full-fledged, were\r\ntrying to lure it to come out of the nest by holding up green palmers at\r\nsome little distance before it.  This was in the evening; by morning it\r\nwas gone, having probably taken flight at sunrise.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook listened with all the pleasure the boys could desire.  She\r\nhad read natural history, and looked at birds stuffed in the British\r\nMuseum, or alive at the Zoological Gardens, on the rare days when her\r\nfather had time to give himself and his children a treat; and her fresh\r\nvalue and interest in all these country things were delightful to the\r\nboys.\r\n\r\nIt was a lovely summer evening.  The sun was low enough to make the\r\nshadows long and refreshing, as they lay upon the blooming grass of the\r\nwilderness, softly swaying in the breeze, all pale with its numerous\r\nchaffy blossoms, and varied by the tall buttercups that raised up their\r\nshining yellow heads, or by white clouds of bold-faced ox-eye daisies.\r\n\r\nThe pear-trees were like white garlands; the apple-trees covered with\r\nwhite blossoms and rosy buds; the climbing roses on the wall were\r\nbursting into blossom; the sky was one blue vault without a cloud.\r\n\r\nSurely Elizabeth had no lack here of what was pretty.  Then why did she\r\nlag behind, unseeing, unheeding of all, but peevishly pushing off John\r\nand Anne, thinking that they always teased her worst on Sundays, and very\r\nmuch discomfited that Miss Fosbrook was not attending to her?  Surely the\r\nfault was not altogether in what was outside her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee!\xe2\x80\x9d cry the boys.  Miss Fosbrook must first look up there, high upon\r\nthe side of the house, niched behind that thick stem of the vine.  What,\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t she see those round black eyes and little beak? They see her plain\r\nenough.  Ah! now she has them.  That\xe2\x80\x99s a fly-catcher.  By and by they\r\nshall be able to show her the old birds flying round, catching flies on\r\nthe wing, and feeding the young ones, all perched in a row.\r\n\r\nNow, can she scramble up the laurels?  Yes, she hopes so; though she\r\nwished she had known what was coming, for she would have changed her\r\nSunday muslin.  But a look of anxiety came on Sam\xe2\x80\x99s face as he peeped\r\ninto the clump of laurels; he signed back the others, sprang upon the\r\ndark scraggy bough of the tree, and Hal called out,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone! has Ralph been there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, the black rascal; at least, I suppose so.  Not an egg left, and they\r\nwould have hatched this week!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Purday calls him his best friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Harry.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe says we should\r\nnot get a currant or a gooseberry if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t for that there raven, as\r\nPapa won\xe2\x80\x99t have the small birds shot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring down the nest, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cGeorgy will like to have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children behind, who never could hear of anything to be had without\r\nlaying a claim to it, shouted that they wanted the nest; but Sam said Sue\r\nhad spoken first, and they fell back discontented, and more bent on their\r\nunkind sport.  Miss Fosbrook was rather shocked at the tearing out the\r\nnest, and asked if the old bird would not have another brood there; but\r\nit was explained that a thrush would never return to a forsaken nest; and\r\nwhen Sam came down with it in his hand, she was delighted with the\r\nwonderful cup that formed the lining, so smooth and firm a bason formed\r\nof dried mud set within the grassy wall.  She had thought that swallows\r\nalone built with mud, and had to learn that the swallows used their clay\r\nfor their outer walls, and down for their lining, whereas the thrush is a\r\nregular plasterer.\r\n\r\nSam promised her another thrush to make up for her disappointment, and\r\nmeantime conducted her to a very untidy old summer-house, the moss of\r\nwhose roof hung down loose and rough over a wild collection of headless\r\nwooden horses, little ships with torn sails, long sticks, battered\r\nwatering-pots, and old garden tools.  She was desired to look up to one\r\nof the openings in the ragged moss, and believe that it housed a kitty\r\nwren\xe2\x80\x99s family of sixteen or eighteen; but she had to take this on trust,\r\nfor to lay a finger near would lead to desertion; in fact, Sam was rather\r\nsorry to be able to point out to her, on coming out, the tiny, dark,\r\nnutmeg, cock-tailed father kitty, popping in and out of the thorn hedge,\r\nspying at the party.\r\n\r\nNow then for a wonder as they came out.  Sam waved everybody away\xe2\x80\x94nay,\r\nwaved is a small word for what he did\xe2\x80\x94shouted, pushed, ordered, would be\r\nmore like it.  He was going to give Miss Fosbrook such a proof of his\r\nesteem as hardly any one enjoyed, not even Hal, twice in the summer.\r\n\r\nEverybody submitted to his violent demonstrations, and Christabel\r\nfollowed him to the back of the summer-house.  There stood a large red\r\nflower-pot upside down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Miss Fosbrook!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam\xe2\x80\x99s finger hooked into the hole at the top.  Off came the flower-pot,\r\nand disclosed something flying off with rushing wings, and something\r\nconfused remaining,\xe2\x80\x94a cluster of grey wings all quill, with gaping yellow\r\nmouths here and there opening, a huddling movement always going on in the\r\nforlorn heap, as if each were cold, and wanted to be undermost.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTits, my tits!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam triumphantly; \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99ve built their nest here\r\nthree years following.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do they get in and out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThrough the hole.  Take care, I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you frighten away the bird?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear no!  Ox-eyes aren\xe2\x80\x99t like wrens; I go to them every day.  See!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand he took up in his hand a creature that could just be seen to be\r\nintended for a bird, though the long skinny neck was bare, and the tiny\r\nquills of the young wings only showed a little grey sprouting feather, as\r\ndid the breast some primrose-coloured down.  Miss Fosbrook had to part\r\nwith some favourite cockney notions of the beauty of infant birds, and on\r\nthe other hand to gain a vivid idea of what is meant by \xe2\x80\x9ccallow young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam quickly put his nestling back, and showed her the parent.  She could\r\nhardly believe that the handsome bird in the smooth grey coat and bright\r\nstraw-coloured waistcoat, with the broad jet-black line down the centre,\r\nthe great white cheeks edged with black, and the bold knowing look, could\r\nbe like what the little bits of deformity in the nest would soon become.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, that\xe2\x80\x99s an ox-eye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll hear them going on\r\npeter\xe2\x80\x94peter\xe2\x80\x94peter\xe2\x80\x94all the spring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sam was cut short by a loud and lamentable burst of roaring where\r\nthey had left the party.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook hurried back, hearing Hal\xe2\x80\x99s rude laugh as she came nearer,\r\nit was Elizabeth, sobbing in the passionate way in which it is not good\r\nto see a child cry, and violently shaking off Susan, who was begging her\r\nto stop herself before Miss Fosbrook should come.\r\n\r\nWhat _was_ the matter?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!  Betty\xe2\x80\x99s nonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohnnie _did_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohnnie only\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell-tale!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cCry-baby!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe only cried that Miss Fosbrook might hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo shouted the little Babel, Bessie sobbing resentfully between her\r\nwords, till Miss Fosbrook, insisting that everybody should be quiet,\r\ndesired her to tell what had happened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohnnie\xe2\x80\x94Johnnie called me a toad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe others all burst out laughing, and Miss Fosbrook, trying to silence\r\nthem with a frown, said it was very rude of John, but she saw no reason\r\nwhy a girl of Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s age should act so childish a part.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been teasing me, and so has Anne, all this time!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve been at me ever since I came out, pulling me and plaguing me,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cI told you to walk in front of Miss Fosbrook, where\r\nthey could not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t do anything to her,\xe2\x80\x9d said John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Johnnie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe only pulled her frock and poked her ankles,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne pleadingly\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly\xe2\x80\x94and why did you do what she did not like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohnnie looked sturdy and cross.  Anne hung her head; and Elizabeth burst\r\nout again,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey always do\xe2\x80\x94they always are cross to me!  I said I\xe2\x80\x99d tell you, and\r\nnow they said Ida was a conceited little toad, and stingy Bet was\r\nanother;\xe2\x80\x9d and out burst her howls again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA very sad and improper way of spending a Sunday evening,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook, who had really grown quite angry.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnne and John, I _will_ put\r\nan end to this teasing.  Go to bed this instant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey did not dare to disobey, but went off slowly with sulky footsteps,\r\nmuttering to one another that Miss Fosbrook always took pipy Betty\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npart; Nurse said so, and they wished Mamma was at home.  And when they\r\ncame up to the nursery, Nurse pitied them.  She had never heard of a\r\nyoung lady doing such a thing as ordering off two poor dear children to\r\nbed for only just saying a word; but it seemed there were to be\r\nfavourites now.  No, she could not put them to bed; they must wait till\r\nMary came in from her walk; she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to put herself out of the\r\nway for any fine London governess.\r\n\r\nSo Johnnie had another conquest over Miss Fosbrook; but Anne was\r\nuncomfortable, and went and sat in a corner, wishing she had had her\r\npunishment properly over, and kicking her brother away when he wanted to\r\nplay with her.\r\n\r\nAs for Bessie, she only cried the more for Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s trying to talk\r\nto her.  It was a way of hers, perhaps from being less strong than the\r\nothers, if once she started in a cry she could not leave off.\r\n\r\nSusan told Miss Fosbrook so; and the boys tried to drag her on with a\r\npromise of a blackbird\xe2\x80\x99s nest; but she thought them unfeeling to such\r\nwoeful distress, and first tried to reason with Bessie, then to soothe\r\nher, till at last, finding all in vain, she thought bed the only place\r\nfor the child, and led her into the house, helped her, still shaking with\r\nsobs, to undress, and was going to see her lie down in the bed which she\r\nshared with Susan.  Elizabeth was still young enough to say her prayers\r\naloud.  The words came out in the middle of choking sobs, not as if she\r\nwere much attending to them.  Miss Fosbrook knelt down by her as she was\r\ngoing to rise, and said in her own words,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost merciful God, give unto this Thy child the spirit of content, and\r\nthe spirit of love, that she may bear patiently all the little trials\r\nthat hurt and vex her, and win her way as Thy good soldier and servant.\r\nAmen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth held her breath to listen.  It was new and odd.  She did not\r\nlike to say Amen; she did not know if the governess were not taking a\r\nliberty.  Perhaps it was a new way of telling her she was\r\nwrong\xe2\x80\x94Christabel, whom she had thought on her side.\r\n\r\nThe bad temper woke up, and would not let her offer a friendly kiss.  She\r\nhid her face in the pillow, and as soon as Miss Fosbrook had shut the\r\ndoor, went off into a fresh gust of piteous sobs, because Miss Elizabeth\r\nMerrifield was the most miserable ill-used child in all the world.\r\n\r\nShe might be one of the most miserable, but it was not because of her\r\nill-usage, but because she had no spirit to be cheerful, and had turned\r\naway from comfort of the right kind.  She was in such a frame as to\r\nprefer thinking everyone against her, to supposing that anything she\r\ncould do would mend matters.\r\n\r\nChristabel was much grieved at this unfortunate end to the Sunday\r\nevening.  She looked over all the boys\xe2\x80\x99 birds\xe2\x80\x99 eggs\xe2\x80\x94they were allowed to\r\nkeep two of every sort as curiosities\xe2\x80\x94and listened to some wonderful\r\nstories of Henry\xe2\x80\x99s about climbing trees, and shooting partridges, and she\r\nkept the remaining children quiet and amused; but she was not happy in\r\nher mind.\r\n\r\nShe thought she must have been wrong in not watching them more closely,\r\nand she felt more dislike and indignation against Johnnie than she feared\r\nwas altogether right in his governess.  Also, she feared to make too much\r\nof Elizabeth, and was almost afraid that notice taught her to be still\r\nmore fretful.  And yet there was a sense of being drawn to her by their\r\ntwo minds understanding each other, by likeness of tastes, by pity, and\r\nby a wish to protect one whom her little world oppressed.\r\n\r\nNurse Freeman could not be more afraid of Miss Fosbrook making favourites\r\nthan she was herself.\r\n\r\nAll she could do in the matter was that which she had already done at\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s bedside, and much more fully than when the little girl was\r\nlistening to her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\n\r\n\r\nWITH Monday morning began the earning of the pig.  Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s first\r\nbusiness after prayers was to deal out the week\xe2\x80\x99s allowance\xe2\x80\x94sixpence to\r\neach of the four elders, threepence apiece to the three younger ones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay there be no fines,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll not have the hundredth part of a fine!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Henry, tossing his\r\nmoney into the air.\r\n\r\nLittle David\xe2\x80\x99s set lips expressed the same purpose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease let me have a whole sixpence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any\r\nchange, I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t spend it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Sukey! you\xe2\x80\x99d better have the four farthings,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe the first to want them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan laughed; and Miss Fosbrook, partly as an example to the plaintive\r\nElizabeth, said, \xe2\x80\x9cYou are so good-humoured, Susie, that I can\xe2\x80\x99t find it\r\nin my heart to demand a fine\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94your hair; and there,\xe2\x80\x9d pointing to the\r\nstout red fingers, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you ever behold such a black little row?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan, in her good-humoured hearty voice, \xe2\x80\x9chow tiresome,\r\nwhen they were _so_ clean this morning, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve only just been feeding\r\nthe chicken, and up in the hay-loft for the eggs, and pulling the\r\nradishes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, go and wash and brush, and to-morrow remember the pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook, unable to help comparing the radishes and the fingers for\r\nredness and for earthiness.\r\n\r\nIt was a more difficult matter when, as Elizabeth put her silver coin\r\ninto her purse, John must needs repeat the stupid old joke, \xe2\x80\x9cThere goes\r\nstingy Bet!\xe2\x80\x9d and Bessie put on her woeful appealing face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn, I shall punish you if I hear those words again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.  Nurse says you have no business to punish me!  She did\r\nnot put me to bed; and I had such fun!  Oh, such fun!\xe2\x80\x9d and the boy looked\r\nup with a grin that set all the others laughing.\r\n\r\nChristabel resolutely kept silence, and hoped her looks did not show her\r\nannoyance, as the boy went on, \xe2\x80\x9cI got lots of goodies, for Nurse said she\r\nhad no notion of no stranger punishing her children.  Oh!  Oh!  Oh!\xe2\x80\x9d  For\r\nSamuel had hold of his ear, and was tweaking it sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!  Go and tell Nurse, if you like, baby!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam, indeed I can\xe2\x80\x99t have my battles fought in that way!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the\r\ngoverness, much distressed, as Johnnie roared, perhaps that old Nurse\r\nmight hear, and, to all attempts to find out whether he were hurt,\r\noffered only heels and fists, till Susan came back and hugged him into\r\nquiet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow Johnnie has cried before breakfast on a Monday morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9call the rest of the week will go wrong with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope no such thing.\xe2\x80\x94Suppose we try and\r\nshow Annie she is wrong, Johnnie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Johnnie was sulky, and even Susan looked as if she thought this a new\r\nand dangerous notion.  Sam laughed, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you joy, Miss\r\nFosbrook.  Now he\xe2\x80\x99ll think he must be naughty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohnnie,\xe2\x80\x9d said David solemnly, \xe2\x80\x9cthe pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pig was a very good master of the ceremonies, and kept all elbows off\r\nthe table at breakfast-time; and Bessie, who was apt to stick fast in the\r\nmidst of her bread and milk, and fall into disgrace for daintiness and\r\ndawdling, finished off quietly and prosperously.\r\n\r\nThen every one was turned loose till nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock.  Susan had charge of\r\nMamma\xe2\x80\x99s keys, and had to go down to the kitchen, see what the cook\r\nwanted, and put it out, but only on condition that no brother or sister\r\never went with her to the store-closet.  Susan was highly trustworthy,\r\nbut Mamma was too wise to let her be tempted by voices begging for one\r\nplum, one almond, or the last spoonful of Jam.  It took away a great deal\r\nof the pleasure of jingling the keys, and having a voice in choosing the\r\npudding.\r\n\r\nThe two elder boys went to their tutor, the other children to the\r\nnursery, except Elizabeth, who was rummaging in her little box, and\r\nDavid, whom Miss Fosbrook found perched on the ledge of the window,\r\nreading a book that did not look as if it were meant for men of his size.\r\n\r\nBut Miss Fosbrook thought David like the oldest person in the\r\nhouse\xe2\x80\x94infinitely older than John, who could do nothing better than he\r\nexcept running and bawling, and a good deal older than even Hal and Sam.\r\nNay, there were times when he raised his steady eyes and slowly spoke out\r\nhis thoughts, when she felt as if he were much more wise and serious than\r\nher twenty-years old self.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Davy,\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, as at the sound of the lesson-bell the little\r\nold man uncrossed his sturdy legs, closed his book, and arose with a\r\nsigh, \xe2\x80\x9chave you found out all about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have found out why a pig is a profitable investment,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered\r\ngravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he will feed upon refuse, and fatten upon cheap food,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavid, in the words of his book; \xe2\x80\x9conly I can\xe2\x80\x99t make out why.  Do you\r\nknow, Miss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite see what you want to know, Davy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to know why a pig gets fat on barley-meal, when an ox wants\r\nmange, and oil-cake and hay.  I asked Nurse, and she said little boys\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t ask questions; and I asked Purday, and he said it was because\r\npigs is pigs, and oxen is oxen.  Why do you think it is, Miss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think; I know it is because the great God has made one sort of\r\ncreature to be easily fed, and made good for poor people to live upon,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were fixed on her as if he still had questions to ask, and\r\nshe was quite afraid of her powers of answering them, for he was new in\r\nthe world, and saw the strangeness of many things to which older people\r\nbecome used by living with them, but which are not the less strange for\r\nall that.\r\n\r\nHowever, the trampling of many feet put an end to question and answer,\r\nand the day\xe2\x80\x99s work had to begin with the Psalms, and reading the Morning\r\nLessons.  Bessie was by far the best reader; and David did very well,\r\nthough he made very long stops to look deliberately at any long new word,\r\nand could not bear to be told before he had mastered it for himself.\r\nEven Susan was sadly given to gabbling and missing the little words that\r\nshe thought beneath her attention; and the other two stumbled so\r\nhorribly, that it was pain to hear them.\r\n\r\nThis beginning might be taken as the sign of how all would do their\r\nlessons.  It is only a child here and there, generally a lonely one, to\r\nwhom lessons can be anything but a toil and an obligation.  Even with\r\nclever ones, who may be interested in some part of their study, some\r\nother branch will be disagreeable; and there is nothing in the whole\r\nworld to be learnt without drudgery, so it would be unreasonable to\r\nexpect lessons to be regarded as delightful; but there is one thing that\r\nis to be expected of any good child\xe2\x80\x94not to enjoy lessons; not to surpass\r\nothers; not to do anything surprising; only to make a conscience of doing\r\nwhat is required as well as possible.\r\n\r\nNow do not many children seem to think that they are to receive as little\r\nas they can possibly take in without being punished; or that, if they\r\nmake any exertion, their teachers ought to be so much obliged to them,\r\nthat some great praise or reward is due to them?\r\n\r\nLet us see whether anyone in Stokesley school-room was making a\r\nconscience of the day\xe2\x80\x99s tasks.  It is not of much use to ask for any at\r\npresent in Johnnie\xe2\x80\x94not for a whole week, as Annie would declare; he does\r\nnot know his single Latin declension; his spelling, is all abroad; his\r\ngeography wild; yet though turned back once, he misses the fine by just\r\nsaying his lessons passably the last time.  They perhaps ought, in strict\r\njustice, to have been sent back; but Miss Fosbrook was very glad to be\r\nsaved the uproar that would have ensued, and almost wondered whether she\r\nwere not timidly merciful to the horrible copy and the greasy slate.  But\r\nJohnnie had no fine, and was as proud of it as if he had been a good boy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe hadn\xe2\x80\x99t caught him out,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as if his kind governess had been\r\nhis enemy.\r\n\r\nAs to Annie, her French verbs were always dreadful things to hear, and\r\nthe little merry face, usually so bright, used to grow quite deplorable\r\nwith the trouble she took not to use her mind.  Using her memory was bad\r\nenough, but saying things by heart was an affliction she was used to, and\r\nit was very shocking of Miss Fosbrook to require her to find out _how_\r\nmany years Richard II. had reigned, if he began in 1377 and ended in\r\n1399.  Susan prompted her, however; so she really got a triumph over Miss\r\nFosbrook, and was quite saved from thinking.  Oh, but the teasing woman!\r\nshe silenced Susan, and would have this poor injured Annie tell how old\r\nthe tiresome man was.  \xe2\x80\x9cBegan to reign at eleven years old, dethroned\r\nafter twenty-two years; how old was he?\xe2\x80\x9d  Annie found bursting out crying\r\neasier than thinking, and then they all cried out, \xe2\x80\x9cO Nanny, the pig!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand Miss Fosbrook had the barbarity to call that _foolish_ crying!  What\r\nmight one cry for, if not at being asked how old Richard II. was?  If the\r\nfine must be paid, there was no use in stopping; so Annie howled till\r\nMiss Fosbrook turned her out to finish on the stairs; and as Nurse\r\nFreeman was out with the little ones, there was no one to comfort her; so\r\nshe cried till she was tired, and when the noise ceased, Susan was\r\nallowed to come and coax her, and fetch her back to go on with her copy,\r\nas soon as her hand was steady enough.  She felt very foolish by this\r\ntime, and thought David eyed her rather angrily and contemptuously; so\r\nshe crept quietly to her corner, and felt sad and low-spirited all the\r\nrest of the morning.  Now that thirty-three had come into her head, it\r\nseemed so stupid not to have thought of it in time; and then she would\r\nhave saved her farthing, and her eyes would not have been so hot.\r\n\r\nMaybe, too, Susan\xe2\x80\x99s French phrases would not have been turned back.  Miss\r\nFosbrook would have given a great deal not to have been obliged to do it,\r\nbut she had prompted flagrantly already, and a teacher is obliged to have\r\na conscience quite as much as a scholar; so the book was given back, and\r\nSusan spent twelve minutes in see-sawing herself, and going over the\r\nsentences in a rapid whispering gabble, a serious worry to the governess\r\nin listening to Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s practising and David\xe2\x80\x99s reading, but she thought\r\nit would be a hardship to be forbidden to learn in her own way at that\r\nmoment, and forbore.  David was interrupted in his \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nHistory,\xe2\x80\x9d and looked rather cross about it, for Susan to try again.  She\r\nmade all the same blunders\xe2\x80\x94and more too!  Back again!  Poor Susie!  Once,\r\ntwice, thrice, has she read those stupid words over, and knows less of\r\nthem than before.  Davy\xe2\x80\x99s loud voice will go into her understanding\r\ninstead of those French phrases.  She looks up in dull stupefaction.\r\n\r\nWilliam Rufus is disposed of, and David, as grave as a judge, is taking\r\nup his slate, looking a little fussed because there is a scratch in the\r\ncorner.  \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Susan,\xe2\x80\x9d says Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nSusan jumps up in desperation, and puts her hands behind her.  Oh dear!\r\noh dear! all that the gentlemen on a journey were saying to one another\r\nhas gone clean out of her head!\r\n\r\nShe cannot recollect the three first words.  She only remembers that this\r\nis the third time, and another farthing is gone!  She stands and stares.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusan,\xe2\x80\x9d says Miss Fosbrook severely, \xe2\x80\x9cyou never tried to learn this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan gives a little gasp; and Elizabeth, who has said her French without\r\na blunder, puts in an unnecessary and not very sisterly word: \xe2\x80\x9cSusan\r\nnever will learn her French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan\xe2\x80\x99s honest eyes fill with tears, but she gulps them back.  She will\r\nnot cry away another farthing, but she does feel it very cross in Bessie,\r\nand she is universally miserable.\r\n\r\nChristabel feels heated, wearied, and provoked, and as if she were fast\r\nlosing her own temper; and that made her resolve on mercy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusie,\xe2\x80\x9d she said with an effort, \xe2\x80\x9crun twice to the great lime-tree and\r\nback.  Then take the book into my room, read this over three times, and\r\nwe will try again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan looked surprised, but she obeyed, came back, and repeated the\r\nphrases better than she had ever said French before.  She was absolutely\r\nsurprised and highly pleased, and she finished off her other lessons\r\nswimmingly; but oh, she was glad to be rid of them!  Yes, they were off\r\nher mind, and so she deserved that they should be!  She flew away to the\r\nnursery, and little Sarah was soon crowing in her arms.\r\n\r\nElizabeth?  Not a blunder in French verbs or geography\xe2\x80\x94very tidy copy.\r\nFrench reading good; English equally so, only it ended in a pout, because\r\nthere was not time for her to go on to see what became of Carthage; and\r\nshe was a most intolerable time in learning her poetry out of the book of\r\nReadings, or rather she much preferred reading the verses in other parts\r\nof the book to getting perfect in her lesson, and then being obliged to\r\nturn her mind to arithmetic.  Miss Fosbrook called her three times; and\r\nat last she turned round peevishly at being interrupted in the middle of\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cFriar of Orders Gray,\xe2\x80\x9d and repeated her twenty lines of Cowper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWinter\xe2\x80\x99s Walk\xe2\x80\x9d in a doleful whine, though without a blunder.\r\n\r\nIt was one of the horrible novelties that Miss Fosbrook was bringing in,\r\nthat she expected people to understand their sums as well as work them.\r\nShe gave much shorter ones, to be sure, than Mamma, who did sometimes set\r\na long multiplication sum of such a huge size, that it looked as if it\r\nwere meant to keep the victim out of the way; but who would not prefer\r\ncasting up any length of figures, to being required to explain the\r\nmeaning of \xe2\x80\x9ccarrying\xe2\x80\x9d?\r\n\r\nReally, if it had not been for the pig, that shocking question might have\r\nled to a mutiny in the school-room.  When it was bad enough to do the\r\nthing, how could anyone ask what was meant by the operation, and why it\r\nwas performed?\r\n\r\nWhat did Bessie do when her sum was being overlooked?  Miss Fosbrook read\r\non: \xe2\x80\x9c4 from 8, 4; 7 from 1\xe2\x80\x94how\xe2\x80\x99s this, Bessie?  7 from 10 are\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c3, and 1 are 4,\xe2\x80\x9d dolorously, as her 3 was changed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, what next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarry one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I tell you was meant by carry one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tens,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, not in the least thinking \xe2\x80\x9cthe tens\xe2\x80\x9d had\r\nanything to do with the matter, but only that she had heard something\r\nabout them, and could thus get rid of the subject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bessie, what tens can you possibly mean?  Think a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure you said tens once,\xe2\x80\x9d said injured innocence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was in an addition sum.  See, here it is quite different.  I told\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie put on a vacant stare.  She was not going to attend to what she\r\ndid not like.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook saw the face.  She absolutely shrank from provoking another\r\nfit of crying, and went quickly through the explanation.  She saw that\r\nher words might as well have been spoken to the slate.  Bessie neither\r\nlistened nor took them in.  Not all her love for her dear Christabel\r\nAngela could stir her up to make one effort contrary to her inclinations.\r\nThe slate was given back to her, she wiped out the sum in a pet, and ran\r\naway.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook turned round, David, whose lessons had been perfectly\r\nrepeated an hour ago, was sitting cross-legged in the window, with his\r\nslate and pencil, and a basket of bricks, his great delight, which he was\r\nplacing in rows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cisn\xe2\x80\x99t this it?  Twelve bricks; take away those\r\nseven, then\xe2\x80\x941, 2, 3, 4, 5\xe2\x80\x94the twelve is only 5: the 10 is gone, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\r\nso you must leave one out of the next figure in the upper line of the\r\nsum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Davy had only begun arithmetic on the governess\xe2\x80\x99s arrival, but he had\r\nlearnt numeration and addition in her way.  She was so delighted, that\r\nshe stooped down and kissed him, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cQuite right, my little man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavy rather disapproved of the kiss, and rubbed his brown-holland elbow\r\nover his face, as if to clear it off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Christabel, as she hurried away for five minutes\xe2\x80\x99 peace\r\nin her own room before the dinner-bell, \xe2\x80\x9cit is a comfort to have one\r\npupil whose whole endeavour is not to frustrate one\xe2\x80\x99s attempts to educate\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor young thing! that one little bit of sense had quite cheered her up.\r\nOtherwise she was not one whit less weary than the children.  She had\r\nbeen learning a very tough lesson too\xe2\x80\x94much harder than any of theirs; and\r\nshe was not at all certain that she had learnt it right.\r\n\r\nNow, readers, of all the children, who do you think had used the most\r\nconscience at the lessons?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\n\r\n\r\nWHAT an entirely different set of beings were those Stokesley children in\r\nlesson-time and out of it!  Talk of the change of an old thorn in winter\r\nto a May-bush in spring! that was nothing to it!\r\n\r\nPoor, listless, stolid, deplorable logs, with bowed backs and crossed\r\nankles, pipy voices and heavy eyes!  Who would believe that these were\r\nthe merry, capering, noisy creatures, full of fun and riot, clattering\r\nand screeching, and dancing about with ecstasy at Sam\xe2\x80\x99s information that\r\nthere was a bonfire by the potato-house!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bonfire!\xe2\x80\x9d said the London governess, thinking of illuminations; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\r\ncan that be for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is not _for_ anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cit is Purday burning weeds.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you smell them?  How nice they are!  I was afraid it was only\r\nFarmer Smith burning couch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the noses were elevated to scent from afar a certain smoky odour,\r\nusually to be detected in July breezes, and which reminded Miss Fosbrook\r\nof a brick-field.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPotatoes!  Potatoes!  We\xe2\x80\x99ll roast some potatoes, and have them for tea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbellowed all the voices; so that Miss Fosbrook could hardly find a space\r\nfor very unwillingly saying,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my dears, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I ought to let you play with fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we always do,\xe2\x80\x9d roared the children; and Susan added,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe always roast potatoes when there\xe2\x80\x99s a bonfire.  Mamma always lets us;\r\nit is only Purday that is cross.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes; Mamma lets us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if Sam and Susan say it is right, I trust to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook gladly; \xe2\x80\x9conly you must let me come out and see what it is.  I am\r\ntoo much of a Londoner to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes; and we\xe2\x80\x99ll roast you some potatoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the uproarious population tumbled upstairs, there to be invested with\r\nrougher brown-holland garments than those that already concealed the\r\nsprigged cottons of the girls; and when the five came down again, they\r\nwere so much alike in dress, that it was not easy to tell girls from\r\nboys.  Susan brought little George down with her, and off the party set.\r\nSam and Hal, who had been waiting in the hall, took Miss Fosbrook between\r\nthem, as if they thought it their duty to do the honours of the bonfire,\r\nand conducted her across the garden, through the kitchen-garden, across\r\nwhich lay a long sluggish bar of heavy and very odorous smoke, to a gate\r\nin a quickset hedge.  Here were some sheds and cart-houses, a fagot pile,\r\nvarious logs of timber, a grindstone, and\xe2\x80\x94that towards which all the\r\neight children rushed with whoops of ecstasy\xe2\x80\x94a heap of smoking rubbish,\r\nchiefly dry leaves, and peas and potato haulm, with a large allowance of\r\ncabbage stumps\xe2\x80\x94all extremely earthy, and looking as if the smouldering\r\nsmoke were a wonder from so mere a heap of dirt.\r\n\r\nNo matter!  There were all the children round it, some on their knees,\r\nsome jumping; and voices were crying on all sides,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO jolly, jolly!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get some potatoes!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you must have some\r\nsticks first, and make some ashes.\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no flame\xe2\x80\x94not a bit!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cGet\r\nout of the way, can\xe2\x80\x99t you?  I\xe2\x80\x99ll make a hot place.\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll each have our\r\nown oven, and roast our own potatoes!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, Sam; you\xe2\x80\x99re pushing me\r\ninto the smoke!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis of course was from Elizabeth; and there followed, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, Bessie,\r\nyou will tread upon Georgie.\xe2\x80\x94Yes, Georgie, you _shall_ have a place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSticks, sticks!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Henry; while Sam was on his knees, poking out a\r\nspecies of cavern in the fire, where some symptoms of red embers\r\nappeared, which he diligently puffed with his mouth, feeding it with\r\nleaves and smaller chips in a very well practised way.  \xe2\x80\x9cSticks, Annie!\r\nJohnnie!  Davy! get sticks, I say, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll make an oven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnnie obeyed; but the two little boys were intent on imitating Sam on\r\nanother side of the fire, and Johnnie uttered a gruff \xe2\x80\x9cGet \xe2\x80\x99em yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwhile David took no notice at all.\r\n\r\nPerhaps Hal would have betaken himself to no gentle means if Susan had\r\nnot hastily put in his way a plentiful supply of dead wood, which she had\r\nbeen letting little George think he picked up all himself; and there was\r\nkeen excitement, which Christabel could not help sharing, while under\r\nSam\xe2\x80\x99s breath the red edges of the half-burnt chip glowed, flushed,\r\nwidened, then went sparkling doubtfully, slowly, to the light bit of\r\npotato-stalk that he held to it, glowing as he blew\xe2\x80\x94fading, smoking, when\r\nhe took breath.  Try again\xe2\x80\x94puff, puff, puff diligently; the fire\r\nevidently has a taste for the delicate little shaving that Annie has\r\nfound for it; it seizes on it; another\xe2\x80\x94another; a flame at last.  Hurrah!\r\npile on more; not too much.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put it out!\xe2\x80\x9d  Oh, there! strong\r\nflame\xe2\x80\x94coming crackling up through those smothering heaps of stick and\r\nhaulm; it won\xe2\x80\x99t be kept down; it rises in the wind; it is a red flaring\r\nbanner.  The children shriek in transports of admiration, little George\r\nloudest of all, because Susan is holding him tight, lest he should run\r\ninto the brilliant flame.  Miss Fosbrook is rather appalled, but the\r\nchildren are all safe on the windward side, and seem used to it; so she\r\nsupposes it is all right, and the flame dies down faster than it rose.\r\nIt is again an innocent smouldering heap, like a volcano after an\r\neruption.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must not let it blaze again just yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9ckeep it down well\r\nwith sticks, to make some nice white ashes for the potatoes.  See, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nmake an oven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were all stooping round this precious hot corner, some kneeling,\r\nsome sitting on the ground, David with hands on his sturdy knees\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nintent on nursing that creeping red spark, as it smouldered from chip to\r\nchip, leaving a black trace wherever it went, when through the thick\r\nsmoke, that was like an absolute curtain hiding everything on the farther\r\nside, came headlong a huge bundle of weeds launched overwhelmingly on the\r\nfire, and falling on the children\xe2\x80\x99s heads in an absolute shower, knocking\r\nJohnnie down, but on a soft and innocent side of the fire among the\r\ncabbage-stumps, and seeming likely to bury Sam, who leant over to shelter\r\nhis precious oven, and puffed away as if nothing was happening, amid the\r\nvarious shouts around him, in which \xe2\x80\x9cPurday\xe2\x80\x9d was the most audible word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, so you\xe2\x80\x99ve got at he, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purday, leaning on the fork\r\nwith which he had thrown on the weeds.  \xe2\x80\x9cNothing is safe from you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, you thought you had a new place, Purday, and circumvented us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried Hal; \xe2\x80\x9cbut we smelt you out, you old rogue; we weren\xe2\x80\x99t going to be\r\nbaulked of our bonfire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook here ventured on asking if they were doing mischief; and\r\nPurday answered with an odd gruff noise, \xe2\x80\x9cMischief enough\xe2\x80\x94ay, to be\r\nsure\xe2\x80\x94hucking the fire all abroad.  It\xe2\x80\x99s what they\xe2\x80\x99re always after.  I did\r\nthink I\xe2\x80\x99d got it safe out of their way this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d in rather a frightened voice, for she felt that it would be a\r\ntremendous trial of her powers, \xe2\x80\x9cshould I make them come away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch her!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Hal.\r\n\r\nThere was horror and disapprobation on Susan\xe2\x80\x99s face.  Annie stood with\r\nher mouth open; while John, throwing himself on the ground with fury,\r\nrolled over, crying out something about, \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cvery cross;\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nDavid lay flat on his face, puffing at his own particular oven, like a\r\nlittle Wind in an old picture.  Sam waited, leaning on the ashen stick\r\nthat served him as a poker.  It was the most audacious thing he had ever\r\nheard.  Rob them of their bonfire!  Would that old traitor of a Purday\r\nabet her?\r\n\r\nPerhaps Purday was as much astonished as the rest; but, after all, much\r\nas the children tormented his bonfires, overset his haycocks, and\r\ndisturbed his wood-pile, he did not like anyone to scold them but\r\nhimself, much less the new London Lady; so he made up an odd sort of\r\ngrin, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Ma\xe2\x80\x99am, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t that they do so much harm; let\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em bide;\xe2\x80\x9d and he proceeded to shake on the rest of his barrowful,\r\ntumbling the weeds down over David\xe2\x80\x99s cherished oven in utter disregard;\r\nbut the children cried with one voice, \xe2\x80\x9cHurrah! hurrah!  Purday, we don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo any harm, so don\xe2\x80\x99t ever grumble again.  Hurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for _her_, the crosspatch,\xe2\x80\x9d said Johnnie to Annie,\r\nnever hearing or heeding Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s fervent \xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd as long as the foolish boy remembered it, he always did believe that\r\nMiss Fosbrook was so cross as to want to hinder them from their bonfire,\r\nonly Purday would not let her.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook did not trouble herself to be understood; she was relieved\r\nto have done her duty, and be free to rejoice in and share the pleasure.\r\nShe ran about and collected materials for Sam till she was out of breath,\r\nand joined in all the excitement as the fire showed symptoms of reviving,\r\nafter being apparently crushed out by Purday.  Sam and Susan, at least,\r\nbelieved that she had only spoken to Purday because she thought it right;\r\nbut even for them to forgive interference with their bonfire privileges\r\nwas a great stretch.\r\n\r\nAt last she thought it time to leave them to their own devices, and seize\r\nthe moment for some quiet reading; but she had not reached the house\r\nbefore little steps came after her, and she saw Elizabeth running fast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are so tiresome,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cSam won\xe2\x80\x99t let me stand anywhere but\r\nwhere the smoke gets into my eyes, and George plagues so!  May I come in\r\nwith you, dear Christabel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very welcome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am sorry to hear so\r\nmany complaints.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are so cross to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie; \xe2\x80\x9cthey always are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must try to be cheerful and good-humoured with them, and they will\r\nleave off vexing you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut may I come in?  It will be a nice time for my secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristabel saw little hope for her intended reading, but she was always\r\nglad of a space for making Bessie happy, so she kindly consented to the\r\nbringing out of the little girl\xe2\x80\x99s treasury, and the dismal face grew\r\nhappy and eager.  The subjects of the drawings were all clear in her\r\nhead; that was not the difficulty, but the cardboard, the ribbon, the\r\nreal good paints.  One little slip of card Miss Fosbrook hunted out of\r\nher portfolio; she cut a pencil of her own, and advised the first attempt\r\nto be made upon a piece of paper.  The little bird that Bessie produced\r\nwas really not at all bad, and her performance was quite fair enough to\r\nmake it worth while to go on, since Miss Fosbrook well knew that mammas\r\nare pleased with works of their children, showing more good-will than\r\nskill.  For why?  Their value is in the love and thought they show.\r\n\r\nThe little bird was made into a robin with the colours in a paint-box\r\nthat Bessie had long ago bought; but they were so weak and muddy, that\r\nthe result was far from good enough for a present, and it was agreed that\r\nreal paints must be procured as well as ribbon.  Miss Fosbrook offered to\r\ncommission her sisters to buy the Prussian blue, lake, and gamboge in\r\nLondon, and send them in a letter.  This was a new idea to Bessie, and\r\nshe was only not quite decided between the certainty that London paints\r\nmust be better than country ones, and the desire of the walk to Bonchamp\r\nto buy some; but the thought that the ribbon, after all, might be\r\nprocured there, satisfied her.  The little doleful maid was changed into\r\nan eager, happy, chattering child, full of intelligence and contrivance,\r\nand showing many pretty fancies, since there was no one to tease her and\r\nlaugh at her; and her governess listened kindly and helpfully.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook could not help thinking how much happier her little\r\ncompanion would have been as an only child, or with one sister, and\r\nparents who would have made the most of her love of taste and refinement,\r\ninstead of the hearty busy parents, and the rude brothers and sisters,\r\nwho held her cheap for being unlike themselves.  But then she bethought\r\nher, that perhaps Bessie might have grown up vain and affected, had all\r\nthese tastes been petted and fostered, and that perhaps her little\r\nhardships might make her the stronger, steadier, more useful woman,\r\ninstead of living in fancies.  It was the unkindness on one side, and the\r\ntemper on the other, that made Miss Fosbrook uneasy.\r\n\r\nThe work had gone on happily for nearly an hour, and Bessie was copying a\r\nforget-me-not off a little painted card-board pincushion of her own, when\r\nsteps were heard, little trotting steps, and Susan came in with little\r\nGeorge.  He had been pushed down by Johnnie, and was rather in a fretful\r\nmood; and Susan had left all her happy play to bring him in to rest and\r\ncomfort him, coming to the school-room because Nurse Freeman was out.\r\nBefore Elizabeth had time to hide away her doings, George had seen the\r\nbright pincushion, and was holding out his hands for it.  Bessie hastily\r\npocketed it.  George burst out crying; and Susan, without more ado, threw\r\nherself on her sister, and, pinioning Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s slight arm by the greater\r\nstrength of her firm one, was diving into her pocket in spite of her\r\nstruggles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusan, leave off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9clet your sister alone.  She has\r\na right to do what she likes with her own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is so cross in her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, obeying however, but only to snatch\r\nup little George, and hug and kiss him.  \xe2\x80\x9cPoor dear little man! is Betty\r\ncross to him?  There! there! come with Sue, and _she\xe2\x80\x99ll_ get him\r\nsomething pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusie, Susie, indeed it\xe2\x80\x99s only that I don\xe2\x80\x99t want him to spoil it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nElizabeth, distressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA foolish thing like that!  Why, the only use of it is to please the\r\nchildren; but you are just such a baby as he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, still\r\npitying George.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better put your things away, Bessie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook,\r\ninterfering to stop the dispute; and as soon as Elizabeth was gone, and\r\nGeorge a little pacified by an ivory ribbon-measure out of Miss\r\nFosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s work-box, she observed to Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, you must not let\r\nyour love for the little ones make you unjust and unkind to Bessie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe always is so unkind to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan resentfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think she feels unkindly; but if you tyrannize over her, and\r\nforce her to give way to them, you cannot expect her to like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma says the elder must give way to the younger,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not try whether she would give way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, because I knew she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t; and I could not have my little Georgie\r\nvexed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I could not see my little Susie violent and unjust,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook cheerfully.  \xe2\x80\x9cJustice first, Susan; you had no right to rob\r\nBessie for George, any more than I should have to give away a dinner of\r\nyour papa\xe2\x80\x99s because he had refused a beggar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa never would,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, rather going off from the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely; but do you understand me, Susan?  I will not have Bessie\r\n_forced_ out of her rights for the little ones.  Not Bessie only, but\r\nnobody is to be tyrannized over; it is not right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBessie is so nonsensical,\xe2\x80\x9d was all Susan said, looking glum.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely she may seem so to you; but if you knew more, you would see\r\nthat all is not nonsense that seems so to you.  Some people would admire\r\nher ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Greville told Mrs. Brownlow that Bessie\r\nwas the only one among us that was capable of civilisation; but Mrs.\r\nGreville is a fine lady, and we always laugh at her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d as Bessie returned, \xe2\x80\x9cyou want to go out to your play again, my\r\ndear.  Will you leave Georgie with us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan was a little doubtful about trusting her darling with anyone,\r\nespecially one who could take Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s part against him; but she wished\r\nexceedingly to be present at the interesting moment of seeing whether the\r\npotatoes were done enough, and George was perfectly contented with\r\nmeasuring everything on the ribbon, so she ran quickly off, without the\r\nmanners to thank Miss Fosbrook, but to assure the rest of the party that\r\nthe governess really was very good-natured, and that she would save her\r\nbiggest and best potato for Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s tea.\r\n\r\nChristabel managed very happily with little George, though not quite\r\nwithout offending Elizabeth, who thought it very hard to be desired to\r\nput away her painting instead of tantalizing her little brother with the\r\nsight of what he must not have.  Miss Fosbrook could not draw her into\r\nthe merry game with little George, which made his shouts of glee ring out\r\nthrough the house, and meet Nurse Freeman\xe2\x80\x99s ear as she came in-doors with\r\nthe baby, and calling at the school-room door, summoned him off to his\r\ntea, as if she were in a pet with Miss Fosbrook for daring to meddle with\r\none of _her_ own nursery children.\r\n\r\nNothing more was heard of the others, and Christabel and Elizabeth both\r\nread in peace till the tea-bell rang, and they went down and waited and\r\nwaited, till Miss Fosbrook accepted Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s offer of going out to call\r\nthe rest.  But Bessie returned no more than the rest; and the governess\r\nset forth herself, but had not made many steps before the voices of the\r\nrabble rout were heard, and they all were dancing and clattering about\r\nher, while Susan and Hal each carried aloft a plate containing articles\r\nonce brown, now black, and thickly powdered with white ashes, as were the\r\nchildren themselves up to their very hair.\r\n\r\nAs a slight concession to grown-up people\xe2\x80\x99s prejudices, they did, at the\r\nrisk of their dear potatoes getting cold, scamper up to perform a species\r\nof toilette, and then sat down round the tea-table, Susie, David, and Sam\r\neach vociferous that Miss Fosbrook should eat \xe2\x80\x9cmy potato that I did on\r\npurpose for her.\xe2\x80\x9d  Poor Miss Fosbrook! she would nearly as soon have\r\neaten the bonfire itself as those cinder-coated things, tough as leather\r\noutside, and within like solid smoke.  Indeed the children, who had been\r\nbathing in smoke all day, had brought in the air of it with them; but\r\ntheir tongues ran fast on their adventures, and their taste had no doubt\r\nthat their own bonfire potatoes were the most perfect cookery in art!\r\nMiss Fosbrook picked out the most eatable bits of each of the three, and\r\nmanaged to satisfy the three cooks, all zealous for their own.  Other\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s potatoes might be smoky, but each one\xe2\x80\x99s own was delicious\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cquite\r\nworthy of the pig when he was bought,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Miss Fosbrook; but she\r\nmade her real pleasure at the kind feeling to cover her dislike of the\r\nblack potatoes, and thus pleased the children without being untrue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLine upon line, precept upon precept; here a little, and there a\r\nlittle.\xe2\x80\x9d  That is the way habits are formed and characters made; not all\r\nat once.  So there had been an opportunity for Susan to grow confirmed in\r\nher kindness and unselfishness, as well as to learn that tyranny is\r\nwrong, even on behalf of the weak; and Bessie, if she would take home the\r\nlesson, had received one in readiness to be cheerful, and to turn from\r\nher own pursuits to oblige others.  Something had been attempted toward\r\nbreaking her habit of being fretful, and thinking herself injured.  It\r\nremained to be seen whether the many little things that were yet to\r\nhappen to the two girls would be so used as to strengthen their good\r\nhabits or their bad ones.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\n\r\n\r\nIT is not worth while to go on describing every day at Stokesley, since\r\nlessons were far too much alike; and play-times, though varied enough for\r\nthe house of Merrifield, might be less entertaining to the readers.\r\n\r\nEnough to say, that by Saturday afternoon John had not only forfeited his\r\nlast farthing, but was charged with another into next week, for the poor\r\npleasure of leaving his hat on the school-room floor because Elizabeth\r\nhad told him of it.  At about four o\xe2\x80\x99clock it set in for rain, catching\r\nthe party at some distance from home, so that, though they made good\r\nspeed, the dust turned into mud, and clung fast to their shoes.\r\n\r\nDavid, never the best runner, was only in time to catch Johnnie by the\r\nskirt upon the third step of the staircase, crying out, \xe2\x80\x9cThe pig!\xe2\x80\x9d but\r\nJohnnie, tired of the subject, and in a provoking mood, twitched away his\r\npinafore, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cBother the pig!\xe2\x80\x9d and rushed up after the four who had\r\npreceded him, leaving such lumps of dirt on the edge of every step, that\r\nwhen Miss Fosbrook came after with Elizabeth she could not but declare\r\nthat a shower was a costly article.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen it\xe2\x80\x99s such fine weather it puts one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfeet out of one\xe2\x80\x99s head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile Sam, Henry, and Bessie were laughing at Susan for this speech,\r\nlittle George trotted in, crying out, \xe2\x80\x9cHalty man come, Halty man come;\r\nGeorgie want sweetie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Gibraltar man!\xe2\x80\x9d cried John and Annie with one voice, and they were\r\nat the bottom of the stairs with a bound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, send him away, send him away.  They\xe2\x80\x99ll spend all their money, and\r\nthere will be none left!\xe2\x80\x9d was David\xe2\x80\x99s cry; while George kept dragging his\r\neldest sister\xe2\x80\x99s frock, with entreaties of \xe2\x80\x9cSusie, Susie, come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey call him the Gibraltar man, because he sells Gibraltar rock, and\r\ngingerbread, and all those things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry in explanation.  \xe2\x80\x9cWe have\r\nalways dealt with him; and he is very deserving; and his wife makes it\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94at least I know she makes ginger-beer\xe2\x80\x94so we must encourage him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Henry hastened downstairs to encourage the Gibraltar man; and Susan,\r\nsaying soothingly, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, Georgie;\xe2\x80\x94never mind Davie, we\xe2\x80\x99ll make up\r\nfor it; I can\xe2\x80\x99t vex him,\xe2\x80\x9d had taken the little fellow in her arms and\r\nfollowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPigs enough here, without sending to the fair,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Sam, please, Miss Fosbrook, send the Gibraltar man away, and\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t let him come,\xe2\x80\x9d cried David quite passionately.  \xe2\x80\x9cNasty man! he will\r\ncome every Saturday, and they\xe2\x80\x99ll always spend all their money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook good-humouredly, \xe2\x80\x9csuppose we have no\r\nright to banish the Gibraltar man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t wan\xe2\x80\x99t him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie; \xe2\x80\x9cit makes my fingers sticky.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re no good,\xe2\x80\x9d said David vehemently.  \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like you, and I hate\r\nthe Gibraltar man, taking away all our money from poor Hannah.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGently, gently, Davie; nobody makes you spend your money; and perhaps\r\nthe poor man has children of his own who want food as much as Hannah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen can\xe2\x80\x99t they eat the Gibraltar rock and bulls\xe2\x80\x99 eyes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam suggested that this diet would make them sick; to which poor little\r\nearnest David answered, that when once the pig was bought, he would give\r\nall his money for a whole month to the Gibraltar man, if he would not\r\ncome for the next four weeks.\r\n\r\nAnd Christabel thought of what she had once read, that people would often\r\ngladly put away from their children friends the very trials that are sent\r\nby Heaven to prove and strengthen their will and power of resisting\r\nself-indulgence.  Before she had quite thought it out, the quick steps\r\nwere back again, and Sam greeted the entrance of John thus: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, if\r\nthat isn\xe2\x80\x99t a shame!  Have you been and done Sukey out of all that, Jack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was only three bulls\xe2\x80\x99 eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, following.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou know he had\r\nnothing of his own, and it was so hard, and Annie gave him some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Nurse some,\xe2\x80\x9d added Hal.  \xe2\x80\x9cTrust Jackie for taking care of himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nWell he might say so, considering how full were John\xe2\x80\x99s mouth, hands, and\r\npockets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Davie has had nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d said kind Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cHere, Davie!\xe2\x80\x9d holding out\r\nto him an amber-like piece of barley-sugar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want your stuff,\xe2\x80\x9d said David roughly.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve spent all away\r\nfrom the pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Davie, indeed, only twopence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cpray have a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might at least say thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\nBut how difficult is that middle road which is the only right one!\r\nDavid, being too much set on one single purpose, good though it was,\r\ncould see nothing else.  It was right and generous to abstain from sweets\r\nwith this end in view; but it was wrong to be rude and unthankful to the\r\nsister who meant all so kindly, and was the most unselfish of all.  She\r\nturned round to Elizabeth with the kind offer of the dainty she had not\r\neven tasted herself, but was not more graciously treated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you, Susie? it is all pulled about with your fingers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a matter on which the Misses and Masters Merrifield were not\r\nwont to be particular; and with one of the teasing laughs that Bessie\r\nhated, Sam exclaimed as Susan turned to him, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, thank you, Sukey, _I_\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind finger sauce,\xe2\x80\x9d but not before John was stretching out a hand\r\nglazed with sugar, and calling out, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, give it to me!\xe2\x80\x9d and as it\r\ndisappeared in his brother\xe2\x80\x99s mouth, he burst out angrily, \xe2\x80\x9cHow cross,\r\nSam!  You did that on purpose!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cI did; for though pigs on four legs are all very well,\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t like pigs on two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, Jackie, never mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, seeing him about to begin to cry,\r\nand offering him her last sugar-plum.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want sugar-plums, I want barley-sugar,\xe2\x80\x9d said John devouring it\r\nnevertheless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t one bit more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan regretfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you had any yourself, Susan?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh then, here Susie, I always keep a reserve,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, not\r\nyou, Jack; I don\xe2\x80\x99t feed little pigs, whatever Susie does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd in spite of Susan, both the elder brothers set on John, teasing him\r\nabout his greediness, till he burst out crying, and ran away to the\r\nnursery.  Miss Fosbrook hated the teasing, but she thought it served John\r\nso rightly, that she would not save him from it; and she only interfered\r\nto remind the others that their fingers would bring them in for fines\r\nunless they were washed before tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how much have you spent?\xe2\x80\x9d reproachfully asked that rigid young\r\njudge, David; but all the answer he got was a pull by the hair from Hal,\r\nand \xe2\x80\x9cHollo, young one! am I to give my accounts to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid gravely put up his hand and smoothed his ruffled locks, repeating,\r\nin his manful way, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to know what you have left for the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhereupon Hal laid hold of him, pulled him off the locker, and rolled him\r\nabout on the floor like a puppy dog, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what, if you\r\nmake such a work about it, I\xe2\x80\x99ll spend all my allowance, and not subscribe\r\nat all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the tormented David, and \xe2\x80\x9cSam!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the governess, really\r\nafraid the little boy would be hurt; but Sam only stood laughing with his\r\nback to the shutter, and Christabel herself hurried to the rescue, to\r\npick Henry off his victim, holding an arm tight, while the child got up,\r\nand ran away to get his hair re-brushed for tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Hal, you might have hurt him,\xe2\x80\x9d argued the governess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good thing for him too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the brothers with one voice.\r\n\r\nShe was very much shocked; but when she thought it over she perceived\r\nthat though Hal might be to blame, yet in the long run even this rough\r\ndiscipline might be more useful to her dear little David than being\r\nallowed to take upon him with his elder brothers, and grow conceited and\r\ninterfering.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was not surprised when, next morning, a frightful bellowing\r\nwas heard instead of Johnnie being seen, and she learnt that Master John\r\nwas in the hands of Nurse Freeman, who was administering to him a dose in\r\nconsequence of his having been greatly indisposed all night.  It must be\r\nconfessed that Christabel was not very sorry to hear it, nor that Nurse\r\nwould keep him to herself all day; for bad company as Johnnie had been on\r\nthe week-days, he had been worse on the Sunday.\r\n\r\nAnd when John came out on Monday, he looked like a different boy; he had\r\nlost his fractious, rebellious look; he spoke as civilly as could be\r\nexpected of a small Merrifield, and showed no signs of being set against\r\nhis lessons.  To be sure it was a bad way of spending a Sunday, to be\r\nlaid up with ailments brought on by over-eating; but even this was better\r\nthan spending it, like the former one, in wilful misbehaviour; and John,\r\nwho knew that Papa, Mamma, brothers, and sisters all alike detested and\r\ndespised real greediness, had been heartily ashamed of himself, both for\r\nthis and his forfeits.  A new week was a new starting-point, and he meant\r\nto spend this one well.  For indeed it is one of the blessings of our\r\nlives that we have so many stages\xe2\x80\x94days, weeks, years, and the like\xe2\x80\x94from\r\neach of which we may make fresh starts, feel old things left behind, and\r\ngo on to lead a new life.\r\n\r\nBesides, Johnnie was quite well now; and perhaps no child, so well\r\nbrought up, could have been so constantly naughty the whole week without\r\nsome degree of ailment, suspected neither by himself nor others.  For\r\nthis is one of our real troubles, when either young or old, that\r\nsometimes there is a feeling of discomfort and vexation about us that,\r\nwithout knowing why, makes everything go amiss, causes everybody else to\r\nappear cross, and all tasks, all orders, all misadventures, to become\r\ngreat grievances.  Grown-up people feel this as well as children; but\r\nthey have gone through it often enough to know what is the matter, and\r\nthey have, or ought to have, more self-command.  But children have yet to\r\nlearn by experience that the outer things are not harder and more\r\nuntoward, so much as that they themselves are out of sorts.  This is poor\r\ncomfort; and certainly it is dangerous to say to ourselves that being\r\npoorly is any excuse for letting ourselves be cross, or for not doing our\r\nbest.  If Mrs. Merrifield had thought so, what miserable lives her\r\nhusband and children would have led!  No, the way to use the certain fact\r\nthat the state of our bodies affects our tempers and spirits, is to say\r\nto ourselves, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, if this person or this thing do seem disagreeable,\r\nor if this work, or even this little bit of obedience, be very tiresome,\r\nperhaps it may really be only a fancy of mine, and if I go to it with a\r\ngood will, I may work off the notion;\xe2\x80\x9d or, \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I am cross to-day,\r\nlet me take good care how I answer.\xe2\x80\x9d  And a little prayer in our hearts\r\nwill be the best help of all.  Self-command and goodness will not come by\r\nnature as we grow up, but we must work for them in childhood.\r\n\r\nWhen the Monday allowances were brought out, and the pig\xe2\x80\x99s chance\r\ninquired into, David alone produced his whole sum, untouched by\r\nforfeiture or waste, and dropped it into \xe2\x80\x9cToby Fillpot.\xe2\x80\x9d  Elizabeth had\r\nher entire sixpence; but a penny had been spent on a letter to Mamma, and\r\nshe gave but one to the fund, in spite of the black looks she met from\r\nDavid.  Sam had lost a farthing by the shower, and had likewise bought a\r\nqueen\xe2\x80\x99s head, to write to his father.  The rest, fourpence-three\r\nfarthings, he paid over.  Poor Johnnie! his last week\xe2\x80\x99s naughtiness had\r\nexceeded his power of paying fines, and a halfpenny was subtracted from\r\nthis week\xe2\x80\x99s threepence; while the Gibraltar man had consumed all that\r\nfines had spared to little Annie, had left Susan only threepence, and\r\nHenry but twopence-halfpenny.  This, with twopence that Miss Fosbrook had\r\nfound in her travelling-bag, made one shilling and fourpence-farthing\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nvery poor collection for one week.  David was quite melancholy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry; \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s brother, the Colonel, is coming to\r\nstay here the last week in July, and he gives us boys half-a-sovereign\r\neach, so that we might buy a stunning pig all ourselves twice over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways?  He never did so but once,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the only time he saw us, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal; \xe2\x80\x9cand we were quite\r\nlittle boys then.  I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what, Sam, he\xe2\x80\x99ll give us each a\r\nsovereign this time, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll buy a bow and arrows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStuff!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cI hope he won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate it!  I hate saying thank you; I shall get out of the way, if I\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam has no manners!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, turning round to Miss Fosbrook.  \xe2\x80\x9cTo\r\nthink that he had rather go without a sovereign or two than say thank\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99m_ too much of a gentleman to lay myself out for presents!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted\r\nSamuel; and the two boys fell on each other, buffeting one another, all\r\nin good part on Sam\xe2\x80\x99s side, though there was some temper and annoyance on\r\nHenry\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nWhen Sam was out of hearing, Hal discoursed very grandly on the sovereign\r\nhe intended Colonel Carey to give him, and the prodigious things he meant\r\nto do with it.  A gentleman once gave Osmond Greville two sovereigns; why\r\nshould not Colonel Carey be equally liberal?  And to hear the boy, those\r\ntwo sovereigns would buy everything in the world, from the pig to a\r\ndouble-barrelled gun.  David began to grow hurt, and to fear the Toby\r\nfund would be lost in this magnificence; but Hal assured him that it\r\nwould be a help, and they should all have a share in the pig, promising\r\npresents to everybody, which Susan and Annie expected with the more\r\ncertainty that Sam was never present to laugh down these fine projects.\r\n\r\nIndeed Miss Fosbrook had laughed at them once or twice, and observed that\r\nshe thought money earned or spared a better thing than money given; and\r\nthis caused Hal to cease to try to dazzle her, though he could not give\r\nup the pleasure of regaling his sisters in private with the wonders to be\r\ndone with Colonel Carey\xe2\x80\x99s possible sovereigns.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE second week was prosperous: the treasury made progress; and\r\nChristabel began to feel as if her pupils were not beyond her management,\r\nas at first she had feared.  Collectively they were less uncouth and\r\nbearish, not so noisy at their meals, nor so needlessly rude to one\r\nanother; and the habit of teasing Elizabeth whenever there was nothing\r\nelse to do was greatly lessened.
