Indeed Sam did not plague her himself,\r\nnor let his brothers do so, unless she tempted him by some very foolish\r\nwhine or bit of finery; and in such eases a little friendly merriment is\r\na sound cure, very unlike the hateful fault of tormenting for\r\ntormenting\xe2\x80\x99s sake.\r\n\r\nNor did Elizabeth give nearly so much cause for their rough laughter,\r\nsince Miss Fosbrook had given wholesome food to her tastes and likings,\r\npartly satisfying the longing for variety, beauty, or interest which had\r\nmade her discontented and restless.  Her head was full of _her_ secret,\r\nand her pretty plans for her gift.  Such lovely drawings she saw in her\r\nmind\xe2\x80\x99s eye, such fairies, such delightful ships, kittens, babies in the\r\ncradle!  But when the pencil was in her hand, the lines went all ways but\r\nthe right; her fairy was a grimy little object, whose second wing could\r\nnever be put on; the ships were saucers; the kitten might have been the\r\npig; the baby was an owl in an ivy-bush; and to look at the live baby in\r\nthe cradle only puzzled her the more.  Miss Fosbrook gave her real\r\ndrawing lessons; but boxes, palings, and tumble-down sheds, done with a\r\nbroad black pencil, did not seem to help her to what she wished.  Yet\r\nsometimes her fingers produced what surprised and pleased herself and\r\nChristabel; and she never was happier than when safely shut into Miss\r\nFosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s bed-room with her card and her paints.  She used to bolt\r\nherself in, with a little parade of mystery that made Annie exceedingly\r\ncurious, though the others generally let it alone as \xe2\x80\x9cBetty\xe2\x80\x99s fancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristabel wanted to learn botany for her own pleasure.  She found a book\r\nwhich Susan and Bessie pronounced to be horridly stupid (indeed Annie\r\ncalled it nasty, and was reproved for using such a word), but when the\r\ninformation in it was minced up small, and brought out in a new form,\r\nBessie enjoyed it extremely.  The whole party were delighted to gather\r\nflowers for Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x94the wetter or the steeper places they grew in\r\nthe better; but the boys thought it girlish to know the names; and Susan,\r\nthough liking gardening, did not in the least care for the inside of a\r\nflower.  Elizabeth, however, was charmed at the loveliness that was\r\npointed out to her; and even Annie, when the boys were not at hand,\r\nthought it very entertaining to look at petals, stamens, and pistils, and\r\nto see that a daisy is made up of a host of tiny flowers.  Both little\r\nsisters were having their eyes opened to see some of the wonder and some\r\nof the glory of this earth of ours.  It made Bessie much less often tired\r\nof everything and everybody; though after all there is but one spirit\r\nthat is certain never to be weary or dissatisfied, and into that she had\r\nyet to grow.\r\n\r\nFines were much less frequent: there were no foolish tears; only one\r\nlesson of John\xe2\x80\x99s turned back, two of Annie\xe2\x80\x99s, one of Susan\xe2\x80\x99s; some\r\nunbrushed hair of Susan\xe2\x80\x99s too\xe2\x80\x94an unlucky mention of the raven by Annie in\r\nlesson-time\xe2\x80\x94and some books left about by Sam.  Henry\xe2\x80\x99s fines were the\r\nserious ones: he had two for incorrect sums, one for elbows on the table,\r\nthree for talking, one for not putting his things away; and besides, he\r\n_could not_ go without a pennyworth of string; and the Grevilles would\r\nhave laughed at him if he had not bought some more marbles.\r\n\r\nBut what did that signify when Colonel Carey was coming? and a sovereign\r\nwould buy a pig three times over\xe2\x80\x94at least, if it was quite a little one.\r\nChristabel wished the hope of that sovereign had never occurred to him,\r\nfor he seemed to think it quite set him free from the little\r\nself-restraints by which the others were earning the pleasure of making\r\nthe gift; and though he still talked the most about the pig, he denied\r\nhimself the least for it.\r\n\r\nOne evening the boys came in with a great piece of news.  Their tutor had\r\nread in the paper that Admiral Penrose was appointed to the _Ramilies_,\r\nto take command in the Mediterranean.  He was a great friend of their\r\nfather, and, said the boys, was most likely to make him his flag-captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd me a naval cadet!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe said he would, when he was here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of you, he said,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it will be me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe looked at the rigging of my\r\nfrigate, and said I knew all the ropes quite well; and he told Papa he\r\nmight be proud of such a son!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! oh!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned the aggrieved multitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94such a family; but he was looking at me; and I know he will give me\r\nthe appointment; and I shall sail in his ship\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll see.  And when I get\r\nto the Mediterranean, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\xe2\x80\x94I shall kill a shark all\r\nmy own self!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA shark in the Mediterranean!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they get in by the Straits of Magellan?  Oh! is that\r\nthe other place?  Well, never mind\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll shoot the shark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStuff, Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam rather gruffly.\r\n\r\nHal went off on another tack.  \xe2\x80\x9cWell, at least he has set me down by this\r\ntime; and Papa will have me up to London for my outfit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will have leave, and come and see us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try; but, you see, I shall be an officer on duty, and I dare say\r\nAdmiral Penrose will hardly be able to spare me; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll send you all\r\npresents out of my pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll spend all your pay on yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of my prize-money then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t get prize-money without a war,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! don\xe2\x80\x99t let there be a war!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but there is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Harry in a tremendous tone; and as Miss\r\nFosbrook held up her hands, \xe2\x80\x9cat least there was one in the Black Sea; and\r\nI know there was a battle in the newspaper\xe2\x80\x94at least, Mr. Carey read about\r\nPalermo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think Garibaldi in Sicily will put much prize-money into your\r\npocket, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! but there\xe2\x80\x99s sure to be a war! and I shall get promoted, and be a man\r\nbefore any of you.  I shall go about, and see condors, and lions, and\r\nelephants, and wear a sword\xe2\x80\x94at least, a dirk\xe2\x80\x94while you are learning Latin\r\nand Greek at Uncle John\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make such a noise about it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam crossly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why you should be the one to go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cSam is\r\nthe eldest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but Sam is such a slow-coach.  Papa said I was the only one fit to\r\nmake a sailor of\xe2\x80\x94at least, he said I was smart, and\xe2\x80\x94Hollo!  Sam, I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave you kicking my legs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t keep up such a row then!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Sam; but Hal was in too full\r\nswing to be reached by slight measures.  He pushed his chair back, tucked\r\nup his feet like a tailor\xe2\x80\x99s, out of reach, and went on: \xe2\x80\x9cThen I shall\r\ncome home in my cocked hat, like Papa\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94at least, my cap\xe2\x80\x94and come and ask\r\nfor a holiday for you all at Uncle John\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUncle John was an under-master at one of the great public schools, and\r\nthe children were all a good deal in awe of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle John won\xe2\x80\x99t give one for _you_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, boys, I can\xe2\x80\x99t have this bickering,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.  \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee you trying which can be most provoking.  Stand up.  Now, David, say\r\ngrace.  There, Annie, finish that bit of bread out of doors.  Go out, and\r\nlet us have no more of this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe spoke now with much less fear of not being minded; and having seen\r\none of the quarrelsome parties safe out of the school-room, she went to\r\nfetch from her own room a glove that wanted mending; and on her return\r\nfound Sam alone there, curled up over his lesson-books on the locker,\r\nlooking so gloomy, that she was afraid she had made him sulky, for which\r\nshe would have been very sorry, since she had a respect for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked; and his \xe2\x80\x9cNothing\xe2\x80\x9d did not at all assure\r\nher that he was in a right mood.  She doubted whether to leave him alone;\r\nbut presently thought he looked more unhappy than ill-tempered, and\r\nventured to speak.  \xe2\x80\x9cHave you a hard piece to learn?  Perhaps I could\r\nhelp you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe let her come and look at his book; but, to her surprise, he had before\r\nhim a very easy problem in Euclid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, if you only gave your mind to this,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou would soon\r\nmake it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStupid stuff!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is all along of that, and the rest of\r\nit, that I have got to be a land-lubber!\xe2\x80\x9d and he threw the book to the\r\nother end of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you no chance?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, without taking notice of this\r\nrudeness, for she saw that the boy could hardly contain himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!  The Admiral did take notice of Hal; and one day when I was slow at\r\na proposition, my father said I was too block-headed to beat navigation\r\ninto, and that Hal is a smart fellow, worth two of me.  I know he is!  I\r\nknow that; only if he would not make such an intolerable crowing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you wish it very much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWish it!  Of course I do.  Why, my father is a sailor; and I remember\r\nthe _Fury_, and I saw the _Calliope_\xe2\x80\x94his ship that he had in the war\r\ntime.  Before I was as big as little George I always thought I should be\r\na sailor.  And now if Papa goes out with Admiral Penrose, and Hal too\xe2\x80\x94oh!\r\nit will be so horrid home!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut can\xe2\x80\x99t you both go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; my father said he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask to have two of us put down, unless\r\nperhaps some younger one had a chance by and by.  And Hal is the\r\nsharpest, and does everything better than I can when he has a mind.  My\r\nfather says, among so many all can\xe2\x80\x99t choose; and if this place is to be\r\nmine, Hal may want to be in the navy more than I.  Yes, it is all right,\r\nand Hal must go.  But\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94when my father is gone\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and Sam fairly burst\r\nout crying.  \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly know how different it is with him away till\r\nthis month.  I was such a little fellow when he went to the Black Sea;\r\nbut now\xe2\x80\x94never mind, though!\xe2\x80\x9d and he stamped his foot on the floor.  \xe2\x80\x9cPapa\r\nsaid it, and it must be.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t tell the others, Miss Fosbrook;\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\nresolutely went and picked up his Euclid, and began finding the place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will do your duty like a man, wherever you are, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nChristabel heartily.\r\n\r\nSam looked as if he had rather that she had not said it, but it was\r\ncomfortable to him for all that; and though she kept further compliments\r\nto herself, she could not but think that there was no fear but that he\r\nwould be a man, in the best sense of the word, before Hal, when she saw\r\nhim so manfully put his sore grievance out of his head, and turn to the\r\npresent business of conquering his lesson.  Nor did she hear another word\r\nfrom him about his disappointment.\r\n\r\nIt made her dislike Henry\xe2\x80\x99s boasts more than ever; and she used to cut\r\nthem short as fast as she could, till the young chatterer decided that\r\nshe was \xe2\x80\x9ccross,\xe2\x80\x9d and reserved all his wonderful \xe2\x80\x9cat leasts\xe2\x80\x9d for his\r\nsisters, and his proofs of manliness for the Grevilles.\r\n\r\nThe Gibraltar man did not come on Saturday; and Miss Fosbrook had been\r\nthe saving of several stamps by sending some queer little letters in her\r\nown to Mrs. Merrifield, so that on Monday morning the hoard was increased\r\nto seven-and-sixpence; although between fines and \xe2\x80\x9c_couldn\xe2\x80\x99t helps_,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHenry\xe2\x80\x99s sixpence had melted down to a halfpenny, which \xe2\x80\x9cwas not worth\r\nwhile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn this day arrived a servant from the Park, bringing a delicate little\r\nlilac envelope, stamped with a tiny rose, and directed to Miss\r\nMerrifield.  There was another rose on the top of the lilac paper; and\r\nthe writing was in a very neat hand.\r\n\r\n    My dear Susan,\r\n\r\n    Mamma desires me to say that she hopes you and Bessie and Annie will\r\n    come to dine early to-morrow, and play with me, and that Miss Fosbury\r\n    will come with you.  She hopes your Mamma is better, and would be\r\n    glad to have her address in London.\r\n\r\n                                                    I am your affectionate\r\n                                                    IDA ARABELLA GREVILLE.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!  Miss Fosbrook, may we go?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the girls with sparkling eyes.\r\n\r\nMrs. Merrifield had written that one or two such invitations might be\r\naccepted, but she had rather it did not happen too often, as visits at\r\nthe Park were unsettling to some of the children.  So as this was the\r\nfirst, Christabel gladly consented, rather curious and rather shy on her\r\nown account.\r\n\r\nElizabeth begged for the rose, to copy it, and as there were no little\r\nones present to seize it, she was allowed to have it; while Susan groaned\r\nand sighed over the misfortune of having to write a \xe2\x80\x9chorrible note\xe2\x80\x9d just\r\nat play-time; and the boys treated it as a sort of insult to the whole\r\nfamily that Ida should have mistaken their governess\xe2\x80\x99s name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell her you won\xe2\x80\x99t go till she has it right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; at which Annie\r\nmade a vehement outcry of \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d such as made them all laugh at her\r\nthinking him in earnest.\r\n\r\nSusan\xe2\x80\x99s note began\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    My dear Ida,\r\n\r\n    We shuold\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nBut then perceiving that something was the matter with her word, Susan\r\nsat and looked at it, till at last, perceiving that her _u_ and _o_ had\r\nchanged places, she tried putting a top to the _u_, and made it like an\r\n_a_; while the filling up the _o_ made it become a blot, such as caught\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Susie, you won\xe2\x80\x99t send such a thing as that up to Ida?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94that _would_ be a \xe2\x80\x98horrible note,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said her governess; and she ruled\r\nthe lines again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan impatiently; \xe2\x80\x9ccan\xe2\x80\x99t one send a message up by the\r\nman that we\xe2\x80\x99ll all come, without this fuss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Miss Fosbrook said that would be very uncivil; and Susan, groaning,\r\nstretched every finger till the lines were finished, and began again, in\r\nher scraggy round-hand\xe2\x80\x94getting safely through the \xe2\x80\x9cshould,\xe2\x80\x9d and also\r\nthrough \xe2\x80\x9clike to come very much;\xe2\x80\x9d but when Miss Fosbrook looked up next,\r\nshe saw that the rest of the note consisted of\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    Mamma is at Grandmamma\xe2\x80\x99s, No. 12,\xe2\x80\x94St., Grovensor Place.\r\n\r\n                                                    I am your affectionate\r\n                                                       SUSANNA MERRIFIELD.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, I am very sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! won\xe2\x80\x99t that do?\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Susan, beginning to get into despair.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook pointed to the word \xe2\x80\x9cGrovensor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear! oh dear!  I thought I had got that tiresome word this time.\r\nWhy can\xe2\x80\x99t it put its _ss_ and _ns_ into their proper sensible places?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried poor Susan, to whom it was a terrible enemy.  She used to try them\r\nin different places all the way round, in hopes that one might at last be\r\nright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you remember what I told you, that the first Grosvenor was the\r\ngrand huntsman?  _Grosveneur_ in French; that would show you where to put\r\nthe _s\xe2\x80\x94gros_, great.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Susan never wished to remember anything French; and Sam observed that\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe man deserved to be spelt wrong if he called himself by a French\r\nname.  Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t he be content to be Mr. Grandhunter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut as he is not, we must spell his name right, or Mrs. Greville will be\r\nshocked,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease can\xe2\x80\x99t you scratch it out?\xe2\x80\x9d said the disconsolate Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ should not like to send a note with a scratch in it.  Besides, yours\r\nis hardly civil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth; \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you know how people answer\r\ninvitations, Susie?  I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you.  \xe2\x80\x98Miss Susanna, and Miss Elizabeth,\r\nand Miss Annie Merrifield will be very happy to do the honour of dining\r\nwith \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99 Sam, why do you laugh at me always?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you are telling Ida you will do her honour by dining with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople always do honour when they dine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cI know they\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey profess to receive the honour, not confer it, Bessie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that is the model for Susie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnote.  It would be as much too formal as hers was too blunt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust I do it again?\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cI had rather not go, if it is to be\r\nsuch a plague.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, I fear you must, Susie.  It is quite needful to learn how to\r\nwrite a respectable note; really a more difficult thing than writing a\r\nlong letter.  I am sorry for you; but if you were not so careless in your\r\nletters to Mamma this would come more easily to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this time Miss Fosbrook not only ruled another sheet, but wrote, in\r\nfair large-hand on a slate, the words, that Susan might copy them without\r\nfresh troubles:\r\n\r\n    We are much obliged to your Mamma for her kind invitation, and shall\r\n    have much pleasure in coming with Miss Fosbrook to dine with you and\r\n    spend the day.  I am sorry to say that Mamma was not quite so well\r\n    when last we heard.  Her address is\xe2\x80\x94No. 12,\xe2\x80\x94St., Grosvenor-place.\r\n\r\nSusan thought that here were a very serious number of words, and begged\r\nhard for leave to leave out her sorrow.  Of course she was sorry, but\r\nwhat was the use of telling Ida so?\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook thought it looked better, but Susan might do as she\r\npleased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say it, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say it only to look\r\nbetter to Ida.\xe2\x80\x9d  With which words he and Hal walked off to the garden.\r\n\r\nWould it be believed?  Susan, in her delight at being near the end,\r\nforgot the grand huntsman, and made the unlucky Place \xe2\x80\x9cGrovesnor,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nthen, in her haste to mend it, put her finger into the wet ink, and\r\nsmeared not only that word, but all the line above!\r\n\r\nIt was a shame and a wonder that a girl of her age should be so incapable\r\nof producing a creditable note; and Miss Fosbrook was very near scolding\r\nher but she had pity on the tearful eyes and weary fingers, and spoke\r\ncheerfully: \xe2\x80\x9cThere, that was almost the thing.  One more trial, Susan,\r\nand you need never be afraid of Ida\xe2\x80\x99s notes again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Susan could not write notes, at least she was not cross; and it would\r\nbe well if many who could send off a much better performance with far\r\nless difficulty could go to work as patiently as she did, without one\r\npettish word to Miss Fosbrook, though that lady seemed to poor Susie as\r\nhard a task mistress as if she could have helped it.  This time Miss\r\nFosbrook authorized the leaving out of the spending the day, and\r\nsuggested that S. would be enough without the whole Susanna, and she\r\nmercifully directed the cover to Miss Greville.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, my dear, you have worked hard for your pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as\r\nSusan extended each hand to its broadest stretch to uncramp them, and\r\nstretched herself backwards as if she wanted to double her head down to\r\nher heels.  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall give you a good mark, Susie, as if it had been a\r\nlesson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan deserved it, for her patient perseverance had been all out of\r\nobedience, not in the mere desire of having her note admired.  Indeed,\r\ngood child, at the best it was a very poor affair for a girl of twelve,\r\nand Miss Fosbrook was ashamed of it when she looked at Ida\xe2\x80\x99s lady-like\r\nlittle billet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d said she to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether I shall feel as if I would\r\nchange my dear stupid Susan for Miss Ida?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Susan flew screaming and leaping out into the garden in a mad\r\ntom-boy fashion; but that could well be pardoned, as there were only her\r\nsisters to see her; and the pleasure of having persevered and done her\r\nbest was enough to make her heart and her limbs dance for merriment.\r\n\r\nDepend upon it, however wretched and miserable hard application to what\r\nwe do not like may seem at the moment, it is the only way to make\r\nplay-times really delicious.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\n\r\n\r\nMISS FOSBROOK soon knew what Mrs. Merrifield meant by saying that visits\r\nat the Park unsettled the children.  Susan indeed, though liking anything\r\nthat shortened lessons by an hour, and made a change, was not so fond of\r\nbeing on her good behaviour at the Park as to be greatly exalted at the\r\nprospect; but Elizabeth and Annie were changed beings.  They were\r\nconstantly breaking out with some new variety of wonder.  They wondered\r\nwhether they should dine in the school-room, or at Mrs. Greville\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nluncheon; they wondered if Mr. Greville would speak to them; they\r\nwondered whether Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Munsterthal would be cross; they wondered if\r\nIda still played with dolls; and they looked as if they thought\r\nthemselves wonderful, too, for going out for a day!\r\n\r\nNay, the wonders were at their tongues\xe2\x80\x99 end even when lessons began, and\r\nput their farthings in great peril; and when they had nothing else to\r\nwonder at, they wondered when it would be twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and took no\r\npains to swallow enormous yawns.  Once, over her copy, Elizabeth\r\nexclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cNow! yes, this is necessary, Miss Fosbrook!  May not we wear\r\nour white frocks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not ironed,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do let me go and tell Mary!  There\xe2\x80\x99s lots of time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, who\r\nhad lately thought it cruel of the clock to point only to half-past ten,\r\nand never bethought herself how Mary would like to be called off from her\r\nscrubbing to iron three white frocks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould your Mamma wish it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Christabel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear no,\xe2\x80\x9d was Susan\xe2\x80\x99s answer; \xe2\x80\x9cwe always wear clean ones of our\r\nevery-day frocks.  Our white ones are only for dinner-parties and\r\nChristmas-trees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie grumbled.  \xe2\x80\x9cHow cross!  I hate those nasty old spotty cottons;\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand Johnnie returned to the old story\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cLittle vain pussy-cat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp went Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s warning pencil, she shook her head, and held out\r\nher hand for two fines.  Elizabeth began to gulp and sob.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t, Betty!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cStop while you can.  You won\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\ngoing up with red eyes.  There, I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay your fine; and there\xe2\x80\x99s another\r\nfor my speaking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Susie; that was not foolish speaking, but kind words,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss\r\nFosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cbut no more now; go on, Annie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Annie, who was reading a little history of St. Paul, would call\r\nCilicia, Cicilia, and when told to spell it she began to cry too\r\ndecidedly for Susan\xe2\x80\x99s good-nature to check her tears.  And not only did\r\nElizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s copy look as if she had written it with claws instead of\r\nfingers, but she was grieving over her spotted cotton instead of really\r\nseeking for places in her map.  Thus the Moselle obstinately hid itself;\r\nand she absolutely shed tears because Miss Fosbrook declared that\r\nFrankfort _was_ on the Maine.  For the first time she had her grammar\r\nturned back upon her hands.  How many mistakes Annie made would be really\r\npast telling; for these two little girls had their whole minds quite\r\nupset by the thought of a day\xe2\x80\x99s pleasure; and as they never tried to\r\nrestrain themselves, and to \xe2\x80\x9cbe sober, be vigilant,\xe2\x80\x9d they gave way before\r\nall the little trials in their paths\xe2\x80\x94were first careless, and then\r\nfractious.  Perhaps when they were older they would find out that this\r\nuplifted sense of excited expectation is the very warning to be heedful.\r\n\r\nIf Miss Fosbrook had been a strict governess, she would have told them\r\nthey did not deserve to go at all; or at any rate, that Bessie must\r\nrepeat her grammar better, and re-write her copy, and that Annie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nunlucky addition sum must be made to prove; but she had seen her little\r\nsisters nearly as bad in prospect of a pantomime, so she was merciful,\r\nand sent them in good time to brush their hair, put on their spotted\r\ncottons, and wash off as much as possible of the red mottling left by\r\nthose foolish tears.\r\n\r\nTheir spirits rose again as fast as they had sunk; and it was a lively\r\nwalk through the park to the great house, with a good deal of skipping\r\nand jumping at first, and then, near the door, a little awe and gravity.\r\n\r\nThey were taken through a side-door of the hall to the school-room, where\r\nIda and her governess received them.  It was the first time that\r\nChristabel had seen her out of her beplumed hat, and she thought her a\r\npleasant, bright-looking little girl, not at all set up or conceited.\r\nHer mauve muslin, flounced though it was up to her waist, showed that it\r\nhad been wise to withstand Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s desire for the white muslins; but\r\nMiss Fosbrook had enough to do on her own account with the endeavour to\r\nunderstand the German governess\xe2\x80\x99s foreign accent, without attending to\r\nthe children more than was necessary.\r\n\r\nIt was not a very remarkable day, and the pleasures of it seemed hardly\r\nenough to justify the little girls\xe2\x80\x99 great excitement.  There was first\r\nthe dinner at the luncheon of the parents, where the children sat up\r\nrather formal and subdued, and not quite certain what all the dishes\r\nmight contain, a little afraid of getting what they _could_ not eat,\r\nthough desirous of making experiments in this land of wonders.  None of\r\nthem had forgotten, and they thought no one else had, how Bessie had once\r\ncome to disgrace by bursting out crying over the impossibility of\r\nfinishing some terrible rice-bordered greenish yellow stuff that burnt\r\nher mouth beyond bearing, and which Ida called curry, and said people in\r\nthe East Indies liked.  However, that was when Bessie had been a very\r\nlittle girl; and she still continued adventurous, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, if you\r\nplease,\xe2\x80\x9d to cutlets set round in a wreath, with all their bones sticking\r\nup, and covered with a reddish incrustation that Susan and Annie thought\r\nso unnatural, that they preferred the boiled chicken that at least they\r\ncould understand, though it had funny-hooking accompaniments in the\r\nsauce.  And Hal\xe2\x80\x99s report of some savoury jelly which he had once\r\nencountered would have deterred them from the pink transparency in the\r\nshape of a shell, if they had not seen Bessie getting on very well with\r\nit, Miss Fosbrook happily perceiving and cutting short Annie\xe2\x80\x99s intended\r\ninquiry whether it were nice.  To her great relief, this was the only\r\nwant of manners betrayed by her little savages, and she was able to keep\r\nher attention tolerably free from them, so as to look at the pictures on\r\nthe walls, observe the two boys, Hal\xe2\x80\x99s friends, and talk to Mrs.\r\nGreville, who made conversation with her very pleasantly.\r\n\r\nShe was much grieved to perceive, from what that lady said, that Mrs.\r\nMerrifield was thought to be much more ill, and in a far more alarming\r\nstate, than she had at all understood.  The girls were too young to enter\r\ninto the tone of sad sympathy with which Mrs. Greville spoke, and the\r\nmanner in which a doubt was expressed whether the Captain would be able\r\nto sail with Admiral Penrose if he should have the offer; and as soon as\r\nshe saw that they and their governess were in ignorance, she turned it\r\noff; but she had said enough to fill Christabel with anxiety and desire\r\nto know more; and as soon as the dinner was over, and the little girls\r\nhad run off together to visit Ida\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful cockatoo in the\r\nconservatory, she turned to Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Munsterthal, and begged to hear\r\nwhether she knew more than had been said.\r\n\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein Munsterthal did not quite know that such a person as Mrs.\r\nMerrifield was in existence; but she was very amiable and warm-hearted,\r\nand said how sad it was to think of the trouble that hung over \xe2\x80\x9cthese so\r\ncareless children,\xe2\x80\x9d and was doubly kind to the girls when they came back\r\nfrom their conversation with pretty \xe2\x80\x9cCocky,\xe2\x80\x9d who set up his\r\nlemon-coloured crest, coughed, sneezed, and said \xe2\x80\x9cCocky want a biscuit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nto admiration, till the boys were seen approaching; when Ida, knowing\r\nthat some torment would follow, took herself and her visitors back to the\r\nprotection of the governesses in time to prevent the cockatoo from being\r\nmade to fly at the girls, and powder them with the white dust under his\r\nfeathers.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was spent in the garden, the little girls betaking\r\nthemselves to a pretty moss-covered arbour, where there was a grand\r\ndoll\xe2\x80\x99s feast.  Ida had no less than twenty-three dolls, ranging from the\r\nmagnificent Rosalind, who had real hair that could be brushed, and was as\r\nlarge as little Sally at home, down to poor little china Mildred, whose\r\nproper dwelling-place was a bath, and who had with great difficulty been\r\nput into petticoats enough to make her fit to be seen out of it.  Now\r\nnobody at home could have saved the life of a doll for a single day, and\r\nSusan and Elizabeth were both thought far above them; but these\r\nbeautifully arrayed young ladies had always been the admiration of the\r\nheart of Bessie as well as of Annie, and they were not too old for\r\nextreme satisfaction in handling their elegant ladyships, and still more\r\ntheir beautiful dinner and tea-service of pink and white ware.\r\n\r\nSusan, though she could not write a note, or do lessons like Ida, was\r\nolder in the ways of life, and played rather as she did with the little\r\nones at home than for her own amusement.  She would much rather have had\r\nthe fun of \xe2\x80\x9ccats and mice\xe2\x80\x9d with her brothers; and but for the honour of\r\nthe thing, so perhaps would Annie.  However, they were all very happy,\r\ngetting the dolls up in the morning, giving Mildred washing enough for\r\nall the twenty-three, making them breakfast, hearing lessons, in which\r\nIda was governess, and made them talk so many languages that Annie was\r\nalarmed.  Of course one of the young ladies was very naughty, and was\r\ntreated with extreme severity; then there was dinner, a walk, an illness,\r\nand a dinner-party.  While all the time the two real governesses sat in\r\nthe shade outside, and talked in English or German as best they might,\r\nthe Fr\xc3\xa4ulein understanding Christabel\xe2\x80\x99s English the best, as did\r\nChristabel the Fr\xc3\xa4ulein\xe2\x80\x99s German.  They began to make friends, and to\r\nwish to see more of one another.\r\n\r\nThere was a walk round the garden, and admiration of the beautiful\r\nflowers, and the fountain and pond of gold-fish, till the boys came home,\r\nand got hold of the garden-engine for watering, crying out, \xe2\x80\x9cFire! fire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand squirting out the showers of water very much in the direction of the\r\ngirls.\r\n\r\nIda became quite crimson red, and got hold of Susan\xe2\x80\x99s hand to drag her\r\naway; then, as the foremost drops of another shower touched her, she\r\nfaced about, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cOsmond! don\xe2\x80\x99t, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell Mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d  There was a\r\ngreat rude laugh, as of boys who well knew the threat was never put in\r\nexecution; and poor Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Munsterthal only shook her head at Miss\r\nFosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s look of amaze, and said in German that \xe2\x80\x9c_die Knaben_\xe2\x80\x9d were far\r\ntoo _unartig_ for her to keep in order.  She pitied Miss Fosbrook for\r\nhaving so many in charge as to destroy all peace.  And if Sam and Hal had\r\nbeen like these two, Christabel felt that she could have done nothing\r\nwith them.  To her dismay, Osmond and Martin came in to the school-room\r\ntea; and she never had thought to feel so thankful for poor dear Susan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nslowness of comprehension, for, from their whispers among themselves, and\r\nfrom their poor tormented sister\xe2\x80\x99s blushes, she was clear that the \xe2\x80\x9cfire\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwas a piece of bad wit on Susan\xe2\x80\x99s red hair.  Boys who could so basely\r\ninsult a guest, and that a girl, she was sure must be bad companions for\r\nSam and Henry.  Such little gentlemen as they had been at dinner too, so\r\npolite and well-behaved before their father and mother!  There could be\r\nno doubt that something must be very wrong about them, or they would not\r\nchange so entirely when out of sight.  It is not always true that a child\r\nmust be deceitful who is less good in the absence of the authorities;\r\nbecause their presence is a help and a restraint, checking the beginning\r\nof mischief, and removing temptation; but one who does not fall by\r\nweakness, but intentionally alters his conduct the instant the elder is\r\ngone, shows that his will has been disobedient all along.\r\n\r\nBy and by Mr. Greville\xe2\x80\x99s voice was heard calling, \xe2\x80\x9cMartin!  Osmond!\xe2\x80\x9d As\r\nthey went out to meet him in the passage, Miss Fosbrook clearly\r\noverheard, \xe2\x80\x9cHere is the spring of the garden-engine spoilt.  Do you know\r\nanything about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not been meddling with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d  And they ran downstairs.\r\n\r\nThe colour flushed into Christabel\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks with horror.  She was glad\r\nthat her little girls were all in Ida\xe2\x80\x99s room, listening to a musical-box,\r\nand well out of hearing of such fearfully direct falsehoods, as it seemed\r\nto her, not knowing that the boys excused it to their own minds by the\r\nnotion that it was not the _spring_ of the engine that they had been\r\nmeddling with, and that so they did not know how the harm had been\r\ndone\xe2\x80\x94as if it made it any better that they lied to themselves as well as\r\ntheir father!  The German saw her dismay, and began to say how unlike her\r\nIda was to her brothers\xe2\x80\x94so truthful, so gentle, and courteous; but poor\r\nChristabel could not get over the thought of the ease and readiness with\r\nwhich deceit came to these boys.  Could their daily companions, Samuel\r\nand Henry, have learnt the same effrontery, and be deceiving her all this\r\ntime?  No, no, she could not, would not think it!  Assuredly not of Sam!\r\nShe was very glad not to see the boys again, and went home with her\r\npupils, rather heavy-hearted, at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, just as Ida was to put on\r\nher white muslin and pink ribbons, and go down after dinner for half an\r\nhour.\r\n\r\nThere were many kisses at parting, and a whole box of sweets, done up in\r\nbeautifully coloured and gold and silver paper, presented to the little\r\nvisitors; but it might be supposed that the girls were tired, for there\r\nwas a fretful snarling all the way across the park, because Elizabeth\r\ninsisted that the gifts should be called _bon-bons_, and the others would\r\nhear of nothing but goodies.  Nobody looked at the beautiful evening sky,\r\nnor at the round red moon coming up like a lamp behind the trees, nor at\r\nthe first stars peeping out, nor even at the green light of the\r\nglow-worm\xe2\x80\x94all which were more beautiful than anything Ida had shown them,\r\nexcept perhaps the hothouse flowers; and at last two such cross\r\nill-tempered voices sounded from Bessie and Annie, that Christabel turned\r\nround and declared that she should not let the sugar-plums be touched for\r\na week if another word were said about them.\r\n\r\nShe hoped that when the visit was over it would be done with; but no such\r\nthing.  Though Susan was her own good hearty self, Elizabeth had not\r\nrecovered either on that day of the next from the effects of the\r\npleasuring.  On each she cried over her lessons, and was cross at\r\nwhatever the boys said to her, made a fuss about keeping the ornamental\r\ncases of the _bon-bons_, and went about round-backed, peevish, and\r\ndiscontented, finding everything flat and ugly after her one peep at the\r\nluxuries of the Park.  Her farthings melted away fast; but she seemed to\r\nthink this her misfortune, not her fault.  She did not try to talk to\r\nMiss Fosbrook, feeling perhaps that she was in a naughty mood, which she\r\nwould not try to shake off; and she made no attempt to go on with her\r\npresent for her Mamma, it looked so poor and trumpery after the beautiful\r\nthings she had seen.\r\n\r\nNor did Christabel like to remind her of it, fearing that the occasion\r\nfor giving it might never come; but she did feel that it was a mournful\r\nthing to see the child, who was in danger of so fearful a sorrow, wasting\r\nher grief in pining after foolish fancies, and turning what should have\r\nbeen a refreshing holiday into an occasion of longing after what she thus\r\nmade into pomps and vanities of this wicked world.  Christabel had heard\r\nthat people who murmur among blessings often have those blessings\r\nsnatched away, and this made her tremble for poor little discontented\r\nElizabeth.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHERE!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cI really have got a letter from Papa himself.\r\nWhat a prize!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to mind your Grosvenor when you answer _him_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut hollo, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor Susan\xe2\x80\x99s eyes had grown large, and her whole face scarlet, and she\r\ngave a little cry as she read.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Mamma, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mamma\xe2\x80\x94Mamma is so very ill!\xe2\x80\x9d and Susan throw the letter down, and\r\nbroke into a fit of sobbing.\r\n\r\nSam caught it up, and Elizabeth came to read it with him, both standing\r\nstill and not speaking a word, but staring at the letter with their eyes\r\nfixed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, tenderly putting her arm round\r\nSusan; but she sobbed too much to make a word distinct, and Bessie held\r\nout the letter to her governess, looking white, and too much awed to\r\nspeak.\r\n\r\nCaptain Merrifield wrote in short, plain, sad words, that he thought it\r\nright that his children should know how matters stood.  The doctors\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntreatment, for which their mother had been taken to London, had not\r\nsucceeded, but had occasioned such terrible illness, that unless by the\r\nmercy of God she became much better in the course of a day or two, she\r\ncould not live.  If she should be worse, he would either write or\r\ntelegraph, and Susan and Sam must be ready to set out at once on the\r\nreceipt of such a message, and come up by the next train to London, where\r\nthey should be met at the station.  He had promised their mother that in\r\ncase of need he would send for them.\r\n\r\n    God bless you, my poor children, and have mercy on us all!\r\n\r\n                                                       Your loving father,\r\n                                                            H. MERRIFIELD.\r\n\r\nThat was all; and Christabel felt, more than even the children did, from\r\nhow full and heavy a heart those words had been written.\r\n\r\nThough she hardly knew how to speak, she tried to comfort Susan by\r\nshowing her that her father had evidently not given up all hope; but\r\nSusan was crying more at the thought of her Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s present illness and\r\npain than with fear of the future; and Sam said sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cHe would not have\r\nwritten at all unless it had been very bad indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I believe, in cases like this, there is\r\noften great fear, and then very speedy improvement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, speaking for the first time, \xe2\x80\x9cI know it will be.\r\nLittle girls in story-books always do have their mammas\xe2\x80\x94die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStory-books are all nonsense, so it won\xe2\x80\x99t happen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; and really\r\nit seemed as if the habit of contradicting Bessie had suggested to him\r\nthe greatest consolation that had yet occurred.\r\n\r\nJust then Henry and the younger ones came in, and learnt the tidings.\r\nHenry wept as bitterly as his elder sister, and John and Annie both did\r\nthe same; but David did not speak one word, as if he hardly took in what\r\nwas the matter, and, going to the window, took up his lesson-books as\r\nusual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam presently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we can\xe2\x80\x99t go to Mr. Carey to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we shall,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose a telegraph should come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then you can send for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Hal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry crossly; \xe2\x80\x9cI know Mr. Carey will give us\r\nleave when he knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want leave,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to kick up a row, as you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndo if you stay at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, if the message comes, I shall take Susie to London instead of\r\nyou.  I\xe2\x80\x99m sure they want me most!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, go down to Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s with your brother, if you please, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMiss Fosbrook decidedly.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf he should tell you not to stay, I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhelp it; but you will none of you do any good by hanging about without\r\ndoing your daily duties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHal saw he had no chance, and marched off, muttering about its being very\r\nhard.  Sam picked up his books, and turned to go, with a grave steady\r\nlook that was quite manly in its sadness, only stopping to say, \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\r\nJackie, you be good!\xe2\x80\x94Please Miss Fosbrook, let him run down after me if\r\nthe message comes, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be back before the horse is out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook promised, and could not help shaking hands with the brave\r\nboy, if only to show that she felt with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen must we all do our lessons?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Annie disconsolately, when he\r\nwas gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my dear; I think we shall all be the better for not neglecting what\r\nwe ought to do.  But there is one thing that we can do for your dear\r\nMamma; you know what I mean.  Suppose you each went away alone for five\r\nminutes, and were to come back when I ring the little bell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe first to come back was Annie, with the question in a low whisper,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, will God make Mamma better if we are very good?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook kissed her, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear little girl, I cannot tell.\r\nAll I can certainly tell you is, that He hears the prayers of good\r\nchildren, and if it be better for her and for you He will give her back\r\nto you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnnie did not quite understand, but she entered into what Miss Fosbrook\r\nsaid enough to wish to be good; so she took up her book, and began to\r\nlearn with all her might.\r\n\r\nElizabeth would have thought it much more like a little girl in a book to\r\nhave done no lessons, but have sat thinking, and perhaps reading the\r\nBible all day; but on the whole Elizabeth had hardly thoughts enough to\r\nlast her so long; nor was she deep or serious enough to have done herself\r\nmuch good by keeping the Bible open before her.  In fact she did lose her\r\nverse in merely reading the chapter for the day!  So it was just as well\r\nthat she had something to do that was not play, and that was a duty, and\r\nthus might give the desire to be good something to bear upon.\r\n\r\nBut Christabel saw by Susan\xe2\x80\x99s face, and heard in the shaken voice with\r\nwhich she took her turn in the reading, that she could not have given her\r\nmind to her tasks, and did not need them to keep her out of mischief.  It\r\nwould have been cruel to have required her to sit down to them just then,\r\nand her governess was glad to be able to excuse her on account of the\r\npacking-up.  All her things and Sam\xe2\x80\x99s must be got ready in case of an\r\nimmediate start, and she was sent up to the nursery to take care of the\r\nlittle ones, while Nurse and Mary mended, ironed, and packed.\r\n\r\nTo be sure Nurse Freeman made poor Susan unnecessarily unhappy by being\r\nsure that it was all the fault of the London doctors; but she was a kind,\r\ntender old woman, and her petting was a great comfort to the poor girl.\r\nWhat did her most good, however, was sitting quite quiet with the little\r\nones while they were asleep, and all alone; it seemed to rest and compose\r\nher, and she always loved to be in charge of them.  Poor child! she might\r\nsoon have to be their little mother!  She was able to play with them when\r\nthey awoke, and cheered herself up with their pretty ways, and by finding\r\nhow quickly Baby was learning to walk.  Ah! but would Mamma ever see her\r\nwalk?\r\n\r\nIf any of the children thought it unjust that Susan\xe2\x80\x99s lessons should be\r\nlet off, they were wrong.  Parents and teachers must have the power of\r\ndoing such things without being judged.  Sometimes they see that a child\r\nis really unable to learn, when the others perceive no difference; and it\r\nwould be very harsh and cruel to oppress one who is out of order for fear\r\nlittle silly, idle, healthy things should think themselves hardly used.\r\n\r\nAt any rate, the lessons were capitally done; and when the children met\r\nagain, they were all so much brighter and more hopeful, that they quite\r\nbelieved that their Mamma was going to get better very fast.  Bessie\r\nespecially was so resolved that thus it should be, that she shut herself\r\ninto Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s room, and drew and painted with all her might, as if\r\npreparing for Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s birthday made it certain that it would be kept.\r\n\r\nThe boys brought word that they would have a holiday the next day, as it\r\nwas the Feast of St. Barnabas, and after morning service Mr. Carey was\r\ngoing to meet his brother and bring him home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be all the more certain to get the sovereign, or two\r\nsovereigns,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry to David, the only person whom he could find to\r\nlisten to him, \xe2\x80\x9cif Sam is gone; and everyone will be caring about me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then you\xe2\x80\x99ll give it to the pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, to be sure.  You will grow into a pig yourself if you go on that\r\nway, David.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, David, partaking the family distrust of Hal\xe2\x80\x99s birds-in-the-bush,\r\nand being started on the subject of the hoard, ran up to Sam, who was\r\nlearning his lessons by way of something to do, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cIf you go to\r\nLondon, Sam, may I have your sixpence on Monday for the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I am going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if you do\xe2\x80\x94or we sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t get the pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you care if we don\xe2\x80\x99t get the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.  Be off with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid next betook himself to his eldest sister, who was trying to write\r\nto her father, and finding such a letter harder and sadder work than that\r\nto Ida Greville, though no one teased her about writing, blots, or\r\nspelling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you go to London, Susie,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, in the very same words, \xe2\x80\x9cmay I\r\nhave your sixpence on Monday for the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Davie, don\xe2\x80\x99t be tiresome!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid only said it over again in the same words, and put his hand down on\r\nher letter in his earnestness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome away, Davie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t tease your sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want her to say I may have her sixpence on Monday for the pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan angrily; \xe2\x80\x9cyou care for the nasty pig\r\nmore than for poor Mamma or anyone else, and you sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo seldom did Susan say anything cross, that everyone looked up\r\nsurprised.  Miss Fosbrook saw that it was sheer unhappiness that made her\r\nspeak sharply, and would not take any notice, except by gently taking\r\naway the pertinacious David.\r\n\r\nHe was very much distressed at the refusal; and when Miss Fosbrook told\r\nhim that his brother and sister could not think of such things when they\r\nwere in such trouble, he only answered, \xe2\x80\x9cBut Hannah Higgins won\xe2\x80\x99t get her\r\npig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was vexed herself that her friend David should seem\r\npossessed with this single idea, as if it shut out all others from his\r\nmind.  He was consoled fast enough; for Susan, with another great sob,\r\nthrew down her pen, and coming up to stroke him down with her inky\r\nfingers, cried out, \xe2\x80\x9cO Davie, Davie, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why I\r\nsaid it.  You shall have my sixpence, or anything!  But, oh dear, I wish\r\nthe message was come, and we were going to dear Mamma, for I can\xe2\x80\x99t write,\r\nand I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she went back to her place, and tried to write, and sat with her\r\nhead on her hand, and dawdled and cried and blotted till it grew so near\r\npost-time that at last Miss Fosbrook took the longest of her scrawls, and\r\nwriting three lines at the bottom to say how it was with them all,\r\ndirected it to Captain Merrifield, thinking that he would like it better\r\nthan nothing from home, sent it off, and made Susan come out to refresh\r\nher hot eyes and burning head in the garden.\r\n\r\nSam presently came and walked on her other side, gravely and in silence,\r\nglad to be away from the chatter and disputes of the younger ones.  That\r\nsummons had made them both feel older, and less like children, than ever\r\nbefore; but they did not speak much, only, when they sat down on a garden\r\nbench, as Miss Fosbrook held Susan\xe2\x80\x99s hand, she presently found some rough\r\nhard young fingers stealing into her own on the other side, and saw Sam\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes glistening with unshed tears.  She stroked his hand, and they\r\ndropped fast: but he was ashamed to cry, and quickly dried them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you will be a man, Sam; take care of Susan,\r\nand be a comfort to your father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I shall,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody can tell how beforehand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cOnly watch to see what he\r\nmay seem to want to have done for him.  Sit quietly by, and don\xe2\x80\x99t get in\r\nthe way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you ever so unhappy, Miss Fosbrook?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, once I was, when my father was knocked down by an omnibus, and was\r\nvery ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell us about it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\nShe did tell them of her week of sorrow and anxious care of the younger\r\nchildren, and the brightening ray of hope at last.  It seemed to freshen\r\nboth up, and give them hopes, for each drew a long sigh of relief; and\r\nthen Sam said, \xe2\x80\x9cPapa wrote to Mr. Carey.  She is to be prayed for in\r\nchurch to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, with a sound as of dismay, which made Christabel ask in\r\nwonder why she was sorry, when, from Susan\xe2\x80\x99s half-uttered words, she\r\nfound that the little girl fancied that a \xe2\x80\x9chappy issue out of all her\r\nafflictions\xe2\x80\x9d meant death.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat it means is, that the afflictions may\r\nend happily in whatever way God may see to be best; it may be in getting\r\nwell; it may be the other way: at any rate, it is asking that the\r\ndistress may be over, not saying how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t there some other prayer in the Prayer-book about it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam,\r\nlooking straight before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will show you where to find it, in the Visitation of the Sick.  I dare\r\nsay it has often been read to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy and girl came in with her, and brought their Prayer-books to her\r\nroom, that she might mark them.\r\n\r\nThis had been a strange, long, sad day of waiting and watching for the\r\ntelegram, and the children even fancied it might come in the middle of\r\nthe night; but Miss Fosbrook thought this unlikely, and looked for the\r\nmorrow\xe2\x80\x99s post.  There was no letter.  It was very disappointing, but Miss\r\nFosbrook thought it a good sign, since at least the danger could not be\r\nmore pressing, and delay always left room for hope.\r\n\r\nThe children readily believed her; they were too young to go on dwelling\r\nlong on what was not in sight; and even Susan was cheerful, and able to\r\nthink about other things after her night\xe2\x80\x99s rest, and the relief of not\r\nhearing a worse account.\r\n\r\nThe children might do as they pleased about going to church on saints\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndays, and on this day all the three girls wished to go, as soon as it had\r\nbeen made clear that even if the message should come before the short\r\nservice would be over, there would be ample time to reach the station\r\nbefore the next train.  Miss Fosbrook was glad to prove this, for not\r\nonly did she wish to have them in church, but she thought the weary\r\nwatching for the telegram was the worst thing possible for Susan.  Sam\r\nwas also going to church, but Henry hung back, after accompanying them to\r\nthe end of the kitchen-garden.  \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go, Sam; just suppose if the\r\nmessage came without anyone at home, and you had to set out at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s no train.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but they always put on a special train whenever anyone is ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there would be plenty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least they did when Mr. Greville\xe2\x80\x99s mother was ill, so they will now;\r\nand then you may ride upon the engine, for there won\xe2\x80\x99t be any carriages,\r\nyou know.  I say, Sam, if you go to church, and the telegraph comes, I\r\nshall set off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll do no such thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou had much better come to\r\nchurch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t.  It is like a girl to go to church on a week-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is much more like a girl to mind what a couple of asses, like the\r\nGrevilles, say,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Sam, taking up his cap and running after his\r\nsisters and their governess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite right,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Henry to John and David, who alone remained\r\nto listen to him, \xe2\x80\x9cthat one of us should stay in case the telegraph comes\r\nin, and there are any orders to give.  I can catch the pony, you know,\r\nand ride off to Bonchamp, and if the special train is there, I shall get\r\nupon the engine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is Sam and Susan who are going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s only because Sam is eldest.  I know Mamma would like to have\r\nme much better, because I don\xe2\x80\x99t walk hard like Sam; and when I get there,\r\nshe will be so much better already, and we shall be all right; and\r\nAdmiral Penrose will be so delighted at my courage in riding on the\r\nengine and putting out the explosion, or something, that he will give me\r\nmy appointment as naval cadet at once, and I shall have a dirk and a\r\nuniform, and a chest of my own, and be an officer, and get promoted for\r\nfiring red-hot shot out of the batteries at Gibraltar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaster Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Purday, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t throw them little apples about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are red-hot shot, Purday!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll red-hot shot you if you break my cucumber frames, young gentleman!\r\nCome, get out with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nProbably anxiety made Purday cross as well as everyone else, or else he\r\ndistrusted Henry\xe2\x80\x99s discretion without Sam, for he hunted the little boys\r\naway wherever they went.  Now they would break the cucumber frames; now\r\nthey would meddle with the gooseberries, or trample on the beds; and at\r\nlast he only relented so far as to let David stay with him on condition\r\nof being very good, and holding the little cabbages as he planted them\r\nout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaster Davie was a solemn one,\xe2\x80\x9d Purday said, and they were great\r\nfriends; but Hal and Johnnie were fairly turned out, as their idle hands\r\nwere continually finding fresh mischief to do in their sauntering\r\ndesultory mood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, \xe2\x80\x9csince Purday is so savage, we\xe2\x80\x99ll go and look out at\r\nthe gate, and then we shall see if the telegraph comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohnnie had no clear idea what a telegraph was, and was curious to know\r\nhow it would come, rather expecting it to be a man in a red coat on\r\nhorseback, blowing a horn\xe2\x80\x94a sight that certainly was not to be missed; so\r\nhe willingly strolled down after Henry to the gate leading to the lane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see any way at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry, looking out into the lane.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall get up, and so see over into the bend of the road;\xe2\x80\x9d and Hal mounted\r\nto the topmost bar of the gate, and sat astride there, John scrambling\r\nafter him not quite so easily, his legs being less long, and his dress\r\nless convenient.  Both knew that their Papa strongly objected to their\r\nclimbing on this iron gate, the newest and handsomest thing about the\r\nplace; but thought Hal, \xe2\x80\x9cOf course no one will care what I do when I am\r\nso anxious about poor Mamma!\xe2\x80\x9d and thought Johnnie, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat Hal does, of\r\ncourse I may do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo there the two young gentlemen sat perched, each with one leg on either\r\nside of the new iron gate.  It was rather like sitting on the edge of a\r\nknife; and John could scarcely reach his toes down to rest them on the\r\nbar below, but he held on by the spikes, and it was so new and glorious a\r\nposition, that it made up for a good deal to be five feet above the road;\r\nmoreover, Hal said it was just like the mast-head of a man-of-war\xe2\x80\x94at\r\n_least_, when the waves didn\xe2\x80\x99t dash right overhead, like the picture of\r\nthe Eddystone Lighthouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHollo! what, a couple of cherubs aloft!\xe2\x80\x9d cried a voice from the road;\r\nand looking round, Henry beheld the two Grevilles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s very jolly up here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! is it?  Riding on a razor, to my mind.  Come down, and have a lark,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Osmond; while Martin, undoing the gate, proceeded to swing it\r\nbackwards and forwards, to John\xe2\x80\x99s extreme terror; but the more he clung\r\nto the spikes, and cried for mercy, the quicker Martin swung it, shouting\r\nwith laughter at his fright.  Henry meanwhile scrambled and tumbled to\r\nthe ground, and caught the gate and held it fast, while he asked what his\r\nfriends had been about.  One held up a scarlet flask of powder, the other\r\na bag of shot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t got a gun!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but we know where gardener keeps his; and the governor\xe2\x80\x99s out for the\r\nday.  Come along, Hal: you shall have your turn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go far from home to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, stuff!  What was it Mamma heard, Osmond?  That your mother was ever\r\nso much better, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it was worse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Osmond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, never mind: your hanging about here won\xe2\x80\x99t do her any good, I\r\nsuppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99ll catch it from the governess!\xe2\x80\x94I say, how many seams shall you\r\nhave to sew to-day, Hal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t sew seams: I do as I please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa!  Is that them coming out of church!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is! it is!\xe2\x80\x9d cried John from his elevation.  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, help me down,\r\nHal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Henry did not want Miss Fosbrook to find him partaking in\r\ngate-climbing; and either that desire, or the general terror a bad\r\nconscience, made him and the Grevilles run helter-skelter the opposite\r\nway, leaving poor little John stuck on the top of the gate, quite giddy\r\nat the thought of coming down alone, and almost as much afraid of being\r\nthere caught by Miss Fosbrook coming home from church.\r\n\r\nIt was a false alarm after all, that the congregation were coming out.\r\nJohn would have been glad if they had; for nothing could be more\r\nmiserable than sitting up there, his fingers tired of clutching the\r\nspikes, his feet strained with reaching down to the bar, his legs chilled\r\nwith the wind, his head almost giddy when he thought of climbing down.\r\nHe would have cried, could he have spared a hand to rub his eyes with; he\r\nhad a great mind to have roared for help, especially when he heard feet\r\nupon the road; but these turned out to belong to five little village\r\nboys, still smaller than himself, who, when they saw the young gentleman\r\non his perch, all stood still in a row, with their mouths wide open,\r\nstaring at him.  Johnnie scorned to let them think he was not riding\r\nthere for his own pleasure; so he tried to put a bold face of the matter,\r\nand look as much at ease and indifferent as he could, under great bodily\r\nfear and discomfort, the injury of his brother\xe2\x80\x99s desertion, the\r\nexpectation of disgrace, and the reflection that he was being disobedient\r\nto his parents in the height of their trouble!\r\n\r\nThere is nothing in grief that of necessity makes children or grown\r\npeople good.  Sometimes, especially when there is suspense, it fills them\r\nwith excitement, as well as putting them out of their usual habits; and\r\nthus it often happens that there are tremendous explosions of naughtiness\r\njust when some one is ill in a house, and the children ought to be most\r\ngood.  But it is certain that unless trouble be taken in the right way,\r\nit makes people worse instead of better.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\n\r\n\r\nHAL had got into a mood in which he was tired of fears and of waiting for\r\ntidings, and was glad to shake off the thought, and be carried along to\r\nsomething new, he and the Grevilles were rather fond of one another\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncompany, in an idle sort of way.  They \xe2\x80\x9cput him up to things,\xe2\x80\x9d as he\r\nsaid; they made a variety; and he was always glad of listeners to his\r\nwonderful stories, which rather diverted the other boys, who, though they\r\nsometimes made game of them, were much less apt to pick them to pieces\r\nthan was Sam.\r\n\r\nPoor Captain Merrifield! what had not befallen him, according to his son?\r\nHe had been stuck on to a rock of loadstone; he had been bitten by\r\nmosquitos as big as jackdaws\xe2\x80\x94at least as jack-snipes; he had sat down to\r\nrest on the trunk of a fallen tree, and it whisked him over on his face,\r\nand turned out to be a rattle-snake\xe2\x80\x94at least, a boa-constrictor!  Nay,\r\nHenry discoursed on the ponies he had himself tamed, the rabbits he had\r\nshot, the trees he had climbed, the nests he had found, the rats he had\r\nkilled, in terms he durst not use when his brother was by; or if he did,\r\nand Sam brought him to book, he always said \xe2\x80\x9cit was all fun.\xe2\x80\x9d  It often\r\nseemed as if he did not himself know whether he meant to be believed or\r\notherwise; and as to his intentions for his sailor life, they were, as\r\nhas been already seen, of the most splendid character!  Sometimes he shot\r\nthe French admiral dead from the mast-head; sometimes he sailed into\r\nPlymouth with the whole enemy\xe2\x80\x99s fleet behind him; sometimes he, the\r\nyoungest midshipman, rescued the whole crew in a wreck where all the\r\nother officers were drowned; sometimes he shot a shark through the head,\r\njust as it was about to make a meal of Prince Alfred!\r\n\r\nHe certainly was thus an entertaining companion to those who did not pay\r\nheed to truth, and liked to hear or laugh at great swelling words; and\r\nthe Grevilles, on their idle day, were glad to have him with them, and\r\nwere rather curious to prove how much fact there was in his boast of\r\nbeing a most admirable shot.\r\n\r\nMeddling with guns was absolutely forbidden to all the three, except by\r\nspecial permission and with an elder looking on; but the Grevilles were\r\nnot in the habit of obeying, except when they were forced to do so; and\r\nHenry, having once begun to think no one would heed his present doings,\r\nwas ready to go on rather than be accused of minding his governess.\r\n\r\nSo the gardener\xe2\x80\x99s gun was taken from the hiding-place, whither it had\r\nbeen conveyed from the tool-house; and the three boys ran off together,\r\ntheir first object being to get out of the Greville grounds, where they\r\ncould be met by any of the men.  They got quite out into the fields,\r\nbefore they ventured to stop that Osmond might load the gun.  Each was to\r\ntake a shot in turn; Osmond tried first, at a poor innocent young thrush,\r\nnewly come out for his earliest flight.  Happily he missed it; Martin\r\nclaimed the next, and for want of anything better to shoot, took aim at\r\nthe scare-crow in the middle of Farmer Grice\xe2\x80\x99s beans.  He was sure that\r\nhe had hit it, and showed triumphantly the great holes in its hat; but\r\nthe other boys were strongly persuaded that they had been there before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, come away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Osmond; \xe2\x80\x9cthis is a great deal too near old\r\nGrice\xe2\x80\x99s farm-yard.  If we go popping about here, we shall have him out\r\nupon us, for an old tiger as he is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martin.\r\n\r\nBut Hal had just got the gun, and saw something so black and shiny\r\nthrough the hedge, that he was persuaded that a flock of rooks were\r\nfeeding in the next field, and he fired!\r\n\r\nSuch a cackling and screeching as arose! and with it one dying gobble,\r\nand a very loud \xe2\x80\x9cHollo! you rascal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy eyes! you\xe2\x80\x99ve been and gone and done it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Osmond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCut! cut!\xe2\x80\x9d screamed Martin; and Hal, not exactly knowing what he had\r\ndone, but sure that it was something dreadful, and hearing voices in\r\npursuit, threw down the gun, and took to his heels; but the others had\r\nthe start of him, and were over the gap long before he could get to it.\r\nAnd even as he did reach it, a hand was on his throat, almost choking\r\nhim, and a tremendous voice cried, \xe2\x80\x9cYou young poacher, you sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\noff that way!  I\xe2\x80\x99ll have you up to the Bench, that I will, for shooting\r\nthe poor old turkey-cock before my very eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t!  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal, turning in the terrible\r\ngrip; \xe2\x80\x9cI thought it was only a rook!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rook, I dare say!  And what business had you to think, coming\r\ntrespassing here on my ground, and breaking the hedges!  I\xe2\x80\x99d have you up\r\nfor that, if for nothing else, you young vagabond!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t!  I\xe2\x80\x99m Henry Merrifield!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care if you\xe2\x80\x99re Henry Merry Andrew!\xe2\x80\x9d said Farmer Grice, who was a\r\nsurly man, and had a grudge of long standing against the Captain, for\r\nwithstanding him at the Board of Guardians.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have the law out of\r\nyou, whoever you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94Mamma is so very ill!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hal, bursting into tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more shame for you to be rampaging about the country this fashion,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the farmer, giving him a shake that seemed to make all his bones\r\nrattle in his skin.  \xe2\x80\x9cServe you right if I flogged you within an inch of\r\nyour life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I mean please do\xe2\x80\x94anything but have me up to the\r\nmagistrates!  I\xe2\x80\x99ll never do it again, never!\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Henry in his terror.\r\n\r\nMr. Grice had some pity, and also knew that his wife and all the\r\nneighbours would be shocked at his prosecuting so young a boy, whose\r\nparents were in such distress.  So he said, \xe2\x80\x9cThere, then, I\xe2\x80\x99ll overlook\r\nit this time, sir, so as I have the value of the bird.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is the value\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d asked Henry, trembling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cValue!  Why, the breed came from Norfolk; he was three years old; and my\r\nmissus set great store on him, he was as good as a house-dog, to keep\r\nidle children out of the yard; and it was quite a picture to see him\r\nposturing about, and setting up his tail!  Value! not less than\r\nfive-and-twenty shillings, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have not five-and-twenty shillings.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t get them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal,\r\nfalling back into misery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have thought of that before you shot poor old Tom Turkey!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nquoth Farmer Grice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what in the world shall I ever do?\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for you to settle, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the farmer, taking up the unlucky\r\ngun.  \xe2\x80\x9cI shall take this, and keep it out of further harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh pray, pray!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Henry.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not my gun; it is Mr. Greville\xe2\x80\x99s;\r\nplease let me have it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! was it those young dogs, the Master Grevilles, that were with\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Mr. Grice.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\xe2\x80\x99d known that, I\xe2\x80\x99d not have let you off so\r\neasy.  Those boys are the plague of the place; I wish it had been one of\r\nthem as I\xe2\x80\x99d caught, I\xe2\x80\x99d have had some satisfaction out of them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry entreated again for the gun, explaining that they had not leave to\r\ntake it; but the farmer was unrelenting.  He might go to them, he said,\r\nto make up the price of the poor turkey-cock; how they could have got the\r\ngun was no affair of his; have it they should not, till the money was\r\nbrought to him; and if it did not come before night, he should carry the\r\ngun up to the Park, and complain to Mr. Greville.\r\n\r\nWith this answer the unhappy Hal was released, and ran after his friends\r\nto tell them of the terms.  He found them sitting on a low wall, just\r\nwithin their own grounds, waiting to hear what had become of him.  When\r\nhe had told his story, they both set upon him for betraying them, and\r\ndeclared that they should send him to Coventry ever after, and never do\r\nanything with him again; but as it was plain that the gun must be\r\nredeemed, if they wished to avoid severe punishment, there was a\r\nconsultation.  Nobody had much money; but Osmond consolingly suspected\r\nthat the farmer would take less; five-and-twenty shillings was an\r\nexorbitant price to set on a turkey-cock\xe2\x80\x99s head, and perhaps half would\r\ncontent him.\r\n\r\nThe half, however, seemed as impossible as the whole.  Osmond had three\r\nshillings, Martin two, Hal fourpence!  What was to be done?  And the boys\r\ndeclared that if it should come to their father\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge, Hal, who had\r\ngiven up their names, should certainly not be shielded by them.  In fact,\r\nhe, who had done the deed, was the only one who ought to pay.\r\n\r\nThe sound of the servants\xe2\x80\x99 dinner-bell at the Park broke up the\r\nconsultation; the boys must not be missed at luncheon; and they therefore\r\nseparated, agreeing to meet at that same place at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock, to hear\r\nthe result of Hal\xe2\x80\x99s negotiation with the farmer; for neither of the\r\nGrevilles would hear of helping him to face the enemy.\r\n\r\nPoor Hal plodded home disconsolately.  Once he thought of telling Sam,\r\nand asking his help; but Sam would be so much shocked at such a scrape at\r\nsuch a time, as possibly to lick him for it before helping him.  Indeed\r\nHal did not see much chance of Sam being able to do anything for them;\r\nand he had too often boasted over his elder brother to like to abase\r\nhimself by such a confession\xe2\x80\x94when, too, it would almost be owning how\r\nmuch better it would have been to have followed Sam\xe2\x80\x99s advice and have\r\ngone safely to church.\r\n\r\nCould he borrow of any one?  Had he nothing of his own to sell or\r\nexchange?  Ah! if it had not been for that stupid hoard of little\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s, he might have had even so much!  By-the-bye, some of that\r\ncollection was his own.  He might quite lawfully take that back again.\r\nHow much could it be?  How much did he put in last week? the week before?\r\nOh, never mind; some of it was his at all events; there was no harm in\r\ntaking that.  Most likely he should be able to restore it four-fold when\r\nColonel Carey made his present; or, if not, nobody knew exactly what was\r\nin Toby Fillpot; and after all very likely they would forget all about\r\nit; people could not think about pigs when Mamma was ill; or, maybe, he\r\nshould go to join his ship, and hear no more of it.  So he came home, and\r\ncrossed the paddock just as the dinner-bell was ringing, opening the\r\nhall-door as the children were running across it to the dining-room.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook, who was walking behind them, turned as he came in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHenry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI have sent Johnnie to dine in the nursery, for his\r\ndisobedience in climbing the gate.  I certainly shall not give you a less\r\npunishment.  You must have led him into it; and how could you be so cruel\r\nas to leave the poor little fellow alone in such a dangerous place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStupid little coward! it was not a bit of danger!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo young a child\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s all your London notions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I climbed up our\r\ngate at Stonehouse, which was twice as high, when I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t near as old as\r\nthat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to argue with you, Henry; but after such an act of\r\ndisobedience, I cannot allow you to sit down to dinner with us.
