Go up to\r\nthe school-room, and Mary shall bring you your dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to dine with a lot of babies and governesses!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nexclaimed Henry, and bounced up-stairs, leaving Miss Fosbrook quite\r\nconfounded at such an outbreak of naughtiness.\r\n\r\nShe intended, as soon as dinner should be over, to go up to him, and try\r\nto lead him to be sorry for his conduct, and to think what a wretched\r\nmoment this was for such audacity; and then she feared that she ought to\r\npunish him farther, by keeping him in all the afternoon.  He was so soft\r\nand easily impressed, that she almost trusted to make him feel that it\r\nwould be right that he should suffer for his misconduct.\r\n\r\nWhen she went up-stairs, almost as soon as grace had been said, he was\r\ngone.  Nobody could find him, and calling produced no answer.  She became\r\nquite distressed and anxious, but could not go far from the house\r\nherself, nor send Sam, in case the message should arrive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cno doubt he\xe2\x80\x99s after something with the Grevilles, and\r\nwas afraid you would stop him in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe tried to believe this, but still felt far from satisfied all the\r\nafternoon, and was glad to see the boy come back in time for tea.\r\n\r\nHe said he had been with the Grevilles; he did not see why anybody need\r\nask him questions; he should do what he pleased without being called to\r\naccount.  Nobody told him not to run away after dinner; he was not going\r\nto stay to be ordered about for nothing.\r\n\r\nThis was so bad a temper, that Christabel could not bear to try to touch\r\nhim with the thought of his sick mother.  She knew that softening must\r\ncome in time, and believed the best thing to do at the moment would be to\r\nput a stop to his disrespectful speeches to her, and his cross ones to\r\nhis brothers and sisters, by sending him to bed as soon as tea was over,\r\nas the completion of his punishment.  He did not struggle, for she had\r\ntaught him to mind her; but he went up-stairs with a gloomy brow, and\r\nangry murmurs that it was very hard to be put under a stupid woman, who\r\nknew nothing about anything, and was always cross.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\n\r\n\r\nSATURDAY\xe2\x80\x99S post brought a letter, and a comfortable one.  All Thursday\r\nMrs. Merrifield had been in so doubtful a state, that her husband could\r\nnot bear to write, lest he should fill the children with false hopes, or\r\nalarm them still more; but she had had a good night, was stronger on\r\nFriday, and when the post went out, the doctors had just ventured to say\r\nthey believed she would recover favourably.  The letter was finished off\r\nin a great hurry; but Captain Merrifield did not forget to thank his\r\nlittle Susan warmly for her poor scrambling letter, and say he knew all\r\nshe meant by it, bidding her give Miss Fosbrook his hearty thanks for\r\nforwarding it, and for telling him the children were all behaving well,\r\nand feeling properly.  His love to them all; they must try to deserve the\r\ngreat mercy that had been granted to them.\r\n\r\nTo the children, this was almost as good as saying that their mother was\r\nwell again; but there was too much awe about them for their joy to show\r\nitself noisily.  Susan ran away to her own room, and Bessie followed her;\r\nand Sam said no word, only Miss Fosbrook remarked that he did not eat two\r\nmouthfuls of breakfast.  She would not take any notice; she knew his\r\nheart was full; and when she looked round on that little flock, and\r\nthought of the grievous sorrow scarcely yet averted from them, she could\r\nhardly keep the tears from blinding her.  They were all somewhat still\r\nand grave, and it was too happy a morning to be broken into by the\r\nreproofs that Henry deserved, even more richly than Christabel knew.  She\r\nhad almost forgotten his bad behaviour; and when she remembered something\r\nof it, she could not but hope that silence, on such a day as this, might\r\nbring it home to him more than rebuke.  Yet when breakfast was ever, he\r\nwas among the loudest of those who, shaking off the strange, awed gravity\r\nof deep gladness, went rushing together into the garden, feeling that\r\nthey might give way to their spirits again.\r\n\r\nSam shouted and whooped as if he were casting off a burthen, and picking\r\nlittle George up in his arms, tossed him and swung him round in the air\r\nin an ecstasy; while John and Annie and David went down on the grass\r\ntogether, and tumbled and rolled one over the other like three kittens,\r\ntheir legs and arms kicking about, so that it was hard to tell whose\r\nproperty were the black shoes that came wriggling into view.\r\n\r\nSusan was quieter.  She told Nurse the good news, and then laid hold upon\r\nBaby, and carried her off into the passage to hug all to herself.  She\r\ncould tell no one but Baby how very happy she was, and how her heart had\r\ntrembled at her mother\xe2\x80\x99s suffering, her father\xe2\x80\x99s grief, and at the\r\ndesolateness that had so nearly come on them.  Oh, she was very happy,\r\nvery thankful; but she could not scream it out like the others, Baby must\r\nhave it all in kisses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristabel,\xe2\x80\x9d said a little voice, when all the others were gone, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall never be pipy again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must try to fight against it, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, coming close up to her, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen dear Mamma was\r\nso ill, it did seem so silly to mind about not having pretty things like\r\nIda, and the boys plaguing, and so on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my dear; a real trouble makes us ashamed of our little\r\ndiscontents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said so many times yesterday, and the day before, that I would never\r\nmind things again, if only Mamma would get well and come home,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlittle girl; \xe2\x80\x9cand I never shall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not always find it easy not to mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel; \xe2\x80\x9cbut if\r\nyou try hard, you will learn how to keep from showing that you mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, (and a great mouthful of an oh! it was,) \xe2\x80\x9cthose\r\nthings are grown so silly and little now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen them in their true light for once, my dear.  And now that\r\nyou have so great cause of thankfulness to God, you feel that your\r\nfoolish frets and discontents were unthankful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, her eyes cast down, as they always were when anything\r\nof this kind was said to her, as if she did not like to meet the look\r\nfixed on her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, Bessie, try to make the giving up of these murmurs your\r\nthank-offering to God.  Suppose every day when you say your prayers, you\r\nwere to add something like this\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and she wrote down on a little bit of\r\npaper, \xe2\x80\x9cO Thou, who hast raised up my mother from her sickness, teach me\r\nto be a thankful and contented child, and to guard my words and thoughts\r\nfrom peevishness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it too small to pray about?\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing is too small to pray about, my dear.  Do you think this little\r\nmidge is too small for God to have made it, and given it life, and spread\r\nthat mother-of-pearl light on its wings?  Do you think yourself too small\r\nto pray? or your fault too small to pray about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth cast down her eyes.  She did not quite think it was a fault,\r\nbut she did not say so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBessie, what was the great sin of the Israelites in the wilderness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe colour on her cheek showed that she knew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey tempted God by murmurs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel.  \xe2\x80\x9cThey tried His patience\r\nby grumbling, when His care and blessings were all round them, and by\r\ncrying out because all was not just as they liked.  Now, dear Bessie, God\r\nhas shown you what a real sorrow might be; will it not be tempting Him to\r\ngo back to complaints over what He has ordained for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall net complain now; I shall not care,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.  But she\r\ntook the little bit of paper, and Christabel trusted that she would make\r\nuse of it, knowing that in this lay her hope of cure; for whatever she\r\nmight think in this first joy of relief, her little troubles were sure to\r\nseem quite as unbearable while they were upon her as if she had never\r\nfeared a great one.\r\n\r\nHowever, nothing remarkable happened; everyone was bright and happy; but\r\nstill the influence of their past alarm subdued them enough to make them\r\nquiet and well-behaved, both on Saturday and Sunday; and Miss Fosbrook\r\nhad never had so little trouble with them.\r\n\r\nIn consideration of this, and of the agitation and unsettled state that\r\nhad put the last week out of all common rules, she announced on Monday\r\nmorning that she would excuse all the fines, and that all the children\r\nshould have their allowance unbroken.  Maybe she was moved to this by the\r\nsuspicion that these four sixpences and three threepennies would make up\r\nthe fund to the price of a \xe2\x80\x9creasonable pig;\xe2\x80\x9d and she thought it time that\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s perseverance should be rewarded, and room made in his mind for\r\nsomething beyond swine and halfpence.\r\n\r\nHer announcement was greeted by the girls with eager thanks, by the boys\r\nwith a tremendous \xe2\x80\x9cThree times three for Miss Fosbrook!\xe2\x80\x9d and Bessie was\r\nso joyous, that instead of crying out against the noise, she joined in\r\nwith Susan and Annie; but they made such a ridiculous little squeaking,\r\nthat Sam laughed at them, and took to mocking their queer thin hurrahs.\r\nYet even this Elizabeth could bear!\r\n\r\nDavid was meanwhile standing by the locker, his fingers at work as if he\r\nwere playing a tune, his lips counting away, \xe2\x80\x9cNinety-two, ninety-three,\r\nninety-four\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s me; ninety-five, ninety-six, ninety-seven\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nJack,\xe2\x80\x9d and so on; till having plodded up diligently, he turned round with\r\na little scream, \xe2\x80\x9cOne hundred and twenty!  That\xe2\x80\x99s the pig!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne hundred and twenty pence.  Sukey said one hundred and twenty pence\r\nwere ten shillings.  That will do it!  That\xe2\x80\x99s the pig!  Oh, we\xe2\x80\x99ve done\r\nit!  May I take it to Purday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was to be let alone till fair-day, you little bother!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no,\xe2\x80\x9d cried many voices; \xe2\x80\x9conly till we had enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am sure nobody knows if we have,\xe2\x80\x9d added Hal hotly.  \xe2\x80\x9cA lot of\r\nhalfpence, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I know, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted David.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere are eighty-nine pence and one\r\nfarthing in Toby Fillpot, and this makes one hundred and twenty-two pence\r\nand one farthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d no business to peep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t peep,\xe2\x80\x9d said David indignantly.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere were forty-eight pence\r\nat first, and then Susie had three, that was fifty-one\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d  And he would\r\nhave gone on like a little calculating machine, with the entire reckoning\r\nin his head, if the others had had patience to hear; but Annie and\r\nJohnnie were urgent to have the sum counted out before their eyes.  Hal\r\nroughly declared it was against the rules, and little inquisitives must\r\nnot have their way.  But others were also inquisitive; and Sam said it\r\nwould be best to know how much they had, that Purday might be told to\r\nlook out for a pig at the price; besides, he wanted to have it over; it\r\nwas such a bore not to have any money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not fair!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Henry passionately.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t keep the rules!\r\nYou sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t have my sixpence, I can tell you; and I won\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t stay\r\nand see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody wants you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know there were any rules,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girls; but Hal was already\r\noff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHal has only put in fivepence-halfpenny,\xe2\x80\x9d said David, \xe2\x80\x9cso no wonder he\r\nis ashamed.  Such a big boy, with sixpence a week!  But if he won\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nus have his sixpence now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, we will make it up next week,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, then, who will take Toby down?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, unbuttoning one\r\nglass door, and undoing the two bolts of the second, behind which the cup\r\nof money stood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusie ought, because she is the eldest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavie ought, because he is the youngest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid stood on a chair to take Toby off his shelf.  Solemn was the face\r\nwith which the little boy lifted the mug by the handle, putting his other\r\nhand to steady the expected weight of coppers; but there was at once a\r\nfrown, a little cry of horror.  Toby came up so light in his hand, that\r\nall his great effort was thrown away, and only made him stagger back in\r\ndismay, falling backward from the chair, and poor Toby crashing to pieces\r\non the floor as he fell, while out rolled\xe2\x80\x94one solitary farthing!\r\n\r\nNobody spoke for some moments; but all stood perfectly still, staring as\r\nhard as if they hoped the pence would be brought out by force of looking\r\nfor them.\r\n\r\nThen David\xe2\x80\x99s knuckles went up into his eyes, and he burst forth in a loud\r\nbellow.  It was the first time Miss Fosbrook had heard him cry, and she\r\nfeared that he had been hurt by the fall, or cut by the broken crockery;\r\nbut he struck out with foot and fist, as if his tears were as much anger\r\nas grief, and roared out, \xe2\x80\x9cI want the halfpence for my pig.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cif you have hid them for a trick, let him have\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I play tricks _now_?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sam in indignation.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen perhaps Hal has,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Bessie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, half in self-defence, half in fright,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat one of you must have been at the baby-house, for I found the doors\r\nopen, and shut them up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why should it be one of us?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Sam; while David stopped\r\ncrying, and listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause none of the younger ones can reach to undo the doors,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nElizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt was as much as I could do to reach the upper bolt, though\r\nI stood upon a chair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was evident; for the baby-house was really an old-fashioned bureau,\r\nand below the glass doors there was a projecting slope of polished\r\nwalnut, upon which only a fly could stand, and which was always locked.\r\nNo one whose years were less than half a score was tall enough to get a\r\ngood hold of the button, even from the highest chair, far less to jerk\r\ndown the rather stiff upper bolt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt cannot have been a little one, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nyou should not be so ready to accuse your brothers, Bessie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid, however, had laid hold of a hope, and getting up from the floor,\r\nhastened out of the room, followed by John; and they were presently heard\r\nshouting \xe2\x80\x9cHal!\xe2\x80\x9d all over the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat day was it that you found the door open, Bessie?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss\r\nFosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was just after dinner,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth, recollecting herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was on Friday.  Yes, I remember it was Friday, because I went into\r\nthe school-room to get my pencil, and I was afraid Hal would jump out\r\nupon me, and looked in first to see whether he was going to be tiresome;\r\nbut he was gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cit was the day we found poor Jack stuck up on the\r\ngate, when he and Hal were in disgrace.  Oh, he never would have played\r\ntricks then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you go up before me, Bessie?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I went up\r\ndirectly after dinner to speak to Henry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I did,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought if you got in first, you would be\r\nscolding him ever so long, and would let nobody in, so I would get my\r\npencil first; and I slipped up before you had left the table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJust then the two boys were heard stumping up the stairs, and ran in,\r\npanting with haste and excitement, David with a fiery red ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; Hal didn\xe2\x80\x99t hide it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he boxed Davie\xe2\x80\x99s ear for thinking he did,\xe2\x80\x9d added John; \xe2\x80\x9cand said\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d do the same for spiteful Bet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he never played tricks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d reiterated David; \xe2\x80\x9cand he said I\xe2\x80\x99d no business to ask; and if Bet\r\nwent prying about everywhere, I\xe2\x80\x99d better ask her.  Have you got it,\r\nBetty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Elizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cHow can you, Davie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have got a secret,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed David; \xe2\x80\x9cand you always were cross\r\nabout Hannah Higgins\xe2\x80\x99s pig.  You have got it to tease me!  Miss Fosbrook,\r\nmake her give it back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, David,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cBessie is quite to be trusted;\r\nand it is wrong to make unfounded accusations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, Betty,\xe2\x80\x9d added Sam kindly; \xe2\x80\x9cif Davie wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a little donkey,\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say such things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Henry?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the governess.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did he not come himself?\r\nCall him; I want to know if he observed this door being open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is gone down to Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is high time you were there too, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook,\r\nstarting.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you are late, beg Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s pardon from me, and tell him\r\nthat I kept you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam was obliged to run off at full speed; and the other children stood\r\nabout, still aghast and excited.  Miss Fosbrook, however, told them to\r\ntake out their books.  She would not do anything more till she had had\r\ntime to think, and had composed their minds and her own; for she was\r\nexceedingly shocked, and felt herself partly in fault, for having left\r\nthe hoard in an unlocked cupboard.  She feared to do anything hastily,\r\nlest she should bring suspicion on the innocent; and she thought all\r\nwould do better if time were given for settling down.  All were\r\ndisappointed at thus losing the excitement, fancying perhaps that instant\r\nsearch and inquiry would hunt up the money; and David put himself quite\r\ninto a sullen fit.  No, he would not turn round, nor read, nor do\r\nanything, unless Miss Fosbrook would make stingy Bet give up the pence.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook and Susan both tried to argue with him; but he had set his\r\nmind upon one point so vehemently, that it was making him absolutely\r\nstupid to everything else; and he was such a little boy (only five years\r\nold), that his mind could hardly grasp the exceeding unlikelihood of a\r\ngirl like Elizabeth committing such a theft, either in sport or earnest,\r\nnor understand the injury of such a suspicion.  He only knew that she had\r\na secret\xe2\x80\x94a counter secret to his pig; and when she hotly assured him that\r\nshe had never touched the money, and Susan backed her up with, \xe2\x80\x9cThere,\r\nshe says she did not,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cShe once told a story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nElizabeth coloured deep red, and Susan cried out loudly that it was a\r\nshame in David; then explained that it was a long long time ago, that Hal\r\nand Bessie broke the drawing-room window by playing at ball with little\r\nhard apples, and had not told, but when questioned had said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo;\xe2\x80\x9d but\r\nindeed they had been so sorry then that she knew they would never do so\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nAgain David showed that he could not enter into this, and sulkily\r\nrepeated, \xe2\x80\x9cShe told a story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will have no more of this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel resolutely.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou are all\r\nworking yourselves up into a bad spirit: and not another word will I hear\r\non this matter till lessons are over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat tone was always obeyed; but lessons did not prosper; the children\r\nwere all restless and unsettled; and David, hitherto for his age her best\r\nscholar, took no pains, and seemed absolutely stupefied.  What did he\r\ncare for fines, if the chance of the pig was gone?  And he was sullenly\r\nangry with Miss Fosbrook for using no measures to recover the money,\r\nfancied she did not care, and remembered the foolish nursery talk about\r\nher favouring Bessie.\r\n\r\nOnce Miss Fosbrook heard a little gasping from the corner, and looking\r\nround, saw poor Bessie crying quietly over her slate, and trying hard to\r\ncheck herself.  She would not have noticed her, though longing to comfort\r\nher, if David had not cried out, \xe2\x80\x9cBet is crying!  A fine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cbut a fine for an ill-natured speech that has\r\nmade her cry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has got the pig\xe2\x80\x99s money,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay that again, and I shall punish you, David.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked her full in the face, and said it again.\r\n\r\nShe was thoroughly roused to anger, and kept her word by opening the door\r\nof a small dark closet, and putting David in till dinner-time.\r\n\r\nThen she and Susan both tried to soothe Bessie, by reminding her how\r\nchildish David was, how he had caught up some word that probably Hal had\r\nflung out without meaning it, and how no one of any sense suspected her\r\nfor a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is so ill-natured and hard,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cTo think I could steal!\r\nI think they hate me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cif you only would never be cross to the boys, Bessie,\r\nand not keep out of what they care for, they would never think it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Susie is right there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you try to be one with\r\nthe others, and make common cause with them, giving up and forbearing,\r\nthey never will take such things into their heads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd _we_ don\xe2\x80\x99t now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan cheerily.  \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you hear Sam say\r\nnobody but a donkey could think it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Bessie has a secret!\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\nAgain stout Susan said, \xe2\x80\x9cFor shame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what my secret is,\xe2\x80\x9d began Bessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t tell it, dear!  We\xe2\x80\x99ll trust you without; and Sam\r\nwill say the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie flung her arms round Susan\xe2\x80\x99s neck, as if she only now knew the\r\ncomfort of her dear good sister.\r\n\r\nLessons were resumed; and as soon as these were done, Miss Fosbrook\r\nresolved on a thorough search.  Some strange fit of mischief or curiosity\r\nmight have actuated some one, and the money be hidden away; so she\r\nbrought David out of his cupboard, and with Susan\xe2\x80\x99s help turned out every\r\ndrawer and locker in the school-room, forbidding the others to touch or\r\nassist.  They routed out queer nests of broken curiosities, disturbed old\r\ndusty dens of rubbish, peeped behind every row of books; but made no\r\ndiscovery worth mentioning, except the left leg of Annie\xe2\x80\x99s last doll, the\r\nstuffing of Johnnie\xe2\x80\x99s ball, the tiger out of George\xe2\x80\x99s Noah\xe2\x80\x99s ark, and the\r\nfirst sheet of Sam\xe2\x80\x99s Latin Grammar, all stuffed together into a\r\nmouse-hole in the skirting.\r\n\r\nAt dinner Christabel forbade the subject to be mentioned, not only to\r\nhinder quarrelsome speeches, but to prevent the loss being talked of\r\namong the servants; since she feared that one of them must have committed\r\nthe theft, and though anxious not to put it into the children\xe2\x80\x99s heads,\r\nsuspected Rhoda, the little nursery-girl, who was quite a child, and had\r\nnot long been in the house.\r\n\r\nHenry ate his dinner in haste, but could not get away till Miss Fosbrook\r\nhad called him away from the rest, and told him that if he had been\r\nplaying a trick on his little brother, it was time to put an end to it,\r\nbefore any innocent person fell under suspicion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve been playing no tricks\xe2\x80\x94at least\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout any _at least_, Henry, have you hidden the money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou dined in the school-room on Friday.  Were the baby-house doors open\r\nthen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t open them to take anything out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat should I want with the things in the baby-house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you, or did you not!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94at least\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn one word, did you open them? yes or no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time did you go out after eating your dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother! how is one to remember!  It\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense making such a fuss.\r\nThe children fancied they put in ever so much more than they did, and\r\nvery likely took out some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; David\xe2\x80\x99s reckoning was accurate.  I wrote down all I knew of; and I\r\nam sure none was taken out, for early that very morning I had put in a\r\nsixpence myself, and the cup was then full of coppers, with that little\r\nsilver threepenny of David\xe2\x80\x99s with the edge turned up upon the top.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you must have left the door undone!\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry delighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare not be positive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I believe I remember\r\nbolting it; and if I had not done so, it would have flown open sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but the wind, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the doors did open, it would not account for the loss of the money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry ungraciously, trying to move off: but\r\nshe first required him to tell her what he had said to the younger boys\r\nto make them suspect Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I?\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I didn\xe2\x80\x99t; at least, if I did, I only said\r\nBess peeped everywhere, and was very close.  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t suspect her, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think not!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook indignantly.  \xe2\x80\x9cNow please to\r\ncome up with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to go out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry.\r\n\r\nNo, she would not let him go.  She thought Elizabeth ought to clear\r\nherself, so far as it could be done, by making her secret known, since\r\nthat had drawn suspicion on her; and when all the children were together,\r\nshe called the little girl and told her so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very unkind of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, with trembling lip; \xe2\x80\x9cbut they\r\nshall see, if they want _that_ to show I am not a thief!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou knows Bessie, I trust you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for people\xe2\x80\x99s secrets.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\npry into Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one followed their example; all either really suspected, or else were\r\nfull of curiosity, and delighted to gratify it.\r\n\r\nHalf a dozen slips of card, with poor little coloured drawings on them,\r\nand as many lengths of penny ribbon!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all?\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie, much disappointed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that\xe2\x80\x99s what Bet made such a fuss about,\xe2\x80\x9d said John; and David\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nfell, as if he had really expected to see the lost pence.\r\n\r\nThe next thing, after the search had been made through all the children\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbed-rooms, was to go to the nursery: and thither Miss Fosbrook allowed\r\nonly Susan and Sam to follow her.  Nurse Freeman was very stiff and\r\nstately, but she had no objection to searching; and the boy and girl\r\nbegan the hunt, while Miss Fosbrook meantime cautiously asked whether\r\nNurse were sure of Rhoda, and if she were trustworthy.\r\n\r\nThis made Mrs. Freeman very angry; and though her words were respectful,\r\nshe showed that she was much offended at the strange lady presuming to\r\nsuspect anyone, especially one under her charge.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook wanted to have asked Rhoda whether the doors were open or\r\nshut when she carried Henry his dinner, but Nurse would not consent to\r\ncall her.  \xe2\x80\x9cI understood the nursery and the girl were to be my\r\nprovince,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf Miss Merrifield heard her mamma say otherwise,\r\nthen it is a different thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan cowered into the dark cupboard.  Nurse must be in a dreadful way to\r\ncall her Miss Merrifield, instead of Missy!\r\n\r\nNothing more could be done.  The pence could not be found.  Nurse would\r\nnot let Rhoda be examined; and all that could be found out from the\r\nchildren had been already elicited.\r\n\r\nChristabel could only beg that no more should be said, and, her head\r\naching with perplexity, hope that some light might yet be thrown on the\r\nmatter.  There must be pain and grief whenever it should be explained;\r\nbut this would be far better, even for the offender, than the present\r\ndeception: and the whole family were in a state of irritation and\r\ndistrust, that hurt their tempers, and made her bitterly reproach herself\r\nwith not having prevented temptation by putting the hoard under lock and\r\nkey.\r\n\r\nShe ordered that no more should be said about it that evening, and made\r\nherself obeyed; but play was dull, and everything went off heavily.  The\r\nnext morning, Susan came back early from her housekeeping business, with\r\nher honest face grave and unhappy, and finding Miss Fosbrook alone, told\r\nher she had something _really_ to say to her if she might; and this being\r\ngranted began, with the bright look of having found a capital notion:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I wish you would do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you would call every one in all the house, and ask them on their word\r\nand honour if they took the pence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, I am not the head of the house, and I have no right to do that;\r\nbesides, I do not believe it would discover it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! could a thief get in from out of doors!\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan looking at the\r\nwindow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly that, my dear; but I am afraid a person who could steal would not\r\nscruple to tell a falsehood, and I do not wish to cause this additional\r\nsin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very horrid; I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, puckering up her face\r\nfor tears.  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know, Miss Fosbrook, the maids are all so angry that\r\nyou said anything about Rhoda?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not mention it, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; nor Sam.  It was Nurse herself!  But they all say that you want\r\nto take away her character; and they won\xe2\x80\x99t have strangers put over them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, Susie; don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me this.  It can do no good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but _please_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then Mary\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t think how she\r\ncould\xe2\x80\x94but she said that poor dear Bessie was always sly, and that she had\r\nbeen at the cupboard, and had got the pence; but she was your favourite,\r\nand so you vindicated her.  And Nurse began teasing her to confess, and\r\ntell the truth, and told her she was a wicked child because she would\r\nnot; but it was all because we were put under strangers!  I\xe2\x80\x99m sure they\r\ndo set on Johnnie and Davie to be cross to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen was this, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast night, when we went to the nursery to be washed.  It was our night,\r\nyou know.  Oh!  I wish Mamma was well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I do my dear.  And how did poor Bessie bear it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe got quite white, and never said a word, even when they told her she\r\nwas sulky.  But when we got into bed, and I kissed her and cuddled her\r\nup, oh! she did cry so; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do.  So, do you know, I got\r\nmy shawl on, and went and called Sam; and he was not gone to sleep, and\r\nhe came and sat by her, and told her that he believed her, and knew she\r\nwas as sound a heart of oak as any of us; and we both petted her, and Sam\r\nwas so nice and kind, till she went to sleep.  Then he went to the\r\nnursery, and told Nurse how horrid it was in her; but Cook said it only\r\nmade her worse, because she is jealous of our taking part with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, I _do_ like to hear of your kindness to Bessie; but I wish you\r\nwould not mind what any of the maids say, nor talk to them about it.  It\r\nonly distresses you for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou could not help this attack in the nursery, but you need not talk to\r\nCook or Mary about it.  It is of no use to vex ourselves with what people\r\nsay who don\xe2\x80\x99t know half a story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you tell them not?\xe2\x80\x9d said simple Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I cannot interfere.  They would only do it the more.  We can only\r\nkeep Bessie as much out of the way of the maids as we can, and show our\r\nconfidence in her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCertainly Elizabeth had been known to look infinitely more glum when\r\nnothing was the matter than under all this vexation, even though the\r\nservants were really very unkind to her; and her two little brothers both\r\nbehaved as ill as possible to her whenever they had the opportunity\xe2\x80\x94David\r\nreally believing that she had made away with the money, and ought to be\r\ntortured for it; and Johnnie taking it on his word, and being one of\r\nthose little boys who have a positive taste for ill-nature, and think it\r\nfun.  They pinched her, they bit her, they rubbed out her sums, they shut\r\nup her lesson-books and lost her place, they put bitten crusts into her\r\nplate, and did whatever they knew she most disliked, whenever Miss\r\nFosbrook or Sam was not in the way; but she never told.  She did not\r\nchoose to be called a tell-tale; and besides, they really did not succeed\r\nin making her life miserable, so much was she pleased with the real\r\nkindness her trouble had brought out from Susan and Sam.  Susan could not\r\nprevent the persecution of the two naughty little boys, but she defended\r\nher sister to her utmost; and Sam cuffed them if they said a word or\r\nlifted a finger against Bessie before him; and he gave her such notice\r\nand kindness as she never had received from him before.  One afternoon,\r\nwhen he was going to walk to Bonchamp, he asked leave for her to come\r\nwith him, and would take nobody else; and hot day as it was, Bessie had\r\nnever had such a charming walk.  She kept herself from making one single\r\nfuss; and in return, he gathered wild strawberries for her, showed her a\r\nkingfisher, and took her to look in at a very grand aquarium in the\r\nfishing-tackle maker\xe2\x80\x99s window, where she saw some gold-fish, and a most\r\ncomical little newt.  And going home, they had a real good talk about\r\ntheir father\xe2\x80\x99s voyage, and how they should get on without him; and Bessie\r\nfound to her great pleasure, that Sam hoped Miss Fosbrook would stay when\r\nMamma Came home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor I do think she has put some sense into you, Bessie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\nShe was so delighted, that instead of preparing to fret if Sam did but\r\nhold up a finger at her, she looked up with a smile when he came in her\r\nway, sure of protection, and expecting something pleasant, as well as\r\nthinking it an honour to be asked to help him in anything.  The next day,\r\nwhen Mr. Carey had insisted on his verifying by the map all the towns\r\nwhich he had been contented to say were in Asia Minor (where every place\r\nin ancient history is always put if its whereabouts be doubtful), she\r\nsaved him so much time and trouble, that he got out into the garden full\r\nhalf an hour earlier than he would otherwise have done.  Thereupon he\r\ntold her she was a jolly good fellow, and gave her such a thump on the\r\nback, as a few weeks ago would have made her scream and whine; but this\r\ntime she took it as a new form of thanks, and felt highly honoured by\r\nbeing invited to help him to fish for minnows, though it almost made her\r\nsick to stick the raw meat upon his hooks.\r\n\r\nThe threatening of a true sorrow, the bearing a real trouble, and the\r\nopening to her brother\xe2\x80\x99s kindness, had done far more to make her a happy\r\nlittle girl than all Miss Fosbrook\xe2\x80\x99s attempts to satisfy her cravings or\r\nplease her tastes.  These had indeed done her some good, and taught her\r\nto find means of enjoyment for those likings that no one else cared for;\r\nbut it had been the _spirit_ of delight that had been chiefly wanting;\r\nand when thankfulness and love were leading her to that, it was much\r\neasier to see that the evening clouds or the rising moon were lovely,\r\nthan when she was looking out for affronts.\r\n\r\nNothing was said in public about the loss; and Christabel hoped that the\r\nbad impression as to Elizabeth would wear out in the young minds of the\r\nlesser children; but David\xe2\x80\x99s whole nature seemed to have been\r\ndisorganized by the disappointment.  Instead of being a pattern child for\r\ndiligence and good behaviour, very fond of Miss Fosbrook, and not only\r\ninoffensive, but often keeping John and Anne in order, he seemed\r\nabsolutely stupid and senseless at lessons, became stubborn at reproof,\r\nseemed to take pleasure in running counter to his governess, and rendered\r\nthe other two, who, though his elders, were both of weak natures compared\r\nwith his own, more openly naughty than himself.  Sometimes it seemed to\r\nChristabel that the habit of spiting Bessie was getting so confirmed,\r\nthat it would last even when the cause was forgotten; and yet the more\r\nshe strove to put it down in sight, the more it throve out of sight; and\r\nwhen she looked at David, and thought how she had once admired him, she\r\ncould not but remember the text that says, \xe2\x80\x9cThy goodness is as the\r\nmorning cloud, and as the dew shall it vanish away.\xe2\x80\x9d  She had thought it\r\ngoodness based upon religious feeling, as well as on natural gravity and\r\norderliness; and so perhaps it had once been, but the little fellow had\r\nfixed his whole soul on one purpose, and though that was a good one, it\r\nhad grown into an idol, and swallowed up all his other motives, till of\r\nlate he had only been good for the sake of the pig, not because it was\r\nright.  Being disappointed of the pig, he had nothing to fall back upon,\r\nbut felt himself so ill-used, that it seemed to him that it was no use to\r\nbe good; and he revenged himself by naughtiness.\r\n\r\nSuch sturdy strong characters as little David\xe2\x80\x99s, when they are once set\r\non the right object, come to the very best kind of goodness; but when\r\nthey take a wrong turn, they are the very worst, both for themselves and\r\nothers.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE Monday after the loss of the pence was a pouring wet day.  The whole\r\ncourt was like a flood, and the drops went splashing up again as if in\r\nplay; Purday wore his master\xe2\x80\x99s old southwester coat, and looked shiny all\r\nover; and when the maids had to cross the court, they went click, click,\r\nin their pattens under their umbrellas.\r\n\r\nBut it was baking day, and Susan and Annie had been down to coax the cook\r\ninto making them a present of a handsome allowance of dough, and Miss\r\nFosbrook into letting them manipulate it in the school-room.  Probably\r\nthis was the only way of preventing the dough from being turned into\r\nbullets, and sent flying at each other\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, or possibly plastered on\r\nsomebody\xe2\x80\x99s nose, and the cook and kitchenmaid from being nearly driven\r\ncrazy.\r\n\r\nThe dough was justly divided, and an establishment set up in each locker.\r\nBessie declined altogether; Sam had lent her his beautiful book of _The\r\nBritish Songsters_, and she was hard at work at the table copying a\r\ntom-tit, since she no longer carried on the work in secret; but at one\r\nlocker were the other three elders, at the other the three lesser ones,\r\nand little George in a corner by Susan, pegging away at his own private\r\nlump, and constantly begging for more.  Susan\xe2\x80\x99s ambition was to make a\r\nset of real twists, just like Cook\xe2\x80\x99s; and she pulled out and twisted and\r\nplaited, though often robbed of her dough by the two boys, whose united\r\nefforts were endeavouring to produce a likeness of Purday, with his hat\r\non his head, plums for eyes, a pipe in his mouth, and driving a cow; but\r\nunluckily his neck always got pinched off, and his arms would not stay\r\non!  No matter; the more moulding of that soft dough the better!  Johnnie\r\nand Annie had a whole party of white clammy serpents, always being set to\r\nbite one another, and to melt into each other; and David was hard at work\r\non a brood of rabbits with currant eyes, and would let no one interfere\r\nwith him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I hear something!\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bessie, looking up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s only the roller,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cPurday always rolls on a wet day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething, however, made the whole party of little bakers hold up their\r\nheads to listen.  There was a gleam on their faces, as a quick alert step\r\nsounded on the stairs, and Bessie, the nearest to the door, and not\r\ncramped like the rest, who were sitting on their heels, sprang forward\r\nand opened it with a scream of joy.\r\n\r\nThere he was\xe2\x80\x94the light, alert, weather-beaten man, with his loose wavy\r\nhair, and bright sailor face!  There was Papa!  Oh, the hurly-burly of\r\nchildren, tumbling up as well as they could on legs crooked under them,\r\nand holding out great fans of floury doughy paws, all coming to be hugged\r\nin his arms in turn, so that before he had come to the end of the eight\r\nin presence, Bessie had had time to whisk off to the nursery, snatch Baby\r\nup from before Nurse\xe2\x80\x99s astonished eyes, rush down with her, and put her\r\ninto his arms.  Baby had forgotten him, and was taken with such a fit of\r\nscreaming shyness, that Susan had to take her, and Annie to play bo-peep\r\nwith her, before she would let anyone\xe2\x80\x99s voice be heard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve taken you by surprise, Miss Fosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, shaking\r\nhands with her in the midst of the clatter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is such a pleasure!\xe2\x80\x9d she began.  \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you left Mrs. Merrifield\r\nmuch better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch better, much better, thank you.  I hope to find her on the sofa\r\nwhen I go back on Thursday.  I could only run down for a few days, just\r\nto settle things, and see the children, before I join the _Ramilies_.\r\nAdmiral Penrose very good-naturedly kept it open for me, till we could\r\ntell how _she_ was,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, with rather a trembling voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are going!  O Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, looking up at him; \xe2\x80\x9cand Baby\r\nwill not know you till\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold your tongue, Miss Croaker,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, roughly but kindly;\r\nand Miss Fosbrook could see that he was as much afraid of crying himself\r\nas of letting Susan cry; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve no time for that.  I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a gentleman on\r\nbusiness down stairs, and your Uncle John and I must go down to them\r\nagain.  We sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t want dinner; only, Sue, tell them to send in some eggs\r\nand bacon, or cold meat, or whatever there may be, for tea; and get a\r\nroom ready for your uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe would have gone, but Susan called out, \xe2\x80\x9cO Papa, may we drink tea with\r\nyou, Georgy and all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, to be sure, if you won\xe2\x80\x99t make a bear-fight, any of you, for your\r\nuncle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMayn\xe2\x80\x99t I come down with you?\xe2\x80\x9d added Sam, looking at him as if he wanted\r\nto make the most of every moment of that presence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter not, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got law business to settle,\r\nand we don\xe2\x80\x99t want you.  Better stay and make yourselves decent for\r\ntea-time.  Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s love, and she hopes you\xe2\x80\x99ll not drive Uncle John\r\ndistracted.\xe2\x80\x9d  And he was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother Uncle John!\xe2\x80\x9d first muttered Sam (I am sorry to say).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t think what he\xe2\x80\x99s come for,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo spoil our fun,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Johnnie disconsolately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take Sam to school,\xe2\x80\x9d added Hal, \xe2\x80\x9cwhile I go to sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know that you are going,\xe2\x80\x9d said Elizabeth.  \xe2\x80\x9cPapa said nothing\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! but I know I shall.  Admiral Penrose promised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know a great many things that don\xe2\x80\x99t happen.  You knew Colonel Carey\r\nwould give you two sovereigns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry looked as if he could bite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I shall finish Purday,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, turning away with a sigh; \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nthey shall have him for tea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTea will be no fun!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Annie.  \xe2\x80\x9cOh dear! what does Uncle John\r\ncome here for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay not he come to be with his brother?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Christabel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! but they are grown up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t he have him in London, without coming here to worry us in our\r\nlittle time!\xe2\x80\x9d added Johnnie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he will not worry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d they all cried, and stopped short.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe plagues about manners,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wanted Susie and me to be sent to school!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said it was like dining with young Hottentots.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe told Papa it was disgraceful, when we had all been sliding on the\r\ngreat pond in the village,\xe2\x80\x9d added Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he gave Sam a box on the ear, for only just taking a dear little\r\nriver cray-fish in his fishing-net to show Aunt Alice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe net was dripping wet,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Bessie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne; \xe2\x80\x9cbut Aunt Alice is so finikin and fidgety; she never\r\nwets her feet, and can\xe2\x80\x99t get over a stile, and is afraid of a cow; and he\r\nwants us all to be like her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he makes Papa and Mamma mind things that they don\xe2\x80\x99t mind by nature,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma always tells us to be good, and never play at hockey in the house\r\nwhen he\xe2\x80\x99s there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Anne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has not told us so this time,\xe2\x80\x9d said John triumphantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but we must mind all the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; and Sam silenced some\r\nindependent murmurs, about not minding Uncle John, by saying it was\r\nminding Mamma.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook herself was a little alarmed, for she gathered that Mamma\r\nwas in some fear of this terrible uncle, that he had much influence with\r\nhis brother, and was rather a severe judge of the young family.  She\r\nsincerely hoped that he would not find things much amiss, for the honest\r\ngoodness of the two eldest had won so much regard from her, that she\r\ncould not bear them to be under any cloud; and indeed she felt as if the\r\nwhole flock were her own property, as well as her charge, and that she,\r\nas well as they, were about to be tried.  She would have felt it all fair\r\nand just before their kindly father, but it seemed hard that all should\r\nbe brought before the school-master uncle; and she was disposed to be\r\ntender for her children, and exceedingly anxious as to the effect they\r\nmight produce.  She was resolved that the Captain should hear of the\r\naffair of the pence; but the presence of his brother would make the\r\nspeaking a much greater effort.  Meantime, she saw that all the fingers\r\nwere clean, and all the hair brushed.  She flattered herself that Susan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyellow locks had learnt that it was the business of hair to keep tidy,\r\nand had been much less unmanageable of late; but she had her fears that\r\nthey would ruffle up again when their owner, at the head of a large\r\ndetachment, rushed out to take the \xe2\x80\x9cfancy bread\xe2\x80\x9d out of the oven, and she\r\ncame half-way down stairs, in case it should be necessary to capture\r\nthem, and brush them over again.\r\n\r\nWhile thus watching, the door of the dining-room (the only down stairs\r\nroom in order) opened suddenly, and the Captain came forth.  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss\r\nFosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cplease come in here: I was just coming to look for\r\nyou.  My brother\xe2\x80\x94Miss Fosbrook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo her surprise, Miss Fosbrook received a very pleasant civil greeting\r\nfrom a much younger man than she had expected to see, looking perhaps\r\nmore stern about the mouth and sharp about the eye than his elder\r\nbrother, and his clerical dress very precise; but somehow he was so\r\ncuriously like his niece, Elizabeth, that she thought that his\r\nparticularity might spring from the same love of refinement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll going on well?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFairly well,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.  \xe2\x80\x9cSam and Susan are most excellent children.\r\nThere is only one matter on which I should like to speak to you, at some\r\ntime when it might suit you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it about this?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, putting into her hand a sheet written in\r\nhuge round-hand in pencil, no words misspelt, but the breaks in them at\r\nthe end of the lines perfectly regardless of syllables:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n    My dear Papa,\r\n\r\n    Please let me\r\n    have a poli\r\n    ceman.  Bet h\r\n    as got at Toby\r\n    and stole our\r\n    pence which was\r\n    for a secret.  Nu\r\n    rse says she is a\r\n    favourite and Miss\r\n    Fosbrook will not\r\n    find them.\r\n\r\n                                                     Your affectionate son\r\n                                                 DAVID DOUGLAS MERRIFIELD.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! this was the letter David insisted on sealing before I put it into\r\nmine!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Miss Fosbrook, as soon as she had made out the words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have been in great trouble at the loss; but we agreed not to write to\r\nyou, because you had so much on your mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Bessie in fault?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; none of us believe it; but I am very anxious that you should\r\nmake an investigation, for the maids suspect her, and have made the\r\nyounger children do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is Toby?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToby is only a jug\xe2\x80\x94called Toby Fillpot, I believe\xe2\x80\x94shaped like a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know!\xe2\x80\x9d put in Mr. John Merrifield, laughing.  \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you remember him,\r\nHarry?  We had the like in our time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d interrogated the Captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust after you left home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel, as shortly and clearly as\r\nshe could, \xe2\x80\x9cthe children agreed to save their allowance to buy a pig for\r\nHannah Higgins.  They showed great perseverance in their object; and by\r\nthe third week they had about seven shillings in this jug, which, to my\r\ngrief and shame, I let them keep in the glass cupboard, not locked, but\r\none door bolted, the other buttoned.  On Friday morning, the 11th, I know\r\nthe cup was full of coppers and silver, for I took it down to add\r\nsomething to it.  On the next Monday morning the money was gone, all but\r\none farthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you guess who took it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should prefer saying nothing till you have examined the children and\r\nservants for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain.  \xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x94I am sorry to treat you to a\r\ncourt-martial, John, but I must hold one after tea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristabel pitied the children for having to speak before this formidable\r\nuncle; but there could be no help for it, since no other sitting-room was\r\nhabitable, and there were torrents of rain out-of-doors.\r\n\r\nThere was just time to show the glass cupboard, and the shelf where Toby\r\nhad stood, and to return to the dining-room, before the children began to\r\nstream in and make their greetings to their uncle, Susan with George in\r\none hand, and her plate of bakings in the other.  Very fancy bread indeed\r\nit was! as Uncle John said.  The edge of Purday\xe2\x80\x99s hat had been quite\r\nbaked off, and one of his arms was gone; he was black in the wrong\r\nplaces, and was altogether rather an uncomfortable-looking object.\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s brood of rabbits were much more successful, though the ears of\r\nmany had fallen off.  Uncle John was very much diverted, and took his\r\nfull share of admiring and tasting the various performances.  On the\r\nwhole, the meal went off much better than Christabel had feared it would.\r\nShe had really broken the children of many of the habits with which they\r\nused to make themselves disagreeable; there was no putting of spoons into\r\neach other\xe2\x80\x99s cups, nor reaching out with buttery fingers; lips were\r\nwiped, and people sat still upon their chairs, even if they fidgeted and\r\nsighed; and there was only one slop made all tea-time, and that was by\r\nJohnnie, and not a very bad one.  Indeed, it might be hoped that Mr.\r\nMerrifield did not see it, for he was talking to Sam about the change of\r\nfootpath that Mr. Greville was making.  There was indeed no fun, but it\r\nmight be doubted whether Papa would have been in a mood for fun even had\r\nhis brother not been there; and Miss Fosbrook was rather glad there was\r\nnothing to make the children forgetful of propriety.\r\n\r\nAs soon as Mary had carried off the tea-things and wiped the table, Uncle\r\nJohn put himself as much out of the way as he could behind the newspaper\r\nin the recess of the window; and Miss Fosbrook would have gone to the\r\nschool-room, but Captain Merrifield begged her to stay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat a very unpleasant thing has taken place in my\r\nabsence, and I wish to learn all that I can about it, that the guilty\r\nperson may be brought to light, and the innocent cleared from any\r\nsuspicion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children looked at one another, wondering how he had heard, or\r\nwhether Miss Fosbrook had told him; but this was soon answered by his\r\ncalling out, \xe2\x80\x9cDavid! Was he there when you came\r\nin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere the doors open when you were dining there, Henry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cN\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure that you did not meddle with them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know why I should,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry, hastily and confusedly.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nonly the girls and the babies that have things there\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94and Miss\r\nFosbrook herself had been at the cupboard in the morning; why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nshe have left it undone herself, and the doors got open?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cif they aren\xe2\x80\x99t fastened they always burst open\r\ndirectly; and we never could have been in the room half the morning\r\nwithout noticing them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are certain that they were closed when you went down to\r\ndinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone was positive that the great glass doors flying out must have\r\nmade themselves observed in that room full of children, especially as\r\nSusan remembered that she had been making a desk of the sloping part\r\nunder them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes anyone remember how long it was between Hal\xe2\x80\x99s leaving the room and\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s coming up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know when he went out,\xe2\x80\x9d said all those who had been in the\r\ndining-room; but there spoke up a voice, quite proud of having something\r\nto tell among the others\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI saw Hal go out, and Bessie come up directly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Johnnie!  How was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook made me dine in the nursery, Papa, because Hal and I had\r\nbeen riding on the new iron gate, to see if the telegraph would come in\r\nwhile the others were at church; and then Hal ran away with the\r\nGrevilles, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get down till Sam came and helped me; and so\r\nMiss Fosbrook made me dine in the nursery; and when I had done, I went\r\nand sat upon the top of the garret stairs, to watch when they came out\r\nfrom dinner, and ask if I might come down again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did you see, Johnnie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, I saw a wasp,\xe2\x80\x9d said Johnnie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind the wasp.  Did you see when Henry went out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw him come in first,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, \xe2\x80\x9cand Miss Fosbrook order him up and\r\nsay she would send him his dinner, and come and speak to him presently.\r\nSo I watched to catch her when she was coming up to him, and I saw Mary\r\nbring him up some mince veal, and the last bit of the gooseberry pie; and\r\nthen, very soon, he bolted right downstairs.  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think he could\r\nhave had time to eat the pie; and I was going to see if there was a bit\r\nleft, when I saw Bessie coming up, and I whipped up again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen nobody went into the room between Henry and Bessie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whole room was quite silent.  There was no sound but a quick short\r\nbreathing from the Captain: but he had rested his brow upon his hand, and\r\nhis face could not be seen.  It was as if something terrible had flashed\r\nupon him, and he was struggling with the first shock, and striving to\r\ndeal with it.  If they had seen him in a tempest, with his ship driving\r\nto pieces on a rock, he would not have been thus shaken and dismayed.\r\nHowever, by the time he looked up again, he had brought his face back to\r\nits resolute firmness, and he spoke in a clear, stern, startling voice,\r\nthat made all the children quake, and some catch hold of each other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands: \xe2\x80\x9cHenry! tell me what you have done with your theft!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiserable Henry!  He did not try to deny it any longer; but burst out\r\ninto a loud sobbing cry, \xe2\x80\x9cO Papa!  Papa!  I meant to have put it back\r\nagain!  I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me what you have done with it!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the Captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I paid it to Farmer Grice; I was obliged; and I thought I could have\r\nput it back again; and some of it was my own!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFivepence-farthing!\xe2\x80\x9d cried David.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou thief, you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child\xe2\x80\x99s fists were clenched, and his young face all one scowl of\r\npassion, quite shocking to see.  His father put him aside, and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, David! no names.\xe2\x80\x94Now, Henry, what do you say to your sister for\r\nyour false accusation, which has thrown your own shame on her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, no, Papa; he never did accuse me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie, for the first\r\ntime bursting into tears.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe never said I did it; that was only Davie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfancy; and it has made Susie and Sam so kind, I have not minded it at\r\nall.  Please don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that, Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome away, Henry!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain; \xe2\x80\x9cnow that your sister has been\r\ncleared, we had better have the rest out of the sight of these\r\ntender-hearted little girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood up, and without a word, stroked down Elizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s smooth brown\r\nhair, raised her face up by the chin, and kissed her forehead, the only\r\nplace free from tears; then he took Henry by the shoulder, and marched\r\nhim out of the room.  Bessie could not stop herself from crying, and was\r\nafraid of letting Uncle John see her; so she flew out after them, and\r\nstraight up-stairs to her own room.  Miss Fosbrook and Susan both longed\r\nto follow her, but they had missed this opportunity; and the sound of\r\nvoices outside showed so plainly that the Captain and Henry were in the\r\nhall that they durst not open the door.\r\n\r\nEveryone was appalled, and nothing was said for a few seconds.  The first\r\nto speak was Annie, in a low, terror-stricken whisper, yet with some\r\ncuriosity in it: \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what Papa will do to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him nine dozen, I hope!\xe2\x80\x9d answered David through his small white\r\nteeth, all clenched together with rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Davie!\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cyou should not wish anything so dreadful\r\nfor your brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been so wicked!  I wish it!  I _will_ wish it!\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, David!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9csuch things must not be said.  I will\r\ntalk to you by and by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad poor Bessie is cleared!\xe2\x80\x9d added Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cthough I always knew\r\nshe could not have done it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be sure\xe2\x80\x94I knew it was Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam! you did?\xe2\x80\x94why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94to say\xe2\x80\x94sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cand I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go and get him into a\r\nscrape.  I thought he might tell himself, if he could ever make up the\r\nmoney again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9che would have done that.  He always fancied he should\r\nget a sovereign from Colonel Carey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe talked till he thought so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what made you guess he had done so, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.  \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndid suspect him myself, but I never felt justified in accusing him of\r\nsuch a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!  I saw he had been getting into a fix with those\r\nGrevilles, and had been sold somehow.  They said something, and got out\r\nof my way directly, and I was sure they had done some mischief, and left\r\nhim to pay the cost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ask him?\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the use?  One never knows where to have him.  He will eat up\r\nhis words as fast as he says them, with his _at least_, till he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what he means.  Nor I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to know much of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill I can\xe2\x80\x99t think how you could let poor Bessie live under such a\r\ncloud,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cnor anyone worth a snap of my finger.\r\nBesides, if I had known, and had to tell, what a horrid shame it would\r\nhave been if the naval cadetship had been to be had for him!  I knew\r\nBessie would have thought so too, and then he would have been out of the\r\nway of the Grevilles, and would have got some money to make it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen is there no chance of the cadetship now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we should have heard of it long ago if there had been!  So I mind\r\nthe coming out the less; but it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly abominable to have had all\r\nthis row, and for Papa to be so cut up in this little short time at\r\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw him more grieved,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Merrifield.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe was hardly more\r\novercome when your mother was at the worst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey started, for they had forgotten Uncle John, or they would never have\r\nspoken so freely; but he now put down his newspaper, and looked as if he\r\nmeant to talk.\r\n\r\nSusan ventured to say, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd indeed they had all been so very good before.\r\nThe pig made them so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA learned pig, I should think,\xe2\x80\x9d said her uncle, laughing good-naturedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were obliged to take care,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan, \xe2\x80\x9cor we got so many fines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristabel, finding that Mr. Merrifield looked at her, helped out Susan\r\nby explaining that various small delinquencies were visited with fines,\r\nand that the desire to save for the pig had rendered the children very\r\ncareful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI was thankful for the incentive, but I am afraid\r\nthat it was over-worked, and did harm in the end:\xe2\x80\x9d and she glanced\r\ntowards David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the way with secondary motives,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer.\r\n\r\nHere Captain Merrifield came back alone; and his brother was the only\r\nperson who ventured to say, \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have sent him to his room,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very bad\r\nbusiness, though of course he made excuses to himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Captain then told them Henry\xe2\x80\x99s confession.  He had been too much\r\nhurried by the fear of being caught, to take out his own share of the\r\nhoard, and had therefore emptied the whole cupful into his\r\npocket-handkerchief, tied it up, and run off with it, intending to\r\nseparate what was honestly his own.  What that was he did not know, but\r\nhis boastful habits and want of accuracy had made his memory so careless,\r\nthat he fancied that a far larger proportion was his than really was, and\r\nhis purposes were in the strange medley that falls to the lot of all\r\nself-deceivers, sometimes fancying he would only take what he had a right\r\nto (whatever that might be), sometimes that he would borrow what he\r\nwanted, and replace it when the sovereign should be given to him, or that\r\nthe Grevilles would make it up when they had their month\xe2\x80\x99s allowance.\r\n\r\nWhen he came to the farm Mr. Grice was resolved to take nothing less than\r\nthe whole sum that he had with him.  Perhaps this was less for the value\r\nof the turkey-cock than for the sake of giving the boys such a lesson as\r\nto prevent them from ever molesting his poultry again.  At any rate, he\r\nwas inexorable till the frightened Henry had delivered up every farthing\r\nin his possession; and then, convinced that no more was forthcoming, he\r\nrelented so far as to restore the gun, and promise to make no complaint\r\nto either of the fathers.\r\n\r\nAt first Henry lived on hopes of being able to restore the money before\r\nthe hoard should be examined, but Colonel Carey went away, and, as might\r\nhave been expected, left no present to his brother\xe2\x80\x99s pupils.  Still Henry\r\nhad hopes of the Grevilles, and even when the loss was discovered, hoped\r\nto restore it secretly, and make the whole pass off as a joke; but the\r\n1st of August came, Martin and Osmond received their pocket-money, but\r\nlaughed his entreaty to scorn, telling him that he had shot the\r\nturkey-cock, not they.  Since that time, his only hope had been in the\r\naffair blowing over\xe2\x80\x94as if a sin ever _did_ blow over!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne question I must ask, Miss Fosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, \xe2\x80\x9cthough after\r\nsuch a course of deceit it hardly makes it worse.  Has he told any direct\r\nfalsehood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe paused, and recollected.  \xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid he did;\r\nhe flatly told me that he had not touched the baby-house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expected nothing else,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain gravely.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has become of\r\nBessie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe ran up-stairs.  May I go and call her?\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said her father.\r\n\r\nHe found Elizabeth in the school-room, all flushed and tear-stained in\r\nthe face; and he told her affectionately how much pleased he was with her\r\npatience under this false accusation.  Delight very nearly set her off\r\ncrying again, but she managed to say, \xe2\x80\x9cIt was Miss Fosbrook and Sam and\r\nSusie that made me patient, Papa; they were so kind.  And nobody would\r\nhave believed it, if I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t always cross, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot cross now, my little woman,\xe2\x80\x9d he said smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!  I said I never could be cross again, now Mamma is better; but Miss\r\nFosbrook says I shall sometimes feel so, and I do believe she is right,\r\nfor I was almost cross to Georgie to-day.  But she says one may _feel_\r\ncross, and not _be_ cross!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did not quite know all that his little girl was thinking of; but he\r\npatted her fondly, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, there is a great deal to be thankful\r\nfor, my dear; and I shall trust to you elder ones to give your Mamma no\r\ntrouble while I am afloat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will try,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd please, Papa, would you tell Nurse about\r\nit?  She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t half believe us, and she is so tiresome about Miss\r\nFosbrook!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiresome! what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe always thinks what she does is wrong, and she puts nonsense into\r\nJohnnie\xe2\x80\x99s head, and talks about favourites.  Mary told Susan it was\r\njealousy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Captain spoke pretty strongly to Nurse Freeman that evening, but it\r\nis doubtful if she were the better for it.  She was a very good woman in\r\nmost things, but she could not bear that the children should be under\r\nanyone but herself; and just as Henry lost the truth by inaccuracy, she\r\nlost it by prejudice.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was glad to get away from the dining-room, where it was\r\nrather awful to sit without her work and be talked to by Mr. Merrifield,\r\neven though she liked him much better than she had expected.\r\n\r\nWhen David came to bed, she sat by him and talked to him about his angry\r\nunforgiving spirit.  She could not but think he was in a fearful temper,\r\nand she tried hard to make him sorry for his brother, instead of\r\nthirsting to see the disappointment visited on him; but David could not\r\nsee what she meant.  Wicked people ought to be punished; it was wicked to\r\nsteal and tell stories, and he hoped Henry would be punished, so as he\r\nwould never forget it, for hindering poor Hannah from getting her pig.\r\n\r\nHe would not understand Henry\xe2\x80\x99s predicament; he was only angry, bitterly\r\nangry, and watching for vengeance.  Miss Fosbrook could not reason or\r\npersuade him out of it, nor make him see that he could hardly say his\r\nprayers in such a mood.  Indeed, he would rather have gone without his\r\nprayers than have ceased to hope for Henry\xe2\x80\x99s punishment.\r\n\r\nPerhaps in this there was sense of justice and indignation against wrong\r\ndoing, as well as personal resentment.  Miss Fosbrook tried to think so,\r\nand left him, but not without praying for him, that a Christian temper of\r\nforgiveness might be sent upon him.\r\n\r\nAll the others were subdued and awe-struck.  It was not yet known what\r\nwas to happen to Henry; but there was a notion that it would be very\r\nterrible indeed, and that Uncle John would be sure to make it worse; and\r\nthey wished Miss Fosbrook good-night with very sad faces.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\n\r\n\r\nNOTHING had as yet befallen Henry, for he came down to breakfast in the\r\nmorning; but his father did not greet him, and spoke no word to him all\r\nthe time they were in the room together.  The children felt that this was\r\nindeed terrific.  Such a thing had never befallen any of them before.\r\nThey would much rather have been whipped; and even David\xe2\x80\x99s heart sank.\r\n\r\nSomething, however, was soon said that put all else out of his sisters\xe2\x80\x99\r\nminds.  The Captain turned to them with his merry smile, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cPray\r\nwhat would Miss Susie and Miss Bessie say to coming up to London with me\r\nto see Mamma?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two girls bounded upon their chairs; Susan\xe2\x80\x99s eyes grew round, and\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s long; the one said, \xe2\x80\x9cO Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d and the other, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand they looked so overwhelmed with ecstasy, and all the three elders\r\nlaughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you will behave discreetly, young women?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan; \xe2\x80\x9cand Bessie always does.  Oh, thank you, Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrandmamma should be thanked; she asked me to bring a child or two, to\r\nbe with Mamma when I go down to Portsmouth.  We had thought of Susan; but\r\nI think Betty deserves some amends for what she has undergone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, Papa! thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Susan, Sam, and David, from their\r\nhearts; John and Annie because the others did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you won\xe2\x80\x99t kick her out if she shares your berth, Sue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am so glad, Papa!  It is so nice to go together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, Miss Fosbrook, will you be kind enough to rig them out?  I must\r\ndrive into Southminster at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock; and if you would be so good as to\r\nsee them smartened up for London there, I should be much obliged to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mere drive to the country town was a great event in itself, even\r\nwithout the almost incredible wonder that it was to lead to; and the\r\ndelights of which Ida and Miss Fosbrook had told them in London went so\r\nwildly careering through the little girls\xe2\x80\x99 brains, that they hardly knew\r\nwhat they said or did, as they danced about the house, and ran up-stairs\r\nto get ready, long before ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nMr. Carey had been informed that his pupils would not come to him during\r\nthe few days of their father\xe2\x80\x99s stay; and Sam begged to ride in on his\r\npony by the side of the carriage; but he was desired to fetch his books,\r\nand call Henry, as his uncle wished to give them both an examination.\r\nWas this the beginning of captivity to Uncle John?  David and Johnnie\r\nwere quite angry.  They considered it highly proper that Hal should be\r\nshut up with Uncle John, but they thought it very hard that Sam should be\r\nso used too; and Sam himself looked very round-backed, reluctant, and\r\nmiserable, partly at the task, partly at being deprived of the sight of\r\nhis father for several hours of one of those few precious days.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook wished Susan to have sat on the front seat of the old\r\nphaeton with her father; but he would not consent to this, and putting\r\nthe two little girls together behind, handed the governess to the place\r\nof honour beside him, where she felt rather shy, in spite of his bright\r\neasy manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, after having flourished his whip merrily at\r\nJohnnie, Annie, and Davie, who were holding open the iron gate, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\nhave had a tough job with those youngsters!  We never meant you to have\r\nbeen left so long to their mercy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know\xe2\x80\x94I know; I only wish I could have done better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have done wonders.
