My brother hardly knows where he is\xe2\x80\x94never saw\r\nthose children so mannerly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook could not show how delighted she was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could hardly have ventured on taking those two girls to town unless\r\nyou had broken them in a little.  I would say nothing last night till I\r\nhad watched Susan; for my mother is particular, and if my wife was to be\r\nalways worrying herself about their manners, they had better be at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, I think you may quite trust to their behaving well.  Those two\r\nand Sam are so thoroughly trustworthy, that I had no real difficulty till\r\nthis unhappy business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Captain wanted to talk this over with her, and hear her account of it\r\nonce more.  She gave it fully, thinking he ought to know exactly how his\r\nchildren had acted in the matter, and wishing to explain where she\r\nthought she had made mistakes.  When she had finished, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cThank\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d and considered a little while; then said, \xe2\x80\x9cA thing like this brings\r\nout a great deal of character; and a new eye sometimes sees more what is\r\nin a child than those that bred him up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been a touchstone, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Hal!\xe2\x80\x9d he said sadly; then resumed, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve said nothing of it yet to\r\nthe boys\xe2\x80\x94but Admiral Penrose has promised to let me take out one with me.\r\nI had thought most of Hal; he seemed to me a smarter fellow, more likely\r\nto make his way than his brother; but this makes me doubt whether there\r\ncan be stuff enough in him.  I might not be able to look after him, nor\r\ndo I know what his messmates may be; and I should not choose to risk it,\r\nexcept with a boy I could thoroughly trust.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose young Grevilles seem to me Hal\xe2\x80\x99s bane and temptation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, ay; but if a boy is of the sort, he\xe2\x80\x99ll find someone to be his bane,\r\nwherever he goes.  I\xe2\x80\x99ll have no more of the Grevilles though.  If he\r\nshould not go with me, my brother John would take him into his house, and\r\nkeep a sharp look out after him.  Just tell me, if you have no objection,\r\nhow the boy strikes you.  Most people think him the most taking of the\r\nlot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel thoughtfully; \xe2\x80\x9che has more ease and\r\nreadiness, and he is affectionate and warm-hearted; but then he is a\r\ngreat talker, and fond of boasting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly.  I told him that was the very way he learnt falsehood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid, too,\xe2\x80\x9d she was obliged to add, \xe2\x80\x9cthat his resolutions run\r\naway in talk.  He has not much perseverance; and he is easily led.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I believe you are right; but then what\xe2\x80\x99s to be done?  I can hardly\r\nafford to lose this chance; but Sam was always backward; and I doubt his\r\neven caring to go to sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!  Captain Merrifield!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! has he given you reason to think that he does?\xe2\x80\x9d  She told him how\r\nshe had found Sam struggling with his longing for the sea and his father;\r\nand how patiently the boy had resigned himself to see his brother put\r\nbefore him, and himself condemned for being too dull and slow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I say so?  I suppose he had put me past my patience with blundering\r\nover his lessons.  I never meant to make any decision; but I did not\r\nthink he wished it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said it had been his desire from the time he could remember,\r\nespecially when he felt the want of you during your last voyage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery odd; how reserved some boys are!  I declare I was vexed that it had\r\ngone out of his head; though I thought it might be for the best.  You\r\nknow I was not born to this place.  I never dreamt of it till my poor\r\nbrother Sam\xe2\x80\x99s little boy went off in a fever six years ago, and we had to\r\nsettle down here.  Before that, we meant my eldest to follow my own\r\nprofession; but when he seemed to take to the soil so kindly, I thought,\r\nafter all, he might make the happier squire for never having learnt the\r\nsmell of salt water, nor the spirit of enterprise; but if it were done\r\nalready, the first choice is due to him.  You are sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk the girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe leant back and shouted out the question, \xe2\x80\x9cSue! do you know whether Sam\r\nwishes to go to sea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing he ever wished so much,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he say so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he thought it would be no use,\xe2\x80\x9d screamed Susan back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use! why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause Hal says Admiral Penrose promised him.  O Papa! are you going to\r\ntake Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear! we can\xe2\x80\x99t get on without him!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Elizabeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure he would like it?\xe2\x80\x9d said her father.  \xe2\x80\x9cI thought he never\r\ncared to hear of the sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to talk of it, because it makes him so sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bessie, \xe2\x80\x9che burnt his dear little ship, the _Victory_,\r\nbecause he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to look at it after you said _that_, Papa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter I said what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat he was not smart enough to learn the ropes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery silly of him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, \xe2\x80\x9cto take in despair what was only\r\nmeant to spur him on.  I suppose now I shall find he has dawdled so much\r\nthat he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get through an examination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis shut up the mouths of both the girls, who were afraid that he might\r\nnot, since they saw a good deal of his droning habits over his lessons,\r\nand heard more of Hal\xe2\x80\x99s superior cleverness.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook ventured to say, \xe2\x80\x9cYou may expect a great deal of a boy who\r\nworks on a pure principle of obedience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think a great deal of that youngster,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, highly\r\ngratified.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the first time I ever knew a stranger take to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not take to him as a stranger.  I thought him uncouth and dull.  I\r\nonly learnt to love and respect him, as I felt how perfectly I might rely\r\non him, and how deep and true his principles are.  If the children have\r\ngone on tolerably well in your absence, it is because he has always stood\r\nby me, and his weight of character has told on them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Merrifield did not answer at once; he bit his lip, then blew his\r\nnose, and cleared his throat, before he said, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook, you have\r\nmade me very happy; it will make his mother so.  She always rated him so\r\nhigh, that I half thought it was a weakness for her eldest son; but\r\nthere!  I suppose he was down-hearted about this fancy of his, poor boy;\r\nand that hindered him from making the most of himself.  I wonder what\r\nsort of a figure he is cutting before his uncle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe town was at length reached; and the shopping was quite wonderful to\r\nthe sisters.  Miss Fosbrook found a shop where the marvellous woman\r\nundertook to send home two grey frocks trimmed with pink, by the next\r\nevening; and found two such fashionable black silk jackets, that Susie\r\nfelt quite ashamed of herself, though rather pleased; and Bessie only\r\nwished she could see her own back, it must look so like Ida\xe2\x80\x99s.  Then\r\nthere were white sleeves, and white collars, that made them feel like\r\nyoung women; and little pink silk handkerchiefs for their necks; and two\r\nstraw hats, which Miss Fosbrook undertook to trim with puffs of white\r\nribbon, and a pink rosette at each ear.  Bessie thought they would be the\r\nmost beautiful things that had ever been in her possession, and was only\r\ndreading that Sam would say they were like those on Ida Greville\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndonkey\xe2\x80\x99s best harness; while Susan looked quite frightened at them,\r\nwhispered a hope that Mamma would not think them too fine, and that Miss\r\nFosbrook would not let them cost too much money; and when assured that\r\nall fell within what Papa had given to be laid out, she begged that Annie\r\nand little Sally might have the like.\r\n\r\nBut as they were not going to London, Miss Fosbrook could not venture on\r\nthis; and as Bessie had set her affections upon a certain white chip hat,\r\nwith a pink border and a white feather, both sisters remained wishing for\r\nsomething\xe2\x80\x94as is sure to happen on such occasions.\r\n\r\nHowever, Elizabeth recovered from the hat when she was out of sight of\r\nit; and they went and saw the cathedral, where the painted windows and\r\ngrave grand arches filled her with a truer and wiser sense of what was\r\nbeautiful; and then they walked a long time up and down under its\r\nbuttressed wall, waiting for Papa, till they grew tired and hungry; but\r\nat last he came in a great hurry, and sorry to have been hindered.  With\r\nnaval politeness, he gave his arm to Miss Fosbrook, and carried them off\r\nto a pastry-cook\xe2\x80\x99s, where he bade them eat what they pleased, and spend\r\nthe rest of the florin he threw them on buns for the little ones, while\r\nhe fetched the carriage; and so they all drove home again, and found the\r\nrest of the party ravenous, having waited dinner for three-quarters of an\r\nhour.\r\n\r\nWonderful to relate, Uncle John had not eaten anybody up! not even Baby;\r\nthough Papa advised Susan to make sure that she was safe, and then sent\r\nSam to ask Purday for a salad.  Perhaps this was by way of getting rid of\r\nthis constant follower while he asked his brother what he thought of the\r\nboys\xe2\x80\x99 attainments.\r\n\r\nUncle John could not speak very highly of the learning of either; but he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cSam knows thoroughly what he does know.  As to the other, he\r\nthinks he knows everything, and makes most awful shots.  When I asked\r\nthem who Dido\xe2\x80\x99s husband was, Sam told me he did not know, and Hal, that\r\nhe was Diodorus Siculus\xe2\x80\x94_at least_, Scipio\xe2\x80\x94no, he meant Sicyon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think neither could stand an examination for the cadetship?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not be sure of Sam; but I am quite sure that Hal could not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the dinner-bell rang; the hungry populace rushed to the dining-room,\r\nand the meal was gone through as merrily as could be, while still the\r\nfather never spoke to Henry.  Uncle John was as pleasant and good-natured\r\nas possible.  Who would have thought of the marked difference he made\r\nbetween dining with barbarians, or young gentlefolks!\r\n\r\nDinner over, Captain Merrifield called Sam,\xe2\x80\x94or rather, since that was not\r\nnecessary, as Sam was never willingly a yard from his elbow, he ordered\r\nthe others not to follow as they went into the garden together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cAdmiral Penrose is kind enough to offer me a berth in\r\nthe _Ramilies_ for one of you.  If you can pass the examination, should\r\nyou wish to avail yourself of the offer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam grew very red in the face, looked down, and twirled the button of his\r\nsleeve.  He certainly was not a gracious boy, for all he said was in a\r\ngruff hoarse voice, without even thanks, \xe2\x80\x9cNot if it is for this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor this!  What do you mean, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Merrifield, thinking\r\neither that the boy was faint-hearted, or that his wish had been the mere\r\nfancy of the girls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if it is to punish Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, with another effort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not the question.  Do you wish it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam hung his head, and made his eyebrows come down, as if they were to\r\nserve as a veil to those horrid tears in his eyes; and after all, his\r\nvoice sounded sulky, as he said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, angry and disappointed.  \xe2\x80\x9cIs that the\r\nway you take such an offer?  If you had rather stay here, and be bred up\r\nto be a country squire, say so at once; don\xe2\x80\x99t mince the matter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sam indignantly, \xe2\x80\x9chow can you think that?  Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I\r\nalways want to be like you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why can\xe2\x80\x99t you say so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to cut Hal out!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, putting his arm over his\r\neyes, as a way he considered secret of disposing of his tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut that out of your head, Sam; or if you don\xe2\x80\x99t fancy the sea, have it\r\nout at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Papa! please listen.  You know, though Miss Fosbrook is very jolly, we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help getting nohow when you were away, _us_ two particularly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have no mischief to confess, surely, Sam?\xe2\x80\x9d said his father, really\r\nimagining that this preference to Hal was acting on him so as to make him\r\nmention some concealed misdemeanour; \xe2\x80\x9cif you have, you know truth is the\r\nbest line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I haven\xe2\x80\x99t, Papa,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, looking up, quite surprised.  \xe2\x80\x9cYou know\r\nI am a year older, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help caring more; and Miss Fosbrook is so\r\nnice, one couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bother her; but you see the Grevilles _would_ put it\r\ninto Hal\xe2\x80\x99s head that it was stupid and like a girl to mind her.  It is\r\nall their fault; and they were sneaks about the turkey-cock, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npay\xe2\x80\x94and I know he would have ended by putting the money back when he\r\ncould, only Davie made such a row before he could; and he did so reckon\r\non the navy\xe2\x80\x94he would pay it back the first thing.\xe2\x80\x9d  The last sentences\r\ncame between gasps, very like sobs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave done with Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Merrifield, still with displeasure.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take him now on any account.  If the Grevilles lead him\r\nwrong, what would he do among the mids?  If he acts dishonourably here,\r\nwe should have him disgracing himself and his profession.  Since he can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake it, and you won\xe2\x80\x99t, I shall try to make some exchange of the chance\r\ntill John or David will be old enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Papa, I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t want to force you to it,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Captain Merrifield, in his\r\nvexed voice.  \xe2\x80\x9cI never mean to force my sons to any profession if I can\r\nhelp it; and you have a right to be considered.  It has always been a\r\ndisadvantage to me, and to this place, that I was bred to the sea instead\r\nof to farming; and though you can\xe2\x80\x99t live on the property without some\r\nprofession, it may be quite as well that you should turn your mind to\r\nsomething else\xe2\x80\x94only if it be the army, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help you on in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had rather go to sea, if you please,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say so to please me,\xe2\x80\x9d said his father.  \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you, the\r\nexaminations are a pretty deal harder than they were in my time.  It is\r\nnot a trade for a youngster to be idle in; and I won\xe2\x80\x99t have you, just\r\nwhen you\xe2\x80\x99ve knocked about a few years, and are getting fit to be of use\r\non board and nowhere else, calling yourself heartily sick of it, and\r\nturning round to say it was my doing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never do that, Papa,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Sam, unable to understand why his\r\nfather should speak as though scolding him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?  And mind, you must take the rough with the smooth, if you sail with\r\nme, and not be always running after me, Papa-ing me.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t see after\r\nyou, and should only get you ill will if I tried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had rather go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to make of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said his father, looking at\r\nhim in a puzzle.  \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, if you do mean to go, you may tell Freeman to\r\nget your things ready to come up with me on Thursday; only if you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreally like the notion, find out your own mind, and let me know in time,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Captain turned away, and gave a long whistle\xe2\x80\x94an accustomed\r\nsignal\xe2\x80\x94that brought children and dogs all rushing and tumbling about him\r\ntogether, to walk with him about the farm, and his brother among them;\r\nbut Sam hung back.  He had not the heart to go with that merry throng;\r\nfor he did not know whether his father were not displeased with him, and\r\nhe therefore thought he must be to blame.\r\n\r\nPeople who, like Sam, rather cultivate the habit of gruffness and\r\nreserve, and prefer to be short and rude, become so utterly unable to\r\nexpress what they mean, that on great occasions they are misunderstood,\r\nand give pain by supposed ingratitude and dislike, even when they feel\r\nmost warmly.  Captain Merrifield could only judge from looks and words;\r\nand even when Sam had been satisfied about Henry, he had shown so little\r\nalacrity or satisfaction, as really to leave a doubt whether he were not\r\nunwillingly yielding to his father\xe2\x80\x99s wishes; which would have been a\r\nmistaken act, as the Captain thought no one ought to be a sailor unless\r\nwith a very strong desire that way.  Thus Sam really perplexed and\r\ndistressed his father, when he least intended it; and unable to\r\nunderstand what was the matter, yet feeling heavy and sad, he turned\r\naside from the rest, and, by way of the quietest place he could find,\r\nclimbed up a tall pear-tree, to the very highest branch he could reach.\r\nHe put himself astride on one bough, his feet upon another below, and his\r\nback leaning against the main stem.  No one could see him up so high\r\namong the thick leaves; but he could see all around the village, and over\r\nthe house; he could look down into the farm-court at the pigs burying\r\nthemselves in the straw; and out beyond at the geese and ducks in the\r\nmeadow, and the broods of chickens pecking and scratching about, or the\r\nolder poultry rolling in the dust-holes they had scraped for themselves.\r\nHe could see Purday among his cabbages in the garden; and further off,\r\ncould watch the walking-party through the fields, his father with little\r\nGeorge in his arms, and Uncle John as often as possible by his side;\r\nwhile the others frisked about, sometimes spreading out like a flock of\r\nsheep in the pasture land, or when they came to the narrow paths in the\r\ncornfields, all getting into single file, and being lost sight of all but\r\ntheir heads.\r\n\r\nSam recollected how, the day when he had heard that he was not likely to\r\nbe a sailor, he had felt as if he hated Stokesley, and as if it would be\r\na prison to him, and how everything reminding him of the sea had been a\r\nmisery to him.  He would not then have believed anyone who had told him\r\nthat he would really hear of his appointment and be so little glad.  Yet\r\nfor two whole years the loss of the hope had weighed on him, and made him\r\ndull whenever he thought of grown-up life, heard of the sea, or was asked\r\nwhat he was to be: and almost always, at his prayers, he had that meaning\r\nin his mind, when he said \xe2\x80\x9cThy Will be done;\xe2\x80\x9d he had really submitted\r\npatiently, and tried to put away the longing from his mind, and would,\r\nthere can be no doubt, have been happy and dutiful at home; but at length\r\nthe wish of his heart was suddenly granted.\r\n\r\nAnd then, wish though it still were, there came all this grief and\r\ndiscomfort.  The gladness was in him somewhere, but he could not get at\r\nit, either for his own comfort, or that of his father.  He missed his\r\nmother exceedingly.  _She_ would know what he meant, and tell Papa that\r\nhe did care to go.  Yet, did he care so very much?  Only think of\r\nbeginning to be a stranger at this dear old home! and seeing no mother,\r\nno Susie, nor any of them, for years together\xe2\x80\x94probably not his father\r\nafter the first voyage!  However, the sailor was too strong in Sam for\r\nthat grief not to pass off; and his chief trouble was the sense of\r\nsupplanting Henry.  He knew the disappointment would be most bitter; and\r\nhe could not get rid of the sense of having taken an unfair advantage of\r\nthe disgrace of Henry\xe2\x80\x99s adventure.  As to his father\xe2\x80\x99s manner, he got\r\nover that more easily, for his conscience was free; he knew that the tone\r\nof displeasure would be gone at the next meeting, and he was too sure of\r\nhis own love of the sea to fear that he should not show it enough.  After\r\nall, he was to be a naval cadet!  He could not be sorry.  Nay, he felt he\r\nhad his wish; the very wish he had thought it wrong to put into a prayer.\r\nHe thought he ought to be thankful that it was granted, in the same way\r\nas he had been when his mother began to recover.  So he put his hands\r\ntogether, and looked up into the summer blue sky through the leaves, and\r\nhis lips moved, as he whispered his thanks, and asked to be helped in\r\nbeing a good brave sailor, and that something as good might happen to\r\npoor Henry.\r\n\r\nAfter this, somehow, the weight was gone, he knew not where.  All he\r\nrecollected was, that he should see Mamma in two days, and that he was to\r\nsail with Papa if he could get through his examination.  There was a sort\r\nof necessity of doing something comical; and just then spying Miss\r\nFosbrook with a book walking slowly below, he could not resist the\r\ntemptation of sending down on her a shower of little hard pears and\r\ntwigs.\r\n\r\nBob came one down on her book, and another on her bonnet.  She looked up,\r\nand saw a leg stretching out for a branch, apparently in such a dangerous\r\nmanner, that she did not know whether she should not have Sam himself on\r\nher head next, and started back, watching as he swung himself from branch\r\nto branch, and then slid down, embracing the trunk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I hit you!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.  \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help trying it; it was such fun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a great liberty; but she was so good-humoured as to laugh, and\r\nsaid he had taken good aim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Miss Fosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d next said he, \xe2\x80\x9cwould you hear how many\r\npropositions I can say!\xe2\x80\x9d  And as she opened her eyes at this holiday\r\namusement, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cPapa has got the appointment after all, and means\r\nme to have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad, Sam!  I give you joy!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and took his hand to\r\nshake it heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish Hal could go too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d she said kindly, and guessing his feelings, as having gone\r\nalong with them, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder you are sorry for him; but indeed I\r\nthink it is better for him to be sheltered from beginning real life just\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa said he would not have taken him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it seems so hard\r\nto have all his life changed for a thing that sounds worse than he meant\r\nit to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cI once read a sermon, that said that our\r\nconduct in little things does decide the tenor of our lives.  You know\r\none moment of hastiness cost Moses the Promised Land; and only a little\r\nwhile ago, we heard how Joash had but few victories allowed to him,\r\nbecause he did not think it worth while to strike the ground as often as\r\nElisha told him.  It is the little things that show whether we are to be\r\ntrusted with great.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is such a tremendous punishment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen he would have put\r\nit back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother knew a banker\xe2\x80\x99s clerk who was transported for borrowing what\r\nhe meant to put back again.  No, Sam; people must bear the result of\r\ntheir doings; and your father judges for Hal as much in kindness as in\r\nanger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know he knows best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may see it as well as trust.  With all his grand talk, do you really\r\nthink that Hal would not be upset at the first hardship, or that he could\r\nface bullying or danger?  Remember the bull, that was at least a vicious\r\ncow, and turned out to be a calf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSam could not help laughing, as he said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, that would never do at\r\nsea; and he would be done for if he were cowardly there.  But I wish I\r\ncould get out of sight of him till I am gone.  And please hear my Euclid;\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll get the book, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll stay out here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTherefore, if the two sides of two triangles be equal to one another,\r\nand the adjacent angles be equal each to each,\xe2\x80\x9d resounded through the\r\nlaurels, as the walking party returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHallo! _al fresco_ Euclid!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Uncle John, as Sam with a blush\r\nran after his blotted diagrams, as a sudden gust of wind blew them\r\ndancing over the garden.  Captain Merrifield caught one, and restored it\r\nto Sam, with a pat on the back that made his teeth rattle in his head,\r\nbut which made him as happy as a young sea-king, showing that they\r\nperfectly understood each other.\r\n\r\nBut to be ever so good a boy does not carry one through the examinations\r\nthat stand at the door of every road of life for those who are not\r\nwealthy.  Sam knew he was the dull boy of Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s four pupils; and\r\nthough from sheer diligence he was less often turned back than the rest,\r\nyet they could all excel him whenever they chose: his lessons all went\r\nagainst the grain, and were a sore trouble to him; and his uncle had\r\nshown much wrath to-day at his ignorance and backwardness.  He was\r\ntherefore in a great fright, and gave himself and Miss Fosbrook no peace,\r\nrunning after her every moment with his Euclid, his Colenso, or his\r\nslate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat boy will stupefy himself and his admirable cramming machine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nexclaimed Uncle John, when coming out into the court after tea to talk to\r\nPurday, the two brothers heard, \xe2\x80\x9cThe complement A E is equal to the\r\ncomplement D E,\xe2\x80\x9d proceeding out of the school-room window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA truce with your complements to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the Captain; \xe2\x80\x9ccome\r\ndown, Sam; I must have a game at hide-and-seek!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThough hide-and-seek on the lawn with Papa was the supremest bliss that\r\nlife had yet offered to the young Merrifields, and though Susan, Bessie,\r\nAnnie, and Johnnie, had all severally burst into the room to proclaim it\r\nand summon Sam, he had refused them all; but this call settled it; he\r\nbroke off in the middle of his rectangle, and dashed down stairs, to the\r\ngreat relief of kind Miss Fosbrook, who, with all her good-will, found\r\nher head beginning to grow weary of angles and right-angles on a hot\r\nevening in the height of summer.\r\n\r\nThe summer-house was to be _home_, and there the party were\r\nassembled\xe2\x80\x94nine in number, for not only Papa, but Uncle John, was going to\r\nplay; and Henry, though forlorn and unnoticed, had wandered about with\r\nthe rest all day, trying to do as usual, to forget the heavy load that\r\npressed on him, and to believe that he was not going to be punished for\r\nmere unluckiness in borrowing, and for not answering impertinent\r\nquestions.  The world was very unlike itself to him; and he saw the\r\nenjoyment without being able to enter into it, just as a sick person sees\r\nthe sunshine without feeling the warmth; but instead of penitence, he\r\nmerely tried to shake off his compunction.\r\n\r\nSo there he stood in the ring, as Susan was finding out who was to be the\r\nfirst to hide, by pointing to each, at each word of the formula,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cEggs, butter, cheese, bread,\r\n    Sticks, stocks, stones, dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDead\xe2\x80\x9d came to Uncle John, as perhaps Susan had contrived; and shrugging\r\nup his shoulders, he went off to hide, and his whoop was presently heard.\r\nHe was not _very_ good game; maybe he did not wish to be very long\r\nsought, for he was no further than in the tall French beans, generally\r\nconsidered as a stupid place to hide in.  The children had been in hopes\r\nthat he would catch Papa, which was always a very difficult matter, for\r\nthe sailor was lighter of foot, as well as, of course, longer in limb,\r\nthan any of the children; but they saw that Uncle John had not the\r\nslightest chance with him, and it was Bessie who was caught in her\r\nhomeward race.\r\n\r\nBessie was rather a good hider, and was searched for far and wide before\r\nSam\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cI spy!  I spy!\xe2\x80\x9d gave the signal that a bit of the spotty cotton\r\nhad been seen peeping out from under Purday\xe2\x80\x99s big potato-basket in the\r\ntool-house, and the whole party flew towards home.  Bessie would not aim\r\nat Papa, for if so, she would certainly catch no one; but she hunted down\r\nDavid, who was too sturdy to be a quick runner, and who was very well\r\npleased to be caught.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have Papa!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as she captured him.  \xe2\x80\x9cI know of such a\r\ncunning place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s place proved to be in among his likenesses, the cabbages,\r\nimmediately in front of the summer-house.  There he lay flat on the very\r\nwet mould, among the stout cabbages, all of which had a bead of wet in\r\nevery wrinkle of their great leaves, so that when Susan had at length\r\nspied him, and he came plunging out, his brown-holland\xe2\x80\x94to say nothing of\r\nhis knees\xe2\x80\x94was in a state that would have caused most mammas to send him\r\nto be instantly undressed; but nobody even saw it, and he charged\r\ninstantly towards the door of the summer-house, not pursuing anyone in\r\nparticular, but cutting all off from their retreat.  He slipped aside,\r\nhowever, and let all the lesser game pass by uncaught; his soul soared\r\nhigher than even Uncle John, who looked on exceedingly amused at the\r\nsmall man\xe2\x80\x99s stratagem, and at the long dodging that took place between\r\nhim and his father, the quick lithe Captain skipping hither and thither,\r\nand trying to pop in one side while his enemy was on the other; and the\r\nsquare, determined, little, puffing, panting boy, guarding his door,\r\nhands on knees, ever ready for a dart wherever the attempt was made.  The\r\nwhole party in the home nearly went into fits at the fun, and at the\r\ndroll remarks Uncle John made at this hare and tortoise spectacle; till\r\nat last either the Captain gave in, or Davie made a cleverer attack than\r\never, for with a great shout he flew upon Papa, and held him fast by the\r\nlegs.  Everyone shrieked with delight; Papa hid in such clever places,\r\nand when found, he roared so splendidly, that it struck the little ones\r\nwith terror, and did the hearts of the elders good, to hear him; indeed,\r\nthe greatest ambition Johnnie entertained was to roar like Papa.  Then he\r\ncould make his voice sound as if out of any place he chose, so that no\r\none could guess by his \xe2\x80\x9cwhoop\xe2\x80\x9d where to look for him; and this time it\r\nseemed to be quite out at the other end of the kitchen-garden, where they\r\nwere all looking, when another \xe2\x80\x9cwhoop\xe2\x80\x9d came apparently down from Sam\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npear-tree on the lawn; and while they were peeping up into it, \xe2\x80\x9cwhoop\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nre-echoed from the stables!  At last, as Annie was gazing up and round as\r\nif she even thought it as well to look right into the sky for Papa, she\r\nsuddenly beheld the two merriest eyes in the world, on the roof of the\r\nsummer-house itself.  He had been lying there on the thatch, watching at\r\nhis ease all the wanderings of the seekers, and uttering those wonderful\r\nwhoops to bewilder them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI spy!  I spy!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked Annie, flying in, even while her father sprang\r\nto the ground, and with Davie\xe2\x80\x99s man\xc5\x93uvre on a larger scale, seemed to be\r\ntaking his choice of all the fugitives rushing up from all parts.\r\n\r\nOne elder boy, and one younger, he was hunting down the gooseberry-path,\r\nwhen just as he was about to pounce on the former, he said that it was\r\nnot Sam, stood still, and folded his arms.  A shriek made him look round;\r\nlittle David stood sobbing and crying piteously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavie! what, Davie!  What is it, my man?  Where are you hurt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!  I\xe2\x80\x99m not hurt!  Catch Hal, Papa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, David.  I do not play with boys that act like Henry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak to him, Papa; oh, speak!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall, before I go,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, now!  Papa.  Oh, do!  I did want him to be punished, but not like\r\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, David.  If he can expect to play with me, and be treated like the\r\nothers, he is not in the state to receive forgiveness.  There, have done\r\ncrying; let us go on with the game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut David could not go on playing; he was too unhappy.  Not to be\r\nforgiven, even if punished, seemed to him too dreadful to happen to\r\nanyone; and he thought that he had brought it all on Henry by his letter\r\nof accusation.  Tardily and dolefully he crept into the house; and Miss\r\nFosbrook met him, looking so woe-begone, that she too thought he had hurt\r\nhimself.  She took him, dirt and all, on her lap; and there he sobbed out\r\nthat Papa wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak to Hal, and it was very dreadful; and he wished\r\nthere were no such things as pigs, or money, or secrets; they only made\r\npeople miserable!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Davie, they only make people miserable when they care too much\r\nabout them.  Papa will forgive Hal before he goes away, I am sure; only\r\nhe is making him sorry first, that he may never do such a thing again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d  And David cried sadly, perhaps because partly he was\r\ntired with having been on his legs more than usual that day; but his good\r\nand loving little self was come home again.  He at least had forgiven his\r\nbrother the wrong done to himself; and there was no hanging back that\r\nnight from the fulness of prayer; no, he rather felt that he had been\r\nunkind; and the last thing heard of him that night was, that as Sam and\r\nHal were coming up-stairs to bed, a little white figure stood on the top\r\nof the stairs, and a small voice said, \xe2\x80\x9cHal, please kiss me!  I am so\r\nsorry I told Papa about\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, hold your tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, cutting him short with the desired\r\nkiss, \xe2\x80\x9cif you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t told, someone else would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut long after Sam was asleep, Hal was wetting his pillow through with\r\ntears.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\n\r\n\r\nSTILL the silence lasted.  Henry had tried at first to persuade himself\r\nthat it was only by chance that he never heard his own name from lips\r\nthat used to call it more often than any other.  Indeed, he was so much\r\nused to favour, that it needed all the awe-struck pity of the rest to\r\nprove to him its withdrawal; and he was so much in the habit of thrusting\r\nhimself before Samuel, that even the sight and sound of the First Book of\r\nEuclid, all day long, failed to convince him that his brother could be\r\npreferred; above all, as Nurse Freeman had been collecting his clean\r\nshirts as well as Sam\xe2\x80\x99s, and all the portmanteaus and trunks in the house\r\nhad been hunted out of the roof.  Once, either the spirit of imitation,\r\nor his usual desire of showing himself off, made him break in when Sam\r\nwas knitting his brows frightfully over a sum in proportion.  Hal could\r\ndo it in no time!\r\n\r\nSo he did; but he put the third term first, and multiplied the hours into\r\nthe minutes, instead of reducing them to the same denomination; so that\r\nhe made out that twenty-five men would take longer to cut a field of\r\ngrass than three, and then could not see that he was wrong; but Miss\r\nFosbrook and Sam both looked so much grieved for him, that a start of\r\nfright went through him.\r\n\r\nSome minds really do not understand a fault till they see it severely\r\nvisited; and \xe2\x80\x9c_at least_\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c_couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help_\xe2\x80\x9d had so blinded Henry\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes that he had thought himself more unlucky than to blame, till his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s manner forced it on him that he had done something dreadful.\r\nVaguely afraid, he hung about, looking so wretched that he was a piteous\r\nsight; and it cut his father to the heart to spend such a last day\r\ntogether.  Mayhap the Captain could hardly have held out all that second\r\nday, if he had not passed his word to his brother.\r\n\r\nThe travellers were to set off at six in the morning, to meet the\r\nearliest train: and it was not till nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock at night, when the four\r\nelder ones said good-night, that the Captain, following them out of the\r\nroom, laid his hand on Henry as the others went up-stairs, and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHenry, have you nothing to say to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry leant against the baluster and sobbed, not knowing what else to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t be more grieved than I am to have such a last day together,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid his father, laying his hand on the yellow head; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nit, you see.  If you will do such things, it is my duty to make you\r\nrepent of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHal threw himself almost double over the rail, and something was heard\r\nabout \xe2\x80\x9csorry,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cnever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor little lad!\xe2\x80\x9d said his father aloud to himself; \xe2\x80\x9che is cut up enough\r\nnow; but how am I to know if his sorrow is good for anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Papa!  I\xe2\x80\x99ll never do such a thing again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I knew that, Hal,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain, sitting down on the stairs,\r\nand taking him between his knees.  \xe2\x80\x9cThere, let us talk it over together.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you expected to steal and deceive when you got up in the\r\nmorning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo back to the beginning.  See how you came to this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he waited for an answer, Hal mumbled out after some time, \xe2\x80\x9cYou said we\r\nneed not go to church on a week-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t go in case the telegraph should come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are different ways of thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d said his father.  \xe2\x80\x9cChurch was the\r\nonly place where I _could_ have gone that St. Barnabas\xe2\x80\x99 Day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would have gone,\xe2\x80\x9d said the self-contradictory Henry, \xe2\x80\x9conly the\r\nGrevilles are always at one for being like a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa! now we see daylight!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The Grevilles are at one,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s more like getting to the bottom of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Papa,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal, glad to make himself out a victim to circumstance;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t think what a pair of fellows those are for not letting one\r\nalone; Purday says they haven\xe2\x80\x99t as much conscience between them as a\r\npigeon\xe2\x80\x99s egg has meat; and going down to Mr. Carey\xe2\x80\x99s with them every day,\r\nthey let one have no peace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will find people everywhere who will let you have no peace, unless\r\nyou do not care for them; though you will not be left to the Grevilles\r\nany longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Papa; when I am away from them, you will see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Hal, I shall not see, I shall hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall not I sail with you, then, Papa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not sail at all: I thought you knew that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought the Admiral must have given you two appointments,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hal\r\ntimidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe gave me _one_, for one of my sons.  The first choice is Sam\xe2\x80\x99s right,\r\neven if he had not deserved it by his brave patient obedience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHal hung his head; then said, \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Papa, if Sam broke down in his\r\nexamination, please mightn\xe2\x80\x99t I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Henry.  Not only does your uncle say that though Sam\xe2\x80\x99s success is\r\nvery doubtful, your inaccuracy would make your failure certain; but if\r\nyour knowledge were ever so well up to the mark, I could not put you into\r\nthe navy.  Left to yourself here, you have been insubordinate, vain,\r\nweak, shuffling: can I let you go into greater temptation, where disgrace\r\nwould be public and without remedy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but, Papa!  Papa!  Away from the Grevilles, and not under only a\r\ngoverness\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall be away from the Grevilles, and not under a governess.  Your\r\nuncle is kind enough to take you with him to his house, and will\r\nendeavour to make you fit to try to get upon the foundation by the time\r\nthere is a vacancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Papa! don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Henry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help it, Hal!  You have shown yourself unfit either for the sea\r\nor for home.  What can I do with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry me\xe2\x80\x94only try me, Papa.  I would\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot go by what you say you would be, but what you are.  Deeds, not\r\nwords.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if you won\xe2\x80\x99t let me go into the navy, only let me be in real\r\nschool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Henry; I have not the means of sending you there: excepting on the\r\nfoundation; and if you get admittance there at all, it will only be by\r\ngreat diligence, and your uncle\xe2\x80\x99s kindness in preparing you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry cried bitterly.  It was a dreadful prospect to do his lessons alone\r\nwith Uncle John in the boys\xe2\x80\x99 play-hours, and be kept in order by Aunt\r\nAlice when his uncle was in school.  Perhaps his father would not have\r\nliked it himself, for his voice was very pitying, though cheering, as he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cOne half year, Hal, very likely no more if you take pains, and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll get into school, and be very happy, so long as you don\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\nGreville of every idle chap you meet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenry cried as though beyond consolation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hate leaving you this way,\xe2\x80\x9d continued his father; \xe2\x80\x9cbut by the time I\r\ncome home you will see it was the best thing for you; and look up to\r\nUncle John as your best friend.  Why, Hal, boy, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be a tall fellow\r\nof fourteen!  Let me find you godly and manly: you can\xe2\x80\x99t be one without\r\nthe other.  There now, good night, God bless you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore might have been said to Henry on his fault and what had led to it;\r\nbut what his father did say was likely to sink deeper as he grew older,\r\nand had more sense and feeling.\r\n\r\nFrom him Captain Merrifield went to the school-room, where Miss Fosbrook\r\nwas packing up for the little girls, and putting last stitches to their\r\nequipments, with hearty good-will and kindness, as if she had been their\r\nelder sister.\r\n\r\nHe thanked her most warmly; and without sending away the girls, who were\r\nboth busy tacking in little white tuckers to the evening frocks, he began\r\nto settle about the terms on which she was to remain at Stokesley.  He\r\nsaid that he could not possibly have left his wife without a person on\r\nwhose friendly help and good management of the children he could depend.\r\nImportant as it was to him to be employed, he must have refused the\r\nappointment if Miss Fosbrook had been discontented, or had not had the\r\nchildren so well in hand.  He explained that he had reason to think that\r\nMrs. Merrifield\xe2\x80\x99s present illness had been the effect of all she had gone\r\nthrough while he was in the Black Sea during the Crimean War.  She had\r\nbeen a very strong person, and had never thought of sparing herself; but\r\nshe and all her little children had had to get into Stokesley in his\r\nabsence; she had to manage the estate and farm, teach the elder children,\r\nand take care of the babies, with no help but Nurse Freeman\xe2\x80\x99s: and though\r\nhe had been wounded when with the Naval Brigade, and had been at death\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndoor with cholera, the effects had done him no lasting harm at all; while\r\nthe over-strain of the anxiety and exertion that she had undergone all\r\nalone had so told upon her, that she had never been well since, and he\r\nmuch feared, would never be in perfect health again.  He must depend upon\r\nMiss Fosbrook for watching over her and saving her, as his little Susie\r\ncould not yet do; and for letting him know from time to time how she was\r\ngoing on, and whether he ought to give up everything and come home.\r\n\r\nHe had tears in his eyes as he thanked Christabel for her earnest promise\r\nto watch and tend Mrs. Merrifield with a daughter\xe2\x80\x99s care; and her heart\r\nswelled with strong deep feeling of sorrow and sympathy with these two\r\nbrave-hearted loving people, doing their duty at all costs so steadily;\r\nand she was full of gladness and thankfulness that they could treat her\r\nas a true and trusty friend.  He walked away, feeling far too much to\r\nbear any eye upon him; and Susan was found to be crying quietly, making\r\nher thread wet through, and her needle squeak at every stitch, at the sad\r\nnews that Mamma never was to be quite well, even though assured that she\r\nwas likely to be much better than she had been for months past.\r\n\r\nBessie shed no tears; but Miss Fosbrook, who had been hindered all day by\r\nSam\xe2\x80\x99s Euclid and Colenso, and had sat up till half-past eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock to\r\nmake the two Sunday frocks nice enough for the journey, on going into the\r\nbed-room to lay them out for the morning, saw a little face raised from\r\nthe pillow of one of the small white beds, and found her broad awake.\r\nBessie never could go to sleep properly when anything out of the common\r\nway was coming to pass, so that was the less wonder; but she had a great\r\ndeal in her head, and she was glad to get Christabel to kneel down by\r\nher, to listen to her whispers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Christabel, I am so sorry.  I never cared about it before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout what, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Papa said about when he was in the Black Sea.  I never knew Mamma\r\ncared so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say not, my dear; you were much younger then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know all about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie, \xe2\x80\x9cor else I\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten.\r\nI have been trying to remember whether we ever thought about Mamma; and\r\noh, Christabel! do you know\xe2\x80\x94I believe we only thought she was cross!  Oh\r\ndear! it was so naughty and bad of us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can guess how it happened, my dear.  You were not old enough to be\r\nmade her friends, and you could not understand quiet sorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo think we should have said she was cross!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was wrong, because it was disrespectful.  You see, my dear, when\r\ngrown people are in trouble, you young ones can\xe2\x80\x99t enter into their\r\nfeelings, nor always even find out that anything is amiss; and you get\r\nvexed at there being a cloud over the house, and call it crossness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrown-up people are sometimes cross, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie.  \xe2\x80\x9cNurse\r\nis; and I heard Papa say Aunt Alice was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have tempers, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook; \xe2\x80\x9cand unless we have\r\nconquered them as children, there will be signs of them afterwards; but\r\nvery few people, and certainly no children, can tell when grave looks, or\r\nwords sharper than usual, come from illness or anxiety or sorrow; and it\r\nis the only way to save great grief and self-reproach to give one\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\nfaults the blame, and try to be as unobtrusive and obliging as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am older now, and can understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bessie; \xe2\x80\x9cbut then, it is\r\nSusie that is right hand, and does everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s plenty in your own line, Bessie\xe2\x80\x94plenty of little kindly services\r\nthat are very cheering; and above all\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAttending to your Mamma\xe2\x80\x99s troubles will drive away your own grievances.\r\nOnly I will not talk to you any more now, for I want you to go to sleep;\r\nif you lie awake, you will be tired to-morrow, and that will incline you\r\nto be fretful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFretful to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie could not believe it possible; and indeed Miss Fosbrook did not\r\nthink the chance great, as long as there was amusement and excitement.\r\nThe danger would be in the waitings and disappointments that will often\r\noccur, even in the height of enjoyable schemes.\r\n\r\nIt would take too long to tell of all the good-byes.  The children old\r\nenough to enter into the parting were setting off too; and Miss Fosbrook\r\nfelt more for the little ones than they did for themselves, as they\r\nwatched their father and uncle and two sisters into the gig, and the boys\r\ninto the cart, with Purday to drive them and the boxes, Sam sitting on\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s old midshipman\xe2\x80\x99s chest, trying, as well as the jolting would\r\nlet him, to con over that troublesome Thirty-fifth Proposition, which\r\nnine times repetition to Miss Fosbrook had failed to put into his head.\r\n\r\nJohnnie and Annie wished themselves going to sea, or to London, or\r\nanywhere, rather than having the full force of Miss Fosbrook on their\r\nlessons!  She did not make them do more, but she took the opportunity of\r\nmaking everything be done thoroughly, and, as they thought, bothered them\r\nfrightfully about pronouncing their words in reading, and holding their\r\npens when they wrote.  After a little while, however, they found that\r\nreally their hands were much less tired, and their lines much smoother\r\nand more slanting, than when they crooked their fingers close down over\r\nthe ink.  Absolutely they began to know the pleasure of doing something\r\nwell, and they felt so comfortable, that they were wonderfully good; and\r\nthe pig fund might have had a chance, but David did not seem to think of\r\nreviving it.  Perhaps his great vehemence had tired itself out; and maybe\r\nhe was ashamed of the great disturbance he had made and all that had come\r\nupon Henry, and did not wish to think of it again, for St. Katherine\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfair-day passed over without a word of the pig.\r\n\r\nThe young ladies were not great letter-writers; and all that was known of\r\nthem was that Mamma was better, they had been to the Zoological Gardens\r\nand the hyena was so funny, and Mrs. Penrose was so nice.  Then that Papa\r\nand Sam were gone to Portsmouth, and that they had telegraphed that Sam\r\nhad succeeded.\r\n\r\nIf it had been her own brother, Miss Fosbrook could not have been much\r\nhappier; and in honour of it she and the three children all went to drink\r\ntea in the wilderness, walking in procession, each with a flag in hand,\r\npainted by her for the occasion.\r\n\r\nThree days after, when the post came in, there was a letter directed to\r\nMaster David Douglas Merrifield, Stokesley House, Bonchamp.  It was a\r\ngreat wonder; for David was not baby enough, nor near enough to the\r\nyoungest, to get letters as a pet, nor was he old enough to be written to\r\nlike an elder one.  He spelt the address all over before he made up his\r\nmind to open it, and then exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is not a letter!  It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngreen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a post-office order, Davie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook.  \xe2\x80\x9cLet me look.\r\nYes, for ten shillings.  Write your name there; and if we take it to the\r\npost-office at Bonchamp, they will give you ten shillings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen shillings!  Oh, Davie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Johnnie, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish it was to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt just makes up for what Hal took, and more too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\r\ncan it come from, Davie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the Queen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davie composedly; \xe2\x80\x9cthe Queen always does justice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was quite sorry to confess, for truth\xe2\x80\x99s sake, that she did\r\nnot think the Queen could have heard of the loss of the pig fund, and\r\nthat it was more likely to be from someone who wished to make up for the\r\ndisaster\xe2\x80\x94who could it be?  She looked at the round stamp upon the\r\ngreen-lettered paper, and read \xe2\x80\x9cPortsmouth.\xe2\x80\x9d  Could it be from Papa?\r\nThen she looked at the cover; but it was not a bit like the Captain\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwriting; it was pretty, lady-like, clear-looking hand-writing, and\r\npuzzled her a great deal more.  If the children had once had a secret of\r\ntheir own, there was a very considerable one to puzzle them now; and they\r\ncould hardly believe that Miss Fosbrook knew nothing about it, any more\r\nthan themselves.\r\n\r\nSo restless and puzzled were they, that she thought they would never be\r\nable to settle quietly to their lessons, and that it would save idleness\r\nif she walked with them at once to Bonchamp to get the money.  It was two\r\nmiles; but all three were stout walkers, and they were delighted to go;\r\nindeed, they would have fancied that someone else might run away with the\r\nten shillings if they had not made haste to secure it.  So \xe2\x80\x9cDavid Douglas\r\nMerrifield\xe2\x80\x9d was written, with much difficulty to make it small enough, in\r\nthe very best and roundest hand.  The boys were put into clean blouses,\r\nAnnie\xe2\x80\x99s striped cotton came to light; and off set the party through the\r\nlanes, each with sixpence in their hand, for it was poor fun to go to\r\nBonchamp, unless one had something to spend there.  David wanted a knife,\r\nJohnnie wanted a whip, Annie nothing in particular, only to go into a\r\nshop, and buy\xe2\x80\x94she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what.\r\n\r\nBut the wonderful affair at the post-office must have the first turn; and\r\nvery grand did David feel as the clerk peeped out from his little hole,\r\nand looked amused and gracious as the little boy stood on tiptoe to give\r\nin his green paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you have it in gold or silver, Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn gold, please,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\nIt was something to have a bit of gold in one\xe2\x80\x99s possession for the first\r\ntime in one\xe2\x80\x99s life; and David felt as if he had grown an inch taller, and\r\nwere as good as six years old, as he walked away with the half sovereign\r\nsqueezed into his hot little palm.\r\n\r\nThe toy-shop was at the end of the street, and in they went; Johnnie to\r\ntry all the whistles in the handles of the whips, and be much disgusted\r\nthat all that had a real sound lash cost a shilling; David to open and\r\nshut the sixpenny knives with the gravity of a judge examining their\r\nblades; and Annie to gape about, and ask the price of everything, after\r\nthe tiresome fashion of people, old or young, when they come out bent on\r\nspending, but without any aim or object.  However, Annie was kind, if she\r\nwere silly, and she was very fond of Johnnie; so it ended, after a little\r\nwhispering, in her sixpence being added to his, to buy a real good whip,\r\nsuch as would crack, and not come to pieces.\r\n\r\nJust then, what should the children espy, but a nice firm deal box,\r\ncontaining a little saw, a little plane, a hammer, a gimlet, a chisel,\r\nand sundry different sizes of nails.  Was there ever anything so\r\ndelightful, especially to David, who loved nothing so well as running\r\nafter George Bowles the carpenter, and handling his tools.  What was the\r\nprice of them?\r\n\r\nJust ten shillings and sixpence.  They were very cheap, the woman of the\r\ntoy-shop said.  They had been ordered by an old lady at her grandson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nentreaty; but afterwards a misgiving had seized her that the young\r\ngentleman would cut his fingers, and she would not take them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Fosbrook,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered David, \xe2\x80\x9cmay I give back the knife? then I\r\ncould buy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have bought and paid for it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomebody else will buy the box,\xe2\x80\x9d said David wistfully.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook, within herself, thought this unlikely, for nobody went to\r\nBonchamp for costly shopping; and she saw that the woman would gladly\r\nhave had the knife back, if she could have sold the tool-box, which, even\r\nat this reduced price, was much too dear for the little boys who\r\nfrequented the shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome away now, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she said decidedly.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo, another time, thank\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid was as nearly crying as ever he was, as he was forced to follow her\r\nout of the shop.  Those tools were so charming; his fingers tingled to be\r\nhammering, sawing, boring holes.  Had he lost the chance for that poor\r\nblunt knife?  Must he wait a whole fortnight for another sixpence, and\r\nfind the delicious tool-chest gone?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Davie, I am very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christabel when they were in the\r\nstreet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat nasty knife!\xe2\x80\x9d cried David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not the knife, Davie,\xe2\x80\x9d said she; \xe2\x80\x9cbut that I want to think\xe2\x80\x94I want\r\nyou to think\xe2\x80\x94why these ten shillings must have been sent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we lost the money for the pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut Kattern Hill\r\nfair is over, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want a pig now; I do want the gimlet to make\r\nholes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, David; but you know what was saved for the pig came from all of\r\nyou; you would have had no right to spend it on anything else, unless\r\nthey all had consented.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my very own,\xe2\x80\x9d said David; \xe2\x80\x9cit was sent to me\xe2\x80\x94myself\xe2\x80\x94me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it seems now; but just suppose you were to have a letter to say that\r\nsomeone\xe2\x80\x94poor Hal himself, perhaps\xe2\x80\x94or Papa\xe2\x80\x94had sent ten shillings to make\r\nup the money for the pig, and directed it to you because you cared so\r\nmuch, would it not be a shame to have spent the money upon yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they should not have sent it without saying,\xe2\x80\x9d said David.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook thought the same, when she saw how hard the trial was to\r\nthe little boy; but she hoped she was taking the kindest course, as she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, David, in nine days time, if you are good, you will have had\r\nanother sixpence.  I see no chance of the tools being sold; or if they\r\nare, I could send for such a box from London.  By that time, perhaps,\r\nsomething will have happened to show who sent the money, and why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if it is all for myself, I may have the tools!\xe2\x80\x9d cried David.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall have them, if you really think it is right, when Monday week\r\ncomes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\n\r\n\r\nIDEAS were slow in both entering or dying out of David\xe2\x80\x99s mind; but while\r\nthere they reigned supreme.  Carpentry had come in as the pig had gone\r\nout, and with the more force, because a new window was being put into\r\nMamma\xe2\x80\x99s room; and George Bowles was there, with all his delightful tools,\r\nletting the little boys amuse themselves therewith, till they had hardly\r\nthree sound fingers between them; and Nurse Freeman, when she dressed\r\ntheir wounds, could not think what was the use of a lady if she could not\r\nkeep the children from hurting themselves; but Miss Fosbrook thought that\r\nit was better that boys should get a few cuts and bruises than that they\r\nshould be timid and unhandy.\r\n\r\nOne evening, all the party walked to carry to Hannah Higgins\xe2\x80\x99s little\r\ngirl a pinafore that Annie had been making.  She was a nice, tidy woman,\r\nbut there was little furniture in her house, and she looked very poor.\r\nThe garden was large, and in pretty good order; and there was an empty\r\npig-sty, into which Annie peeped significantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Miss Annie, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t no pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Higgins.  \xe2\x80\x9cBen says, says\r\nhe, \xe2\x80\x98Mother, when I\xe2\x80\x99m taken on for carter boy, see if I don\xe2\x80\x99t get you a\r\nnice little pig, as will eat the garden stuff, and pay the rent.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Annie, and there she came to a sudden stop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he likely soon to be a carter boy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Miss Fosbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Ma\xe2\x80\x99am; he is but ten years old, and they don\xe2\x80\x99t often take them on\r\nunder twelve; but he is a good boy to his mother, and a terrible one for\r\nleasing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was obliged to have it explained to her that \xe2\x80\x9cleasing\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nmeant gleaning; and she saw the grand pile of small neat bundles of wheat\r\nput out to dry on the sunny side of the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Davie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Annie, as soon as they were outside the gate, \xe2\x80\x9csha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwe get the pig for Hannah?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my money, not yours; I shall do what I please with it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavid, rather crossly.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook pulled Annie back, and desired her to let David alone;\r\nherself wondering what would be the effect of what he had seen.\r\n\r\nHe had been eager to do good to Hannah when no desire of his own stood in\r\nthe way; but a formed wish had arisen in his mind, and he loved himself\r\nbetter than Hannah.  Christabel dreaded the clearing-up of the secret of\r\nthe post-office order, lest he should be proved to love himself more than\r\nright and justice.\r\n\r\nThere were not many letters from the absent pair of sisters; they seemed\r\nto be much too busy and happy to write, and appeared to be \xe2\x80\x9cseeing\r\neverything,\xe2\x80\x9d and to be only just able to put down the names of the\r\nwonders.  The chief of all, however, was that kind Mrs. Penrose had\r\nactually taken them to Portsmouth for a couple of nights, to see the\r\n_Ramilies_, in which she was going to remain till it sailed.  They had\r\nsat in the Admiral\xe2\x80\x99s cabin, and had slept upon \xe2\x80\x9cdear little sofas,\xe2\x80\x9d where\r\nthey wished they always slept; they had been in Papa\xe2\x80\x99s cabin, which was\r\nhalf filled up with a great gun, that can only be fired out at the window\r\n(scratched out, and \xe2\x80\x9cport-hole\xe2\x80\x9d put in.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how delightful!  I wish I had a big gun in my room!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Johnnie.\r\n\r\nAnd they had seen Sam\xe2\x80\x99s chest; and Sam did look so nice in his uniform;\r\nand he had dined with them every day.  They had dined late, with the\r\ngrown-up people; and the Admiral was so kind, only rather funny.\r\n\r\nAnnie wished she were as old as Bessie, as much as John wished to have a\r\ngun filling up his whole bed-room.\r\n\r\nThe next day, their Papa had taken them into the country to see Lady\r\nSeabury, Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s godmother, a very old lady indeed, older than\r\nGrandmamma, and who could not move out of her chair.  \xe2\x80\x9cShe gave me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwrote Bessie.  There again something had been scratched out, and \xe2\x80\x9ca kiss\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwritten overhead.\r\n\r\nThat something was quite a long word, but it had been very completely\r\nblotted out; not like the \xe2\x80\x9cwindow,\xe2\x80\x9d which had only a couple of cross\r\nbars, through which it could be plainly read; but there had plainly been\r\nfirst an attempt at smearing it out with the finger, and that not\r\nsucceeding, an immense shiny black mess, like the black shade of a chafer\r\ngrub, had been put down on it, and had come off on the opposite side of\r\nthe sheet.\r\n\r\nWhat could the word be?  Annie and David were both sure they could read\r\nthe lines through all the blot.  The first letter was certainly S.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Fosbrook, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think it is quite honourable to try to\r\nread what Bessie did not mean us to see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey did not quite enter into this, but they left off trying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma had been out in the carriage several times; and they were all\r\ncoming home on Saturday week\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that was the best news of all\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand then we\r\nhave a secret too for Miss Fosbrook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid said he was tired of secrets, and would not guess.  Annie guessed a\r\ngreat deal; but Miss Fosbrook thought more about the word she would not\r\ntry to read.  She began to have a strong suspicion from whom the\r\npost-office order had come, and was the more uneasy about the spending of\r\nDavid\xe2\x80\x99s half sovereign; but she durst say nothing, for she knew it could\r\ndo no good if he felt himself compelled against his own will; and she saw\r\nthat he was full of thought.\r\n\r\nOne day the lawn had been mown, and the children where all very busy\r\nwheeling their little barrows, and loading them with the short grass;\r\nDavid was with them at first, but when Purday left off work, he marched\r\nafter the old man in his grave deliberate way, and was seen no more till\r\nnearly tea-time, when he walked into the school-room with a very set look\r\nupon his solemn face, and sat himself down cross-legged on the locker,\r\nwith a sigh that seemed to come out of the very depths of his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, David?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made no answer, and Miss Fosbrook let him alone; but Annie presently\r\nbounced in, crying out, \xe2\x80\x9cDavie, Davie! where were you? we have been\r\nhunting for you everywhere!  Where did you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went with Purday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, to milk the cows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went with him to Farmer Long\xe2\x80\x99s, to see his little Chinese pigs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have bought one!  O Davie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPurday is to ask the farmer about the price to-morrow morning, because\r\nhe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you won\xe2\x80\x99t get the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s tools?\xe2\x80\x9d said Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said David; \xe2\x80\x9cPurday said tools that they make for little boys never\r\nwill cut.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you told Purday all about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavid nodded his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do tell me what Purday said!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Annie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said David bluntly.  But by and by, when John came\r\nin, and a few more questions were asked, David let out that Purday had\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, he thought sure enough if the money was sent to Master David\r\nfor that intent, he did not ought to spend it no other ways; and whether\r\nor not, Hannah Higgins was a deserving woman; and Master Davie didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what it was like never to have a bit of bacon ne\xe2\x80\x99er a Sunday in the\r\nwinter.  He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t say but it was hard that those poor folks should get\r\nnothing but bread and cabbages from week\xe2\x80\x99s end to week\xe2\x80\x99s end, just that\r\nMaster Davie might spoil bits of deal board with making chips of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd when David was sure he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t spoil his wood, Purday had told him\r\nthat them toy-shop young gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s tools were made to sell, and not\r\nmade to cut.  Best save up his money, and buy one real man\xe2\x80\x99s tool after\r\nanother; and then he\xe2\x80\x99d get a set equal to George Bowles\xe2\x80\x99s in time!\r\n\r\nThough so young, David was long-sighted and patient enough to see the\r\nsense of this, and had already made up his mind that he would begin with\r\na gimlet.  And though he did not say so, and the first resolution had\r\ncost a very tough struggle, his heart seemed to have freed itself in that\r\none great sigh, and he was at peace with himself.\r\n\r\nMiss Fosbrook was very glad he had gone to so wise and good an adviser as\r\nPurday, and was almost as happy as David himself.  She gave him and John\r\nleave to go with Purday the next day to bargain for the pig, as David was\r\nvery anxious for one in especial, whose face he said was so jolly fat;\r\nand it was grand to see the two little boys consequentially walking on\r\neither aide of Purday, who had put on his whitest round frock for the\r\ngreat occasion.\r\n\r\nFarmer Long was at home; he came out and did the honours of his ten\r\nlittle pigs; and when he found which was David\xe2\x80\x99s favourite, he declared\r\nthat it was the best of the lot, and laughed till David blushed, at the\r\nyoung gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s having got such an eye for a pig.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a regular\r\nlittle Trudgeon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purday, (meaning perhaps a Trojan;) and it was\r\nworth at least twelve shillings, but the farmer in his good-nature\r\ndeclared that little Master should have it for the ten, as it was for a\r\npresent.  Hannah\xe2\x80\x99s boy was working for him, and was a right good lad, and\r\nhe would give him some straw for the pig\xe2\x80\x99s bed when he went home at\r\nnight.  Then he took the two boys into the parlour, and while Purday had\r\na glass of beer in the kitchen, Mrs. Long gave each of them a big slice\r\nof plum-cake, and wanted very much to have given them some wine, but that\r\nthey knew they must not have; and she inquired after their Mamma and\r\nPapa, and made them so much of visitors, that David was terribly shy, and\r\nvery glad when it was over, though John liked it, and talked fast.\r\n\r\nAs to the giving the pig, that was a serious business; and David felt hot\r\nand shy, and wanted to get it over as soon as possible without a fuss.\r\n\r\nSo he bolted into Mrs. Higgins\xe2\x80\x99s cottage, put his hands behind his back,\r\nand spoke thus:\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Mrs. Higgins, put your pig-sty in order!  We\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nall done it\xe2\x80\x94at least they all wanted to\xe2\x80\x94and a green order came down in a\r\nletter\xe2\x80\x94and we\xe2\x80\x99ve bought the pig, and Ben will drive it home when he comes\r\nfrom work!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then, as if he had been in a great fright, he ran away; while Johnnie\r\nstayed, and, when Hannah understood, received so many curtsies, and\r\nlistened to so much pleasure, that he could hardly think of anything\r\nelse, and felt very glad that _some_ pence of his had been in Toby\r\nFillpot.\r\n\r\nAnnie said that it was not fair that she had not been at the giving the\r\npig; and Miss Fosbrook was a little disappointed too; but then it was\r\nmuch better that David should not want to make a display, so she would\r\nnot complain, and comforted Annie by putting her in mind that they could\r\ngo and see the little pig in his new quarters.\r\n\r\nA few days more, and the carriage was driving up to the door with dear\r\nMamma in it, and\xe2\x80\x94why, there were three little girls, not two!  One look,\r\nand the colour came into Christabel\xe2\x80\x99s face.  It was her youngest little\r\nsister, Dora, who sat beside Bessie!  Mrs. Merrifield had gone to see\r\nMrs. Fosbrook, and ask if she could take anything for her to her\r\ndaughter; and she had been so much shocked at the sight of the little\r\npale London faces, that she had begged leave to take home one of the\r\nchildren to spend a month with her sister at Stokesley, since Miss\r\nFosbrook could not be spared to go home at present.  Was not that a\r\nsecret for Christabel?  How these two sisters did hug each other!\r\n\r\nBut the Stokesley secrets have lasted long enough; and there is no time\r\nto tell of the happy days of Dora\xe2\x80\x99s visit, and the good care that Johnnie\r\ntook of her whenever she went out, and of her pretty quiet ways that made\r\nBessie take her for her dearest of friends.  And still less can be told\r\nof the smooth, peaceful, free spirit that seemed to have come home with\r\nMamma, even though she was still able to do little among the children,\r\nfor the very having her in the house appeared to keep things from going\r\nwrong.\r\n\r\nOne thing must be told, however, and that is, that when Annie told all\r\nthe wonderful story of the post-office order and the Chinese pig, Bessie\r\ngrew redder and redder in the face, and Susan squeezed both her hands\r\ntight together, and said \xe2\x80\x9cMay I tell, Bessie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n***END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE STOKESLEY SECRET***\r\n\r\n\r\n******* This file should be named 3411-0.txt or 3411-0.zip *******\r\n\r\n\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\nhttp://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/3/4/1/3411\r\n\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United\r\nStates without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
